The Beehive Objection and Its Refutation
Sanusi refutes the objection that bees' complex honeycombs disprove design.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objection from the bee's honeycomb by affirming God's exclusive creation and the concept of kasb.

Core Idea
The bee's honeycomb proves God's knowledge, not the bee's.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a classic objection against the argument from design: the example of the bee constructing a perfect hexagonal honeycomb without reason or instruction. How can we claim that precise actions indicate a wise agent when an irrational bee does the same? Sanusi's answer reasserts the Ash'ari doctrine of divine causality and explains that the bee is merely a locus for God's direct creation. This response also prepares the ground for later discussions on kasb (acquisition) and tawallud (generation).

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objector's argument carefully. The bee constructs a hexagonal shape that only engineers can design, yet the bee is irrational. This challenges the proof from design, because the bee's lack of knowledge seems to contradict the link between precision and knowledge.
The objector assumes that the bee's action is its own.
2
Review the Ash'ari position
Sanusi begins his answer by restating the core belief: God alone creates all actions. Creatures have no real effect. The actions of rational and irrational beings are all created by God.
This is foundational for the refutation.
3
Apply the doctrine to the bee
Sanusi argues that the hexagonal shape is not produced by the bee's power; rather, God creates it directly and inspires the bee. The bee has no kasb (acquisition) in this because it cannot even acquire an effect due to the impossibility of tawallud.
The bee is like a tool, not an agent.
4
See the conclusion
The honeycomb, far from disproving design, becomes a sign of God's tremendous knowledge and guidance of all creatures. The objection backfires.
Sanusi quotes the Quran: 'He gave everything its creation, then guided.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: ينتقص هذا الدليل بما يتخذه النحل بغير آلة من البيوت المحكمة المسدسة التي لا يعرف وضع مثلها إلا المهندسون واختارت خصوصية هذا الشكل لجمع بين مصلحتين؛ وهما قربه من شكل الدائرة القريب من شكل النحلة، والأمن معه من فرج تبقى بين الأشكال ضائعة لغير فائدة، ومعرفة كون الجمع بين هاتين المصلحتين خاصا بهذا الشكل المسدس . مما لا يستخرجه إلا أذكياء المهندسين بعد سبر وبحث عظيم، ومعلوم: أن النحلة من الحيوان غير العاقل، وقد صدر من فعلها ما صدر، فكيف يصح مع هذا أن يستدل بإحكام الفعل واشتماله على دقائق الصنع على علم صانعه؟
فالجواب: أنك قد عرفت أن معتقد أهل السنة: أن الله جل وعلا منفرد بخلق كل شيء، لا تأثير لغيره في شيء أيا كان، وأن الأفعال التي تتصف بها العقلاء وغيرهم كلها منسوبة إلى الله جل وعلا خلقا واختراعا، وإن كان بعضها ينسب إلى بعض من يتصف بها كسبا من غير تأثير أصلاً، وسيأتي في فصل خلق الأفعال تفسير معنى الكسب. فليس في الوجود عند أهل السنة إلا الله جل وعلا موصوفا بصفاته العليا، وكل ما سواه من الكائنات فهي أفعاله، فالشكل المسدس الذي اتخذته النحلة إذا ليس لها فيه تأثير أصلاً، بل ولا كسب من غير تأثير؛ لما يأتي في فضل إبطال التولد من امتناع تعلق القدرة الحادثة بمحلها، وإنما وقوع ذلك الشكل بمجرد خلق الله جل وعلا واختراعه، وألهم النحل لاتخاذه مسكنا كما ألهم سائر الحيوانات لمصالحها، الذي أعطى كل شيء خلقه ثم هدى [طه: 50]، فهو من جملة ما يدل على عظيم علمه تعالى.
If it is said: This proof is undermined by what the bee takes, without an instrument, of well-constructed hexagonal chambers whose like only engineers know how to place, and it chose the specificity of this shape to combine two benefits: its proximity to the circular shape close to the bee's shape, and the safety from gaps that remain between shapes wasted without benefit. And knowledge that the combination of these two benefits is specific to this hexagonal shape is something that only the cleverest of engineers extract after great examination and research. And it is known that the bee is an irrational animal, and what has issued from its action has issued. So how can it be valid, with this, to infer from the precision of the act and its inclusion of subtle craftsmanship the knowledge of its maker?
The answer: You have known that the belief of Ahl al-Sunna is that God, may He be exalted, is unique in creating everything; nothing else has any effect in anything whatsoever. And the actions that rational beings and others are described by are all attributed to God, may He be exalted, as creation and invention, although some of them are attributed to some of those who are described by them as acquisition (kasb) without any effect at all. And it will come in the chapter on the creation of acts the explanation of the meaning of acquisition. So in existence, according to Ahl al-Sunna, there is only God, may He be exalted, described by His high attributes, and everything else among the beings are His acts. So the hexagonal shape that the bee took, then, has no effect for it in it at all, nor even acquisition without effect, because of what will come in the section on refuting generation (tawallud) of the impossibility of the originated power attaching to its locus. Rather, the occurrence of that shape is solely by the creation of God, may He be exalted, and His invention, and He inspired the bee to take it as a dwelling, just as He inspired the rest of animals for their interests. He who gave everything its creation then guided [Taha 20:50]. So it is among the things that indicate His tremendous knowledge, exalted is He.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objector raises the example of the bee's honeycomb to challenge the proof from design: if the bee, an irrational animal, can produce such a geometrically perfect structure, then the precision of an act does not necessarily indicate the knowledge of its agent, since the bee lacks knowledge. Sanusi's answer first restates the Ash'ari position: God alone creates all actions, and creatures have no real causal efficacy. The bee's honeycomb is not produced by the bee's own power or knowledge; rather, God creates the shape directly and inspires the bee to take it as a dwelling. Thus, the honeycomb actually demonstrates God's knowledge and power, not the bee's. Sanusi also mentions the concept of kasb (acquisition) and the futility of tawallud (generation of effects), which will be explained later. The conclusion is that the objection backfires, as the honeycomb is a sign of God's wisdom.

الاتفاق
الكسب
خلق الأفعال
التولد
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The bee's complex honeycomb shows that the bee has knowledge or that precise actions can occur without a wise creator.
Correction: Sanusi explains that all actions, including the bee's honeycomb, are directly created by God. The bee is merely a locus with no causal effect. Thus, the honeycomb is a sign of God's knowledge, not the bee's.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's answer, why does the bee's honeycomb not prove that the bee has knowledge?
A) The bee is irrational and cannot possess knowledge.
B) The honeycomb is actually created directly by God, and the bee is merely a locus with no causal effect.
C) The bee learned the honeycomb shape from instinct.
D) The honeycomb is not truly complex or precise.

Key Takeaway
The bee's honeycomb does not show the bee's knowledge but rather God's direct creation and guidance, making it a sign of His immense wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see the incredible complexity of a beehive or any natural wonder, do you truly recognize that it is directly from God, or do you unconsciously attribute it to nature or the creature itself? How can you train your heart to see God’s hand in everything?
?
Reflect on a skill or achievement you are proud of. Do you believe that your ability and the outcome came from your own power, or do you acknowledge that God created both the action and its result? What would change in your humility and gratitude if you lived the Ash'ari conviction that God is the sole creator of your actions?
?
The bee’s honeycomb is a sign of God’s knowledge, not the bee’s. When you see order and precision in your life—your health, your provisions, your relationships—do you look past the apparent causes to see the real Cause? What is one area where you tend to forget this and credit yourself or others, and how will you remind yourself starting today?

Teaching Sciences to the Unworthy as Divine Proof
Sanusi states that teaching precise sciences to those unfit for any knowledge is a strong proof of God's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the phenomenon of Allah teaching precise sciences to unworthy creatures demonstrates His knowledge, power, will, and sovereignty.

Core Idea
Even the most unlikely creatures receive profound knowledge from Allah, proving His limitless attributes.

This passage presents a striking argument from creation: the fact that Allah teaches intricate sciences to beings that do not even possess basic knowledge is one of the clearest proofs of His attributes. Think of bees building perfect hives or ants navigating complex colonies — these creatures exhibit a wisdom they did not learn. Sanusi uses this observation to point directly to Allah's knowledge, power, and will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice the rhetorical phrase 'how then with its minute aspects?' which emphasizes the wonder.
2
Identify the components of the proof
The passage mentions four attributes proved: honor of Allah's knowledge, brilliance of His power, penetration of His will, and submission of all possible things to His will. Underline each attribute.
These are the key divine attributes being argued.
3
Reflect on the logic
Ask yourself: Why does teaching precise sciences to someone unfit for any knowledge serve as proof? The answer lies in the fact that such knowledge cannot come from the creature itself — it must be directly given by Allah.
This is an argument from causality and inability.
4
Connect to everyday examples
Think of examples in nature where animals display complex behaviors that seem like 'sciences' (e.g., spider web construction, bird migration). How do these fit the argument?
This helps contextualize the abstract argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تعليم دقائقِ العلومِ وخلْقُها لِمَنْ ليسَ أهلاً لمطلق العلم، فكيف بدقائقِهِ .. مِنْ أدلّ دليل على شرف علمِهِ جَلَّ وعلا، وباهرِ قدرته، ونفوذ إرادتِهِ، وانقياد جميع الممكنات لمشيئته تعالى
Then teaching the minute aspects of sciences and creating them for one who is not fit for absolute knowledge, how then with its minute aspects? … is among the clearest proofs of the honor of His knowledge, exalted is He, and the brilliance of His power, and the penetration of His will, and the submission of all possible things to His will, exalted is He.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is a general argument from divine teaching. It states that the fact that Allah teaches the subtle, precise sciences (daqā'iq al-ʿulūm) and creates them in beings who are not even fit for any knowledge at all (e.g., bees, ants) is among the strongest proofs of the greatness of Allah's knowledge, the brilliance of His power, the effectiveness of His will, and the total submission of all possible existents to His decree. The phrase 'how then with its minute aspects?' emphasizes that if even basic knowledge is not present, the endowment of detailed sciences is even more striking. Thus, this is an argument for divine omniscience, omnipotence, and volition.

Key Terms
تعليم دقائق العلوم
خلق العلم
شرف علم الله
قدرة
إرادة
مشيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that only rational beings can receive knowledge from God, or that knowledge requires prior learning.
Correction: The passage shows that even non-rational creatures can be endowed with precise sciences directly by Allah, without any prior learning or fitness.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is one of the clearest proofs of God's knowledge and power?
A) The existence of rational scholars
B) The creation of complex machines
C) Teaching precise sciences to those unfit even for basic knowledge
D) The natural order of the cosmos

Key Takeaway
The fact that Allah grants intricate sciences to creatures lacking basic understanding is a direct sign of His perfect knowledge, power, and will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt unworthy of knowledge or guidance? How does this passage—showing that Allah grants intricate sciences even to those unfit for basic knowledge—confront that feeling? What step will you take this week to seek knowledge from Him despite your perceived shortcomings?
?
The passage declares that 'all possible things' are submissive to Allah's will. Is there a single area in your life—your finances, relationships, or future plans—where you are still resisting that submission? What would it practically mean to surrender that area today?
?
Consider a specific creature (like a bee or spider) that displays complex knowledge without learning. How does recognizing its abilities as a direct gift from Allah, as this passage teaches, change your view of His knowledge and power? What specific gratitude or reliance will you express to Him in response?

Defending the Proof from Precision (Ihkam)
Ibn al-Tilmisani refutes Imam al-Haramayn's weakening of the proof from precision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proof from precision (ihkām) is considered clearer than the proof from choice (ikhtiyār) for establishing God's knowledge, and how the objection of Ibn al-Tilmisani refutes Imam al-Haramayn's weakening.

Core Idea
Precision proves God's knowledge necessarily; choice proves it only through reflection.

This passage tackles a critical debate in kalām: which proof for God's knowledge is strongest? Imam al-Haramayn had argued that the proof from choice is sufficient and that precision merely involves spatial positioning. Ibn al-Tilmisani objects, arguing that precision involves far more—specific qualities and quantities—and that it yields necessary knowledge, making it the clearer proof. Let's follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the overall argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the flow: a concession, a rebuttal of Imam al-Haramayn, and a final conclusion favoring precision.
There are several embedded objections and responses; track who is speaking.
2
Identify the concession about the angel
Find where the author grants an opponent's point about angels acting and then turns it to his advantage using the story of the ant.
This shows that even if a miracle is done by an angel, knowledge must be given.
3
Analyze Imam al-Haramayn's weakening
Read his statement: he reduces precision to mere accidents partitioning substances, and says only choice proves knowledge.
Notice he says 'discussion with the opponent is after his being a purposeful maker'.
4
Study Ibn al-Tilmisani's objection
He argues precision involves more than spatial arrangement—it includes specific qualities and quantities. He quotes Quran 13:8.
He also argues that even non-precise actions indicate knowledge, but precision is clearer.
5
Grasp the final conclusion
The response ends by stating that precision indicates knowledge necessarily (bi-ḍarūra), while choice indicates it through reflection (bi-l-naẓar), so precision is superior.
This is the key doctrinal point: necessary knowledge is stronger than inferential knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو سلَّمْنا أَنَّهُ مِنْ فعلها فلا نسلّمُ أَنَّهَا غيرُ عالمةٍ بِهِ حِينَئِذٍ خُرِقَتْ في حقها العادةُ ، والهِمَتْ علمَ ذلك ، وخُلِقَ لها كما خُلِقَ للنملة علم بسليمانَ عليهِ السلامُ وبجنودِهِ حتى قالَتْ : ﴿ يَتَأَيُّهَا النَّمْلُ ادْخُلُوا مَسَكِنَكُمْ ﴾ [النمل : ١٨] . ثم تعليم دقائقِ العلومِ وخلْقُها لِمَنْ ليسَ أهلاً لمطلق العلم ، فكيف بدقائقِهِ .. مِنْ أدلّ دليل على شرف علمِهِ جَلَّ وعلا ، وباهرِ قدرته ، ونفوذ إرادتِهِ ، وانقياد جميع الممكنات لمشيئته تعالى وقد ضعف إمام الحرمين في البرهان » دلالة الإحكام على العلم وقال : ( لا معنى للإحكام سوى أنَّ الأكوانَ حَصَّصَتِ الجواهر بأحياز (١) حتى انتظم منها خطوط مستقيمة (۱) ، ولا اختصاص للأكوانِ بالدلالةِ على العلم ، وإِنَّما الكلامُ معَ الخصم بعد كونه صانعاً مختاراً ، والاختيار دليل كونِهِ عالماً ((۲) واعترض عليه شرفُ الدين بنُ التَّلمساني بأنَّ الإحكام لا تُسلم رجوعَهُ إِلى مُجرَّدِ تخصُّص الجواهر بأكوان ، بل هو يرجع إلى اختصاص بأكوان وكيفيَّاتٍ خاصّةٍ ، وضروبٍ مِنَ الصفاتِ والأعراض على مقدارٍ ، وَكُلُّ شَيْءٍ عِندَمُ بِمِقْدَارِ ﴾ [الرعد : ۸] ، ثم دلالة غير الإحكام مِنْ وقوع الفعل على وَفي الاختيار وإنْ كانَ مُتبجا (١).. لا يمنعُ مِنْ دلالةِ الإحكام عليه ، بل دلالة الإحكام أوضح ؛ لأنَّه يدلُّ على العلم بالضرورة ، والاختيارُ يدلُّ عليه بالنظرِ ( انتهى)
And if we grant that it is from her action, we still do not concede that she is not knowing of it at that time, for the usual course of events was broken concerning her, and she was inspired with knowledge of that, and it was created for her just as knowledge of Solomon, peace be upon him, and his troops was created for the ant, so that she said: 'O ants, enter your dwellings' [27:18]. Then teaching the subtle points of sciences and creating them for one who is not even worthy of absolute knowledge, let alone its subtleties, is among the clearest proofs of the honor of His knowledge, glory and exalted is He, and the brilliance of His power, the effectiveness of His will, and the submission of all contingent things to His will. And Imam al-Haramayn weakened in al-Burhan the indication of precision (ihkām) for knowledge, saying: 'Precision means nothing other than that the accidents (akwān) partitioned the substances into positions until straight lines were composed from them, and there is no specific indication of the accidents to knowledge. Rather, the discussion with the opponent is after his being a purposeful maker, and choice is evidence of His being knowing.' And Sharf al-Din ibn al-Tilmisani objected to him that precision does not reduce to mere specification of substances by accidents, but rather it entails specification by accidents, specific qualities, and types of attributes and accidents in a measure, 'and everything before Him is by measure' [13:8]. Then the indication of other than precision from the occurrence of an action, whether precise or not, does not prevent the indication of precision from it; rather, the indication of precision is clearer, because it indicates knowledge necessarily, while choice indicates it through reflection (end).
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is an extended refutation defending the proof from precision (ihkām) for God's knowledge. It begins by presenting a concession argument: even if one claims a miracle is performed by an angel, the angel must be granted knowledge of the act, just as the ant was given knowledge of Solomon. The author then argues that creating subtle sciences in beings unworthy of them proves God's perfect knowledge. He then quotes Imam al-Haramayn, who weakened the proof from precision by reducing it to mere positioning of substances and argued that the proof from choice is sufficient. Ibn al-Tilmisani objects that precision involves more than spatial positioning; it involves specific qualities and quantities, as confirmed by the Quranic verse 'everything before Him is by measure'. The response concludes that even if non-precise actions indicate knowledge, the indication from precision is clearer because it gives necessary knowledge, while the proof from choice gives knowledge through reflection.

Key Terms
الإحكام
الاختيار
العلم
الأكوان
الضرورة
النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the proof from choice is the primary or strongest proof for God's knowledge.
Correction: The passage argues that the proof from precision is even clearer because it gives necessary (ḍarūrī) knowledge, while choice gives only reflective (naẓarī) knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the response to Ibn al-Tilmisani, why is the proof from precision (ihkām) clearer than the proof from choice (ikhtiyār)?
A) Because precision involves many details that only an all-knowing being could create.
B) Because precision indicates knowledge necessarily (bi-ḍarūra), while choice indicates it through reflection (bi-l-naẓar).
C) Because choice is a sign of ignorance in a creator.
D) Because the proof from precision is mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The proof from precision (ihkām) is the clearest argument for God's knowledge because it yields necessary knowledge, not just reflective inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the precision in creation around you—like the intricate design of a leaf or the human eye—do you feel a necessary certainty that God knows every detail, or do you only infer it after some thought? What does your answer say about your level of conviction?
?
If the ant could recognize Solomon's army by divine inspiration, what prevents you from recognizing God's handiwork with the same immediacy? Are you relying too much on your reasoning and not enough on opening your heart to the obvious signs?
?
Ibn al-Tilmisani insists that precision gives necessary knowledge, not just reflection. Reflect on a time when you had a sudden, undeniable awareness of God's knowledge and power. Was it through a 'precise' sign? How can you seek more of such direct certainty in your daily life?

Two Proofs for God's Knowledge: Precision and Choice
Sanusi concludes the discussion on proofs for God's knowledge and elaborates the proof from choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational proofs for God's knowledge—especially the proof from choice—and why divine choice necessarily implies knowledge.

Core Idea
Choice without knowledge is impossible for God.

Sanusi wraps up his discussion on God's knowledge by confirming two valid proofs: the proof from the precision of creation and the proof from divine choice. He notes that the first is clearer, but then he unpacks the second in detail, drawing on Ibn al-Tilmisani's formulation. This lesson walks through that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation, to get the overall meaning.
2
Identify the two proofs
Notice that Sanusi mentions two proofs: precision (iḥkām) and choice (ikhtiyār). He says the first is clearer, but focuses on the second.
3
Follow the logic of the proof from choice
The argument: God is an agent by choice → He must intend → intention without knowledge is impossible → therefore God knows.
4
Note the qualification about contingent beings
Sanusi adds that contingent beings can intend with contract, conjecture, or delusion, but not God. This strengthens the conclusion.
5
Reflect on why precision is clearer
Consider why the proof from precision (design in creation) is considered more evident than the proof from choice.
This is mentioned but not elaborated in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلتُ : فخرجَ مِنْ هذا أَنَّهُ يصح الاستدلال على كونه جل وعلا بوجهين : الإحكام والاختيارُ ، وأنَّ الأَوَّلَ أوضحُ مِنَ الثاني ووجه الاستدلال بالاختيار على ما قرَّرَهُ ابنُ التلمساني في ( شرح المعالم : (۳) : أَنَّهُ قد تقرَّرَ فيما مضى بالبراهين القاطعة أنَّ الله تعالى فاعل بالاختيار ، والفاعلُ بالاختيار لا بد وأن يكون قاصداً إلى ما يفعله ، والقصد إلى الشيء معَ الجهل به محال ، ولا يُتصوَّرُ القصدُ مِنَ اللهِ تعالى إلا مع العلم بالمقصودِ - وإِنْ كانَ يُتصوَّرُ مِنَ الحادثِ مَعَ العَقْدِ والظنِّ والوهم - ؛ لئلا يُتصوَّرَ مِنَ اللهِ تعالى بناءً على ذلكَ
I say: So from this it emerges that it is valid to infer about His being, exalted and high, in two ways: precision (iḥkām) and choice (ikhtiyār), and that the first is clearer than the second. And the way of inference by choice according to what Ibn al-Tilmisani established in (Sharḥ al-Maʿālim): that it has been established previously by decisive proofs that Allah is an agent by choice, and the agent by choice must necessarily intend what he does, and intending something while being ignorant of it is impossible, and intention from Allah is not conceivable except with knowledge of the intended object - although from the contingent it is conceivable with contract, conjecture, and delusion - so that intention is not conceived from Allah based on that.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is the author's conclusion and summary. He states that two proofs for God's knowledge are valid: the proof from precision (iḥkām) and the proof from choice (ikhtiyār), with the first being clearer. He then explains the proof from choice as formulated by Ibn al-Tilmisani. The argument is: (1) It has been proven that God is an agent by choice. (2) An agent by choice must intend what he does. (3) Intending something while ignorant of it is impossible. (4) Therefore, God must know what He intends. The author notes that while contingent beings can intend based on contract, conjecture, or delusion, this does not apply to God, so for Him intention necessarily implies knowledge.

الإحكام
الاختيار
الفاعل بالاختيار
القصد
العلم بالمقصود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine choice implies that God deliberates or changes His mind.
Correction: The proof from choice shows that choice requires knowledge of the intended outcome, not uncertainty or deliberation.

Checkpoint
In the proof from choice (ikhtiyār), why does the fact that God is an agent by choice necessitate that He knows?
A) Because any agent must know everything.
B) Because an agent by choice must intend his action, and intention without knowledge of the intended is impossible for God.
C) Because choice is a sign of wisdom.
D) Because God cannot be ignorant of anything.

Key Takeaway
The proof from choice demonstrates that because God is an agent by choice, He must know what He intends to do.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that Allah's every action is done with full knowledge and intention, how does that change the way you view the events in your life—especially the trials you face? Are you willing to trust that even what you do not understand is part of a perfectly known plan? What would change in your heart if you truly believed that?
?
The proof from choice shows that God's intention is never based on guesswork or uncertainty. In contrast, your own intentions are often clouded by ignorance and doubt. How often do you act without truly knowing the outcome, relying on conjecture? Does this difference humble you before Allah? In what specific area of your life do you need to repent of acting based on your own flawed assumptions rather than seeking His guidance?
?
If Allah knows exactly what He intends for you, including the consequences of every choice you make, then your ignorance of the future is not an excuse for disobedience. When you sin, do you forget that Allah's command is based on perfect knowledge, while your desire is based on your own limited understanding? What would it look like for you to submit your choice to His knowledge today, even if it is hard?

God's Knowledge Encompasses Particulars in Detail
Sanusi proves that God's knowledge extends to all particular things in detail, refuting the philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's rational proof that God's knowledge includes all particular things in their specific details, and how it refutes the philosophical view that God only knows universals.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is all-encompassing, not limited to universals.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a compelling argument that God's knowledge is not restricted to universal concepts but extends to every particular detail. He begins with a premise about absolute essences and then refutes the philosophers who claim otherwise. Let's dive into the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full literal translation. Let the flow of the argument sink in.
Notice the refutation at the end.
2
Identify the argument structure
Break down the passage: claim, premise, reasoning, conclusion, refutation. Write down each part.
The premise is about absolute essences needing specification.
3
Understand the refutation
Pay attention to how Sanusi explicitly rejects the philosophers' statement and declares divine transcendence.
4

Read the footnote about the nature of essences. Note the two views and how the argument works on the first view.
This is a technical point for deeper understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيانُ أنَّ علمَهُ يتعلَّق بالمعلوم تفصيلاً ] ولمَّا كانَتِ الماهيَّاتُ المطلقات لا يمكن دخولها في الوجودِ إلا مع تخصيصها بزمان ومحل وكيفية ووضع ومقدار (۱) ، وكلُّ وجْهِ وُجِدَتْ عليهِ أمكن في العقل وقوعها على خلافِهِ أو مِثْلِهِ ، ولا يتخصَّص إلا بالقصد إليه .. وجبَ أنْ يكونَ عالماً بها مِنْ كلّ وجه ، وذلك أدلُّ دليل على أنه تعالى عالم بالجزئيات ، لا كما تقوله الفلاسفةُ : إِنَّ علمَةٌ لا يكون إلا كليّاً ، تعالى الله عمَّا يقولُ الظالمون علوّاً كبيراً . قوله : ( وإمدادِهِ بما يحفظها عليهِ ( الضمير في ( إمدادِهِ ) يعودُ على الجزء ، والمنصوبُ في ( يحفظها ) يعود على المنفعة (۱) هذا بناءً على أن للماهيات وجوداً خارجياً، إلا أن وجودها مخصص بوجود أفرادها، وعليه فهي جزء من كل فرد من أفرادها؛ فالإنسانية جزء من زيد وعمرو. والأكثرون على أن الماهية أمر اعتباري منتزع من أفراده، فهي وصف فقط.
Demonstration that His knowledge relates to the known in detail. And since the absolute essences (al-mahiyyaat al-mutlaqaat) cannot enter existence except with their specification by time, place, quality, position, and quantity, and every aspect upon which they exist is intellectually possible to occur contrary to it or similar to it, and they are not specified except by intending it... it is necessary that He be knowing of them from every aspect, and that is the strongest evidence that He, exalted, knows particulars — not as the philosophers say: His knowledge is only universal. Exalted is Allah far above what the wrongdoers say, a great exaltation. His statement: 'and His supplying what preserves them for him' — the pronoun in 'His supplying' refers to the part, and the object in 'preserves them' refers to the benefit. (1) This is based on the view that essences have external existence, but their existence is specified by the existence of their individuals; accordingly they are part of every individual of them; so humanity is part of Zayd and Amr. The majority hold that the essence is a mental consideration abstracted from its individuals, so it is only a description.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God's knowledge encompasses individual particulars (al-juz'iyyaat) in all their detail. Sanusi begins by stating the proposition: God's knowledge relates to the known in detail. Then he presents the argument: the absolute essences (al-mahiyyaat al-mutlaqaat) cannot enter external existence without being specified by particularizing factors such as time, place, quality, position, and quantity. For any specific manifestation of an essence, the mind can conceive it being otherwise or identical; specification only occurs through intention. Therefore, God must know these essences from every aspect — that is, in their specific individual details as well as their universal nature. This directly refutes the philosophers who claim God's knowledge is only universal and does not extend to particulars. Sanusi then comments on a preceding phrase, interpreting the pronouns. The footnote explains that this argument assumes essences have external existence as parts of individuals, though most theologians consider essences to be mental constructs. Sanusi implies the argument is sound regardless.

Proof that God's Knowledge Encompasses Particulars
Sanusi proves God's knowledge of particulars and refutes philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi proves that God's knowledge includes every detail of particular things, refuting the philosophical claim that God only knows universals.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is not limited to universals; He knows each particular in every detail.

This passage from Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin provides a concise yet powerful proof that God knows all particulars. The argument builds on the nature of essences entering existence with specifications, leading to the conclusion that God must know each aspect. This refutes the philosophical position that restricts divine knowledge to universals.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the first line is a fragment continuing from previous context.
2
Identify the initial principle
The passage begins with a principle: if something could be otherwise, that possibility would be a deficiency, which God is above. Therefore, God must be knowing.
This sets up the logical necessity of God's knowledge.
3
Follow the reasoning on essences
Sanusi explains that absolute essences (e.g., humanity) only exist when specified by time, place, quality, etc. For each aspect, it could be different, so specification requires an intentional act.
This shows that God must know every aspect to specify them.
4
Understand the conclusion
From the above, it follows that God knows each particular in all its aspects. This is the clearest proof that God knows particulars.
The conclusion directly refutes the philosophers.
5
Contrast with the philosophers' view
The philosophers claim God's knowledge is only universal (kulli). Sanusi explicitly rejects this and ends with a strong condemnation.
This highlights the doctrinal stakes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاحتمال وقوع ذلك على خلافِ ما هو عليهِ ، وهو نقص يتعالى الله عنه ، فتعيَّنَ أنْ يكونَ عالماً
بيانُ أنَّ علمَهُ يتعلَّق بالمعلوم تفصيلاً
ولمَّا كانَتِ الماهيَّاتُ المطلقات لا يمكن دخولها في الوجودِ إلا مع تخصيصها بزمان ومحل وكيفية ووضع ومقدار ، وكلُّ وجْهِ وُجِدَتْ عليهِ أمكن في العقل وقوعها على خلافِهِ أو مِثْلِهِ ، ولا يتخصَّص إلا بالقصد إليه .. وجبَ أنْ يكونَ عالماً بها مِنْ كلّ وجه ، وذلك أدلُّ دليل على أنه تعالى عالم بالجزئيات ، لا كما تقوله الفلاسفةُ : إِنَّ علمَةٌ لا يكون إلا كليّاً ، تعالى الله عمَّا يقولُ الظالمون علوّاً كبيراً .
The possibility of that occurring contrary to what it is is a deficiency from which God is exalted, so it is determined that He is knowing. Statement that His knowledge pertains to the known in detail. Since absolute essences cannot enter existence except with specification by time, place, quality, position, and quantity, and every aspect in which they exist is possible in the mind to occur contrary to it or similar to it, and they are not specified except by intending them, it is necessary that He be knowing of them in every respect. That is the clearest proof that He, exalted, is knowing of particulars, not as the philosophers say: His knowledge is only universal. Exalted is God above what the wrongdoers say, with great exaltation.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God's knowledge encompasses every detail of particular things, refuting the philosophical claim that God only knows universals. Sanusi begins by stating a principle: if something could be otherwise, that possibility would imply deficiency, and God is above deficiency, so He must be knowing. Then he explains a key point: absolute essences (like humanity) only enter existence when specified with particular attributes (time, place, quality, etc.). For each aspect under which a thing exists, it is possible for it to be different or the same. Specification to one particular aspect requires an intentional act. Therefore, God must know the thing in every aspect. This demonstrates that God knows particulars (juz'iyyat), contrary to the philosophers who claim His knowledge is only universal (kulli). The passage ends with a strong condemnation of that view.

Wisdom in the Design of Skin, Hair, and Eyebrows
Sanusi enumerates examples of divine wisdom in human anatomy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses specific features of the human body—blood, skin, hair, eyebrows, and eyelashes—as evidence for divine wisdom and intentional creation.

Core Idea
Every detail of the human body reveals purposeful divine design.

Sanusi presents a teleological argument by examining specific features of the human body. He shows that the consistency of blood, the covering of skin, the protection of hair, and the design of eyebrows and eyelashes all serve clear purposes. This lesson walks through each example to appreciate how Sanusi builds his case for divine wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the use of 'ثم' (then) to sequence the examples.
2
Identify each body part and its purpose
List the body parts mentioned: blood, skin, hair, eyebrows, eyelashes. For each, determine the function Sanusi assigns (e.g., skin as cover, hair as protection).
3
Analyze the hypothetical consequences
For each example, note the conditional statements (e.g., 'ولو كان ألطف... لم تتغذ'). These show that if the design were different, harm would result, highlighting intentionality.
This is a key feature of teleological arguments.
4
Reflect on the cumulative evidence
Consider how these examples together demonstrate God's wisdom. Ask yourself: could these features be random, or do they point to a designer?
Sanusi's goal is to move from observed design to divine attribute of wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ممَّا هو عليه . . لم يجرِ في العروقِ ، ولو كانَ ألطفَ ممَّا هو عليه .. لم تتغذ به الأعضاء . ثم كسا اللحم بالجلد ليسترَهُ كلَّهُ كالوعاء له ، ولولا ذلك لكانَ قِشْراً أحمر ، وفي ذلك هلاكه عادةً . ثم كساهُ الشَّعَرَ وقايةً للجلدِ وزينةً في بعض المواضع ، وما لم يكنْ فِيهِ شَعَرٌ جعلَ لهُ اللباس عوضاً منه ، وجعل أصوله مغروزةً في اللحم ؛ ليتم الانتفاعُ ببقائه ، ولَيَّنَ أصوله ولم يجعلها يابسة مثل رؤوس الإِبَرِ ؛ إذْ لو كانَتْ كذلكَ لم يَهْنَهُ عيش ، وجعلَ الحواجب والأشفار وقايةً للعين ، ولولا ذلك لأهلكها الغبارُ والسَّقَطُ ، وجعلَها على وجه يتمكَّنُ بسهولةٍ مِنْ رفعِها عن الناظرِ عند قصدِ النظرِ ، ومِنْ إرخائها على جميع العين عند إرادة إمساكِهِ النظر إلى ما تؤذي رؤيتُهُ ديناً أو دنيا ، ولم يجعلْ شَعَرَها طَبَقاً واحداً لينظرَ مِنْ خِلالِها
From what it is... it did not flow in the veins, and if it were thinner than what it is... the organs would not be nourished by it. Then He clothed the flesh with skin to cover it entirely, like a container for it, and were it not for that it would be a red crust, and therein usually lies its destruction. Then He clothed it with hair as protection for the skin and adornment in some places, and what does not have hair He made clothing as a substitute for it, and He made its roots implanted in the flesh so that benefit from its permanence is achieved, and He made its roots soft and did not make them dry like the tips of needles; for if they were like that, life would not be pleasant for him. And He made the eyebrows and eyelashes protection for the eye, and were it not for that, dust and falling particles would destroy it, and He made them in a way that one can easily raise them from the eye when intending to look, and lower them over the entire eye when intending to withhold looking at something whose sight harms religion or worldly affairs, and He did not make their hair a single layer so that one could see through them.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is a teleological argument for divine wisdom in the creation of the human body. It begins by noting that the consistency of blood is precisely suited to flow in the veins and nourish the organs—neither too thin nor too thick. Then it moves to the skin: God covered the flesh with skin as a container; without it, the flesh would be an exposed red mass and perish. Then hair: God provided hair as protection and adornment, and where there is no hair, He gave clothing as a substitute. The roots of hair are implanted in the flesh for permanence, and they are soft, not like needle tips, which would cause discomfort. Then eyebrows and eyelashes: they protect the eyes from dust and debris. They are designed to be raised easily for vision and lowered to block harmful sights, and they are not a single solid layer so that one can still see through them when needed. Each element is presented as an evidence of meticulous design.

Correction: Sanusi argues they are intentionally designed with specific functions, as evidenced by the harm that would occur if they were different.

Wisdom in the Creation of Lips, Tongue, and Teeth
Sanusi details the wisdom behind the creation of lips, tongue, and teeth for eating and speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific wisdoms behind the creation of lips, tongue, and teeth, and how their design points to the Creator.

Core Idea
Every part of the mouth is designed with specific purpose and timing.

In this passage, Sanusi continues his teleological argument by examining the mouth. He shows how each part — lips, teeth, tongue — is perfectly suited for its functions, and even the timing of their appearance demonstrates wisdom. This lesson will walk through these details and help you appreciate the intricate design.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall meaning. Pay attention to the sequence of creation described.
The passage follows a clear order: lips, then teeth, then tongue, then timing.
2
Identify each part and its function
List the parts mentioned (lips, teeth, tongue) and note the specific functions Sanusi assigns to each. Also note the two types of teeth.
3
Understand the reasoning for delayed teeth
Sanusi explains why teeth are not present at birth. Identify the two reasons he gives and reflect on the wisdom behind them.
This shows that creation happens at the optimal time.
4
Reflect on the concluding doxology
Read the final sentences praising God. Consider how the details of creation serve as signs pointing to the Creator.
Sanusi emphasizes that we see only by God's success.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم خلقَ شفتينِ ينطبقانِ على الفم ، يصونان الحلق والفم مِنَ الرياح والغبار ، وينفتحان بسهولة عند الحاجة إلى الانفتاح ، ولما فيهما أيضاً مِنْ كمال الزينة وغيرها . خلق بعدهما الأسنانَ ؛ ليتمكَّنَ بها مِنْ قطع مأكولِهِ وطحنِهِ . وجعلَ اللسانَ آلةٌ يجمعُ بهِ ما تفرَّقَ مِنَ المأكولِ في أرجاء الفم ليتمكَّنَ تسهيله للابتلاع بطحْنِ الأرحاء ، وخلق فيه معنى الذوق لكلَّ مأكول ومشروب ، ولم يخلق جلَّ وعلا الأسنانَ في أَوَّلِ الخِلقةِ ؛ لئلا يضرَّ بأُمِّهِ في حالِ رضاعِهِ بالعضُ ، ولأنَّه لا يحتاجُ لها حينَئِذٍ ؛ لضَعَفِهِ عمَّا كثفَ مِنَ الأغذية التي تفتقرُ إلى الأسنان ، فلما ترعرع وصلح للغذاء خلق له الأسنان ، وجعلها نوعين ؛ بعضُها محدودة الأطرافِ ؛ وهي التي للقطع يقطعُ بها المأكول ، وبعضُها منبسطة ؛ وهي التي للطحن ، فسبحانه ما أكثر عجائب صنعِهِ ! وأوسعَ الآياتِ الدالة عليه ! ولكنَّا لا نبصر شيئاً إلا بتوفيق الله تعالى
Then He created two lips that close over the mouth, protecting the throat and mouth from winds and dust, and they open easily when opening is needed, and also for the perfection of beauty and other things in them. After them He created the teeth, so that he can cut and grind his food with them. And He made the tongue an instrument with which he gathers what is scattered of food in the corners of the mouth to facilitate its swallowing by grinding of the molars, and He created in it the sense of taste for every food and drink. And He, Glorious and Exalted, did not create the teeth at the beginning of creation, lest he harm his mother during breastfeeding by biting, and because he did not need them then, due to his weakness from the solid foods that require teeth. When he grew up and was ready for food, He created the teeth for him, and He made them two types: some with limited edges, which are for cutting, with which he cuts food, and some are flat, which are for grinding. So glory be to Him! How abundant are the wonders of His making! And how vast are the signs indicating Him! But we do not see anything except by the success of God Most High.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage continues the teleological argument by examining the mouth. First, lips: they are designed to close the mouth, protecting the throat and mouth from wind and dust, yet open easily when needed, and also serve beauty. Then teeth: created after lips to enable cutting and grinding food. The author notes the wisdom in the delayed creation of teeth: they are not present at birth because they would harm the mother during breastfeeding and are not needed for milk. Only when the child grows and requires solid food do teeth appear. Teeth are of two types: incisors (limited edges) for cutting and molars (flat) for grinding. The tongue is described as an instrument that gathers food and aids swallowing, and it has the sense of taste. The passage ends with a doxology praising God's wonders.

Correction: Sanusi explains that teeth are created at the appropriate time (not at birth) for specific purposes: cutting and grinding. Their delayed appearance prevents harm to the mother and matches the child's dietary needs.

Wisdom in the Creation of Saliva for Swallowing
Sanusi explains the divine wisdom in the continuous saliva gland functioning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the divine wisdom in the continuous creation of saliva and its precise regulation for swallowing.

Core Idea
Saliva is a continuously gushing spring designed perfectly for nourishment.

Sanusi opens our eyes to the subtle wisdom in a seemingly ordinary bodily function. He describes saliva as a divine spring that moistens dry food, enabling it to slide down the throat with ease. This passage invites us to reflect on the intricate design that makes daily sustenance possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp Sanusi's description of saliva as a 'continuous gushing spring'.
Notice the poetic language: 'sweeter than any sweet thing'.
2
Identify the two wonders
First, the spring is continuous; second, it does not overfill the mouth. These show perfect balance.
Consider what would happen if either wonder were missing.
3
Reflect on the consequence of its absence
Sanusi mentions illness causing dryness, making swallowing difficult. This proves the necessity of saliva.
The passage ends with praise to Allah as the Best of creators.

ثم لمَّا كانَ المأكولُ شديداً كثيفاً ، ولم يكن ليجري في الفم إلى الحلْقِ وهو كذلك على يبسِهِ . . أنبعَ الله تعالى في الفم عيناً نباعةً على الدوام، أحلى مِنْ كلّ حلو ، وأعذبَ مِنْ كُلِّ عذب ، فيحرّكُ اللسان الغذاء ويمزجُهُ بذلك الماء ، فيعودُ زَلَقاً فينحدر في الحلْقِ بلا مؤنة ، ولهذا إذا أعدم الله سبحانَهُ تلكَ العينَ بخَلْقِ جفوفٍ مِنَ المرض.. لم يمض على الحلْقِ شيء ، وإن مضى فبمشقة عظيمة . ومن عجيب هذهِ العين : أنَّها مع عدم انقطاعِها لم يكنْ ماؤُها يملأُ الفم في كل وقتِ حتى يتكلَّفَ الإنسانُ مَوْنةً عظيمة في طرح ذلك عنه ، بل جرَتْ على وجهِ الجَمَتْ فيهِ أنْ تتعدَّى وجْهَ منفعتِها ، فَتَبَارَكَ اللهُ أَحْسَنُ الْخَلِقِينَ
Then, when the food was hard and dense, and it would not flow in the mouth to the throat while being dry... God, Exalted, caused to flow in the mouth a continuously gushing spring, sweeter than any sweet thing, and fresher than any fresh thing. So the tongue moves the food and mixes it with that water, and it becomes slippery and descends into the throat without effort. For this reason, if God, Glorified, deprives that spring by creating dryness from disease... nothing passes to the throat, and if it passes, it is with great difficulty. And among the wonders of this spring: that despite its continuity, its water does not fill the mouth at all times such that a person would have to undertake great effort to expel it, but it flows in a manner that does not exceed the measure of its benefit. So blessed is God, the Best of creators.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage addresses the creation of saliva, described as a 'continuously gushing spring' in the mouth. The argument is that since food is dry and dense, it cannot move easily to the throat. God therefore created saliva, which is described as sweeter and fresher than all sweet and fresh things, to moisten the food. The tongue mixes the food with saliva, making it slippery so it goes down the throat with no effort. When a person is ill and loses saliva (dryness), swallowing becomes painful or impossible, showing the necessity of this design. Another wonder is that saliva is continuous but does not fill the mouth excessively, which would require constant spitting. It flows only as needed, perfectly balanced. The passage ends with a Quranic verse praising God as the Best of creators.

Every detail of creation, even fingernails, points to purposeful divine design.

Proof That God Must Be Alive
Sanusi proves the necessity of life in God using conditional logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical necessity of God being alive as a condition for His other essential attributes.

Core Idea
God's life is the logical foundation for all His other attributes.

Sanusi has already established that God possesses attributes like power and will. Here he proves that these attributes cannot exist without the attribute of life. Using a simple conditional argument, he demonstrates that because the other attributes are necessary, their condition—life—must also be necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the compact logical style.
2
Identify the conditional relationship
Find where the author states that the previous attributes are 'conditioned' by life. Understand what it means for an attribute to be a condition.
3
Follow the logical entailment
The author argues: if life were absent, the conditioned attributes would be absent. Since the attributes are necessary, life must be necessary.
This is a classic conditional syllogism.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: why is this proof important? It establishes life as a foundational attribute without which other attributes are impossible.
This connects to the core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعني : ويجب أيضاً لصانعِكَ أنْ يكونَ حيّاً ، وإلا لزمَ ما ذكرَ وبيانُ الملازمة : أنَّ تلكَ الأوصاف السابقة ـ وهي كونه قادراً وما بعده - مشروطةٌ عقلاً بكون المتّصف بها حيّاً ، فلو قُدِّرَ عدمُهُ لوجب عدمُها ؛ لوجوب انتفاء المشروطِ عند انتفاء شرطه ، لكنَّ انتفاء تلك الأوصاف المشروطة محالٌ ؛ لوجوبها على ما تقدَّمَ ، فنفي شرطها ـ وهو كونه تعالى حياً - محال
Meaning: It is also necessary for your Maker to be alive; otherwise what was mentioned would follow. And the explanation of the entailment is that those previous attributes — namely that He is powerful and what follows — are rationally conditioned by the one described with them being alive. So if His absence were supposed, their absence would be necessary, because the conditioned must be negated when its condition is negated. But the negation of those conditioned attributes is impossible, because they are necessary as previously established. So the negation of their condition — that is, that He Almighty is alive — is impossible.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God must possess the attribute of life. The author states that the previous attributes (power, will, knowledge, etc.) are rationally conditional upon the existence of life in the one described. This is a logical entailment: if something is conditioned, the absence of the condition necessitates the absence of the conditioned. Since those attributes are necessary for God (as proven earlier), their absence is impossible, so the absence of their condition (life) is also impossible. Therefore, God must be alive.

Key Terms
حي
قادر
شرط
مشروط
ملازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Life is an optional attribute for God; He could be powerful without being alive.
Correction: The passage proves that life is a necessary condition for the other attributes; if God were not alive, His power, will, etc. would be impossible.

Checkpoint

A) Because life is a praiseworthy attribute.
B) Because His other necessary attributes depend on life as a condition.
C) Because life is mentioned in the Quran.
D) Because all living things have a creator.

Key Takeaway
God must be alive because His other necessary attributes depend on life as a condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson proves that God's life is the foundation for His power, knowledge, and will. When you face a situation where you feel God is distant or unaware, are you treating Him as if He were not alive? ?

?
Conditional logic makes it clear: God's life is non-negotiable. Does your heart truly rest in the fact that the All-Powerful, All-Knowing God is alive right now? What would change in your prayers, your fears, or your hopes if you fully internalized that truth?

Proof That God Must Be Hearing, Seeing, and Speaking
Sanusi proves the necessity of God's attributes of hearing, seeing, and speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for why God must possess the attributes of hearing, seeing, and speech, based on the impossibility of their opposites being defects.

Core Idea
God's perfection necessitates His hearing, seeing, and speech.

In this lesson, Sanusi presents a concise yet powerful argument for God's attributes of hearing, seeing, and speech. The logic is rooted in the nature of living beings and the absolute self-sufficiency of God. By understanding that the opposites of these attributes are defects, and defects are impossible for God, we affirm these attributes as necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage ends with an incomplete sentence; focus on the main argument.
2
Identify the main premise
The argument relies on the premise that every living being capable of an attribute must have either that attribute or its opposite. Find this statement in the passage.
3
Understand why opposites are impossible for God
Sanusi argues that the opposites (deafness, blindness, muteness) are defects and imperfections. Defects imply neediness, which contradicts God's absolute self-sufficiency (ghani).
Recall that God is described as 'al-Ghani' (the Self-Sufficient).
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the opposites are impossible for God, and every living being must have either the attribute or its opposite, God must necessarily have the perfect attributes of hearing, seeing, and speech.

Source Text

بأضدادها ، وأضدادها آفات ونقص ، وهي عليه محال ؛ لاحتياجهِ حينَئِذٍ إلى مَنْ يُكمّلُهُ ، كيف وهو الغني بإطلاقِ

And [we affirm that He is] Hearing, Seeing, and Speaking; otherwise, because He is alive, He would be described by their opposites. And their opposites are defects and imperfections, and they are impossible for Him, because He would then need someone to perfect Him. How, when He is absolutely self-sufficient, and everything other than Him is in need of Him? 6 That is: It is necessary for your Maker to be Hearing, Seeing, and Speaking; because every living being capable of an attribute does not lack it except for either its like or its opposite, according to what you have known previously (and we will repeat it later) of the impossibility of a receptive being being devoid of the genus of what is received. And the proof that every living being is capable of being described by these attributes or their opposites:
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God must have the attributes of hearing, seeing, and speech. The author argues that since God is alive, if He did not have these perfect attributes, He would have their opposites (deafness, blindness, muteness), which are defects. Defects are impossible for God because they imply neediness, whereas God is absolutely self-sufficient. The author then restates the argument more formally: every living being capable of an attribute must have either that attribute or its opposite; it cannot be devoid of both. Since the opposites are impossible for God, the perfect attributes are necessary. The passage ends with an incomplete statement about the proof for this principle.

Correction: This passage treats them as necessary literal attributes, proven by rational argument that their opposites are defects impossible for God.

The Necessity of God Being Alive
Sanusi proves God must be alive as a condition for His other attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God must be alive as a necessary condition for His other attributes like power.

Core Idea
Life is the logical condition for all of God's other essential attributes.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a compact proof that God must be alive. He argues that the attributes we have already established—such as power—are rationally dependent on the subject being alive. If God were not alive, those attributes would vanish, but they are necessary, so life is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice how Sanusi structures the argument as a conditional: if life is negated, then the conditioned attributes are negated.
The phrase 'وإلا لزم ما ذكر' indicates the consequence of denying life.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the terms 'مشروط' (conditioned) and 'شرط' (condition). Understand their logical relationship: if the condition is absent, the conditioned must be absent.
Sanusi relies on the rational principle: انتفاء المشروط عند انتفاء شرطه.
3
Follow the logical chain
Trace the proof: (1) Attributes like power are conditioned on life. (2) If life were absent, those attributes would be absent. (3) But those attributes are necessary for God (proved earlier). (4) Therefore, life cannot be absent—God must be alive.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: denying life leads to an impossible conclusion.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why does Sanusi need to prove life as a condition? How does this fit into the overall project of establishing God's attributes?
Life is foundational—without it, no other attribute can be meaningfully affirmed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعني : ويجب أيضاً لصانعِكَ أنْ يكونَ حيّاً ، وإلا لزمَ ما ذكرَ وبيانُ الملازمة : أنَّ تلكَ الأوصاف السابقة ـ وهي كونه قادراً وما بعده - مشروطةٌ عقلاً بكون المتّصف بها حيّاً ، فلو قُدِّرَ عدمُهُ لوجب عدمُها ؛ لوجوب انتفاء المشروطِ عند انتفاء شرطه ، لكنَّ انتفاء تلك الأوصاف المشروطة محالٌ ؛ لوجوبها على ما تقدَّمَ ، فنفي شرطها ـ وهو كونه تعالى حياً - محال
He means: It is also necessary for your Maker that He be living, otherwise what was mentioned would follow. And the demonstration of the implication: those previous attributes—namely His being powerful and what follows—are rationally conditioned by the one described with them being living. So if His non-existence [as living] were supposed, their non-existence would be necessary; because the conditioned must cease when its condition ceases. But the non-existence of those conditioned attributes is impossible, due to their necessity as established before. So the negation of their condition—namely His being exalted and living—is impossible.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of God being alive. Sanusi states that the attributes established earlier (power, etc.) are rationally conditioned upon life in the subject. If God were not alive, those attributes would not exist, but they are necessary for God, so their non-existence is impossible. Hence, the condition (life) must exist. He structures it as a conditional: if life is negated, the conditioned attributes are negated, but they cannot be negated, so life cannot be negated.

Key Terms

المشروط
الشرط
القدرة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think life is an additional attribute that could be absent without affecting other attributes.
Correction: Sanusi shows that life is a necessary condition for the other attributes; without it, they cannot exist. So life is not optional.

Checkpoint
Why must God be alive according to Sanusi?
A) Because life is an intrinsic perfection
B) Because His other necessary attributes are conditioned upon life
C) Because the Quran says so
D) Because all living things need a creator

Key Takeaway
God must be alive because the attributes we already know He must possess (like power) logically depend on life as a condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's life is the condition for His power and knowledge, how does that change the way you think about His constant awareness of your actions? Are you living as if every moment is witnessed by a living, powerful, and knowing Creator?
?
This proof shows that God's life is logically necessary—not an optional attribute. Does your heart truly affirm this necessity, or do you sometimes act as if God is 'alive' in a vague, powerless sense? What would it mean for you to deeply internalize that His life is the foundation of all His attributes?
?
The argument forces you to confront a contradiction: if you deny God's life, you must also deny His power and knowledge. Have you ever, even unintentionally, doubted God's life in times of hardship? How can you strengthen your certainty in this rational truth today?

The Rational Necessity of God's Hearing, Seeing, and Speaking
Sanusi presents a rational argument for God's hearing, seeing, and speaking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that God must be described as hearing, seeing, and speaking because the alternatives are impossible for a perfect being.

Core Idea
God's perfection demands the perfect attributes; defects are impossible.

Sanusi opens with a logical principle: any living being capable of a quality must possess either that quality or its opposite. Since God is living and absolutely perfect, He must have the perfect attributes of hearing, seeing, and speaking. This lesson walks through the steps of that argument.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the fundamental principle
Read the first part of the passage where Sanusi states that every living being capable of a quality is never free from it except to its like or opposite. This is the key rule.
This principle is derived from the impossibility of a substance being empty of a genus of accidents.
2
Apply the principle to God
Understand that God is living and capable of these attributes. Therefore, He must either have hearing, seeing, and speaking or their opposites: deafness, blindness, muteness.
God's life is established earlier in the text.
3
Eliminate the opposites
Sanusi argues that the opposites are defects and imperfections, which are impossible for God. Defects imply need and temporality, contradictory to God's necessary existence.
The argument also notes that if God had defects, a creature with perfections would be more perfect than the Creator, which is absurd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي : ويجب لصانعِكَ أنْ يكونَ سميعاً بصيراً مُتكلّماً؛ لأنَّ كلَّ حي قابل لصفةٍ فإنَّهُ لا يخلو عنها إلا إلى مثلها أو ضدها؛ لما عرفت ـ فيما سبق، وسنعيده فيما يأتي - مِن استحالة عرو القابل عن جنس المقبول. ودليلُ أنَّ كلَّ حي قابل للاتصاف بهذه الصفات أو أضدادها: امتناع اتصاف الموتى بها، وصحَّةُ اتصافِ الأحياء بها، فالمصحح إذاً لقبولِ هذه الصفاتِ: إمَّا الحياةُ، أو أمر يلازم الحياةَ، وأياً ما كان يلزمُ عليهِ قبولُ اتّصافِ كلَّ حيّ بها، فإذا لم يتّصِفِ الحيُّ بكونه سميعاً بصيراً مُتكلّماً .. لزمَ أن يتصف بأضدادها؛ وهي كونه أصم أعمى أبكم، لكن هذه الأضداد في حقِّهِ تعالى مستحيلةٌ؛ لكونها آفات ونقائص، وهو جلَّ وعلا مُنزَّه عن كل نقص نقلاً وعقلاً؛ لأنَّ الناقص مُفتقِرُ إِلى مَنْ يُكمِّلُهُ، وذلك يستلزم حدوثَهُ، والحدوث والافتقارُ على واجب الوجودِ الغني بإطلاق المفتقِرِ إليه كلُّ ما سواه .. مستحيلان على الضرورة، ويلزمُ على تقدير تلك النقائص أنْ يكونَ المخلوق المتصف بالكمالاتِ أضدادِها .. أكملَ مِنَ الخالقِ! وذلكَ ممَّا لا يُعقَلُ. [ أدلة كونه تعالى سميعاً بصيراً متكلما سمعية ] والتحقيق : الاعتماد في هذه الثلاثة على الدليل السمعي؛ لأنَّ ذاتَهُ تعالى لم تُعرَف حتى يُحكَمَ في حقِّهِ بأَنَّهُ يجب الاتصافُ بأضدادها عند عدمها
Meaning: It is also necessary for your Maker that He be hearing, seeing, and speaking; because every living being capable of a quality is not free from it except to its like or its opposite, due to what you have learned—in what preceded, and we will repeat it later—of the impossibility of the capable being empty of the genus of the accepted. And the proof that every living being is capable of being described with these attributes or their opposites: the impossibility of the dead being described with them, and the validity of the living being described with them. So the predisposing factor for accepting these attributes is either life or something concomitant with life. Whichever it is, it follows that every living being accepts being described with them. So if the living being is not described as hearing, seeing, speaking, it necessarily follows that it be described with their opposites: deaf, blind, mute. But these opposites are impossible with respect to Him, the Exalted, because they are defects and imperfections, and He, may He be glorified and exalted, is transcendent above every imperfection by transmitted and rational proof; because the imperfect is in need of one who perfects it, and that necessitates its temporality. Temporality and neediness are necessary impossible for the Necessary Existence, the absolutely independent, to whom all others are in need. And it follows, upon supposing those imperfections, that the creature described with perfections opposite to them would be more perfect than the Creator! And that is inconceivable. [The proofs of His being hearing, seeing, speaking are scriptural.] The verified position: reliance in these three is upon scriptural evidence; because His essence, exalted, is not known such that it be judged about Him that it is necessary to be described with their opposites when they are absent.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of God being hearing, seeing, and speaking, followed by a methodological note. Sanusi first establishes a principle: every living being capable of a quality must either have that quality or its opposite; it cannot be empty of both. He then proves that every living being is capable of these attributes (since the dead cannot have them, the living can). Therefore, a living being must either have hearing, seeing, and speaking or their opposites (deafness, blindness, muteness). Since God is living, He must have one of the two sets. The opposites are imperfections, and God is perfect, so He must have hearing, seeing, and speaking. Sanusi supports this by noting that imperfections imply need for a perfecter, which would entail temporality and need—impossible for God. Moreover, if God had defects, a creature with the corresponding perfections would be more perfect than God, which is absurd. Finally, Sanusi notes that while this rational argument establishes the necessity of these attributes, the ultimate reliance for affirming them is scriptural evidence (Sam'iyyat), because God's essence is not known otherwise.

Key Terms
السميع
البصير
المتكلم
الأضداد
النقائص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's attributes are arbitrary or that reason alone cannot prove they are necessary.
Correction: Sanusi shows that reason demonstrates the necessity of these attributes because the alternative (defects) is rationally impossible for God.

Checkpoint
What is the fundamental principle Sanusi uses in his rational argument for God's attributes?
A) Every living being must have hearing, seeing, and speaking.
B) Every living being capable of a quality must have either that quality or its opposite.
C) God's essence is unknown, so we rely on scripture.
D) The dead and living are equally capable of attributes.

Key Takeaway
God must be hearing, seeing, and speaking because the alternative would entail defects, which are impossible for the perfect, necessary Being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God were not hearing, seeing, speaking—if He were unaware of your cries, blind to your struggles, silent in your life—would your faith survive? How does the certainty that He possesses these perfect attributes change the way you speak to Him today?
?
The proof rests on the principle that imperfection implies need. What imperfections in your own character are you comfortable with, even though they reveal your need for God? Are you willing to let Him perfect you, or do you prefer the comfort of your flaws?
?
Sanusi says that if God lacked these attributes, a creature with them would be more perfect than the Creator. Do you ever act as though God is less aware or less capable than you? In what area of your life do you rely on your own sight, hearing, or speech instead of trusting His?

Reliance on Scriptural Evidence for Divine Attributes
Sanusi states that the proof for God's hearing, seeing, and speaking is ultimately scriptural.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the affirmation of God's hearing, seeing, and speaking ultimately depends on scriptural evidence rather than pure reason.

Core Idea
Reason shows necessity; scripture reveals the specific attributes.

After presenting a rational argument for God's hearing, seeing, and speaking, Sanusi makes a crucial methodological point: the ultimate proof for these attributes is scriptural (sam'ī). This lesson explains why reason alone cannot finalize the matter and why revelation is essential.

Guided Path
1
Read the verification statement
Read Sanusi's concluding remark: 'The verified position: reliance in these three is upon scriptural evidence.' Notice the shift from rational to revealed proof.
The word 'al-tahqīq' (التحقيق) indicates the author's settled position.
2
Understand the reason
Sanusi explains that God's essence is not known directly, so we cannot judge by reason alone that He must have these specific attributes. Instead, we rely on revelation to tell us which perfect attributes He possesses.
This does not negate the rational argument; it limits its scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Divine Attributes
