Rational Proof for Attributes and the Need for Revelation
Sanusi presents and critiques the rational argument for divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument for God's hearing, sight, and speech, and why it is insufficient without transmitted evidence.

Core Idea
Rational perfection in this world does not guarantee perfection in the divine.

Sanusi presents a rational argument that seems to prove God must be described with hearing, sight, and speech, or else He would be imperfect. But then he shows why this reasoning is weak, using the example of pleasure and pain. The lesson introduces the crucial distinction between the witnessed and unseen realms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the rational argument's steps
The passage first builds a rational case: every living being must have hearing, sight, and speech, or else be described with their opposites, which are imperfections. Since God cannot be imperfect, He must have these attributes.
Pay attention to the logical chain: life → attributes or opposites → opposites are defects → defects impossible → attributes necessary.
3
Understand the critique
Sanusi then argues that this rational proof is weak. He explains that what is a perfection in this world (shahid) may not be a perfection in the unseen (gha'ib), using pleasure and pain as an example.
The key point: we cannot infer divine attributes simply from human experience.
4
Note the final conclusion
Sanusi concludes that for these three attributes, we rely on transmitted evidence (Qur'an and Sunnah). Reason alone is insufficient; we need revelation.
This illustrates an important methodological principle in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
امتناع اتصاف الموتى بها ، وصحَّةُ اتصافِ الأحياء بها ، فالمصحح إذاً لقبولِ هذه الصفاتِ : إمَّا الحياةُ ، أو أمر يلازم الحياةَ ، وأياً ما كان يلزمُ عليهِ قبولُ اتّصافِ كلِّ حيّ بها ، فإذا لم يتّصِفِ الحيُّ بكونه سميعاً بصيراً مُتكلّماً .. لزمَ أنْ يتصف بأضدادها ؛ وهي كونه أصم أعمى أبكم ، لكن هذه الأضداد في حقِّهِ تعالى مستحيلةٌ ؛ لكونها آفات ونقائص ، وهو جلَّ وعلا مُنزَّه عن كل نقص نقلاً وعقلاً ؛ لأنَّ الناقص مُفتقِرُ إِلى مَنْ يُكمِّلُهُ ، وذلك يستلزم حدوثَهُ ، والحدوث والافتقارُ على واجب الوجودِ الغني بإطلاق المفتقِرِ إليه كلُّ ما سواه . . مستحيلان على الضرورة ، ويلزمُ على تقدير تلك النقائص أنْ يكونَ المخلوق المتصف بالكمالاتِ أضدادِها . . أكملَ مِنَ الخالقِ ! وذلكَ ممَّا لا يُعقَلُ . أدلة كونه تعالى سميعاً بصيراً متكلما سمعية والتحقيق : الاعتماد في هذه الثلاثة على الدليل السمعي ؛ لأنَّ ذاتَهُ تعالى لم تُعرَف حتى يُحكَمَ في حقِّهِ بأَنَّهُ يجب الاتصافُ بأضدادها عند عدمها يعني : أنَّ الاعتماد في ثبوتِ تلكَ الأوصاف على الدليل العقليّ ؛ مِنْ كون تلك الأوصاف كمالاتِ ، فيجب اتصافه بها ، وإلا لاتّصفَ بأضدادها ، فيكون ناقصاً ؛ لأنَّه قد فاته الكمال ، وفوتُ الكمال نقصان .. ضعيفٌ ؛ لأنَّهُ إنَّما ثبت لتلك الأوصاف الكمال في الشاهد ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ كونِ الشيء كمالاً في الشاهد أنْ يكونَ في الغائبِ كذلك. ألا ترى أنَّ اللَّذةَ والألم في الشاهدِ كمال ، وهما ممتنعان على الله تعالى ؛ لأنهما مِنْ عوارض الأجسام ، وذاته جلَّ وعلا لم تُعرَف حتى يُعلَمَ أنَّ هذه الأوصاف كمالات في حقه تعالى يصح اتصافه بها بحيثُ يلزم إذا لم يتّصف بها أنْ يتّصف بأضدادها ، وإِنَّما نعرفُ مِنْ صفاتِهِ جلَّ وعلا بالعقل ما دلّتْ عليه أفعاله ، فإِنْ لم يدلَّ الفعلُ لجأنا إلى السمع ، فإن لم يَرِدْ وجبَ الوقف ولا شك أنَّ السمع وارد في هذه الصفاتِ الثلاثِ
The impossibility of the dead being described with them, and the correctness of the living being described with them. So the corrector for accepting these attributes is either life or something concomitant with life, and whichever it is, it necessitates the acceptance of the description of every living being with them. So if a living being is not described as hearing, seeing, speaking, it would necessitate that he be described with their opposites: deaf, blind, mute. But these opposites with respect to Him, exalted, are impossible, because they are defects and imperfections, and He, glorified and exalted, is transcendent of every imperfection by transmitted proof and by reason, because the imperfect is in need of one who perfects him, and that entails his temporality, and temporality and neediness with respect to the Necessary Existent, the absolutely self-sufficient, to Whom everything other than Him is in need, are necessarily impossible. And it would necessitate, on the assumption of those imperfections, that the creature described with the perfections (their opposites) would be more perfect than the Creator! And that is inconceivable. [Heading:] Transmitted proofs for His being Hearing, Seeing, Speaking. And the verification: reliance for these three is on transmitted evidence, because His Essence, exalted, is not known such that it be judged regarding Him that He must be described with the opposites when they are absent. That is, reliance in establishing those attributes on rational proof – from the fact that those attributes are perfections, so He must be described with them, otherwise He would be described with their opposites, and be imperfect, because He would have missed perfection, and missing perfection is deficiency – is weak. Because the perfection of those attributes is only established in the witnessed realm, and it does not necessarily follow that because something is a perfection in the witnessed realm it is likewise in the unseen. Do you not see that pleasure and pain in the witnessed realm are perfection, yet they are impossible for God, exalted, because they are accidents of bodies? And His Essence, glorified and exalted, is not known such that it be known that these attributes are perfections with respect to Him, exalted, such that He may be described with them in a way that if He is not described with them He must be described with their opposites. Rather, we know from His attributes, glorified and exalted, by reason only what His actions indicate. So if the action does not indicate, we resort to transmitted evidence. If none comes, then suspension is obligatory, and there is no doubt that transmitted evidence has come concerning these three attributes.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage presents and then critiques the rational argument for God's attributes of hearing, sight, and speech. It begins by establishing that the dead cannot possess these attributes, while the living can, so the basis for possessing them is life or something concomitant. If a living being lacks them, it must have their opposites (deafness, blindness, muteness), which are imperfections. For God, such imperfections are impossible because He is transcendent of all imperfection by both revelation and reason; an imperfect being needs a perfecter, which implies temporality and need – impossible for the Necessary Existent. Moreover, if God lacked these attributes, a creature possessing the perfections would be more perfect than the Creator, which is absurd. This seems to prove the necessity of these attributes rationally. However, the author then says 'the verification is reliance on transmitted evidence for these three' because God's essence is not known to us in advance, so we cannot judge that He must have the opposites if the attributes are absent. This is explained in further detail: the rational argument from perfection is weak because it argues that since these attributes are perfections in the witnessed world, God must have them in the unseen world. But that inference doesn't hold: what is a perfection in the witnessed realm may not be in the unseen. For example, pleasure and pain are perfections in this world but are impossible for God because they are accidents of bodies. Therefore, we cannot know by reason alone that these attributes are perfections in God's case. Instead, we know God's attributes by reason only from His actions. If actions indicate an attribute, we accept it; otherwise we look to revelation. Since revelation has indeed come concerning these three attributes (hearing, sight, speech), we rely on revelation, not pure reason.

Key Terms
سميع
بصير
متكلم
كمال
نقص
شاهد
غائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because hearing, sight, and speech are perfections in humans, God must logically possess them.
Correction: Perfection in this world does not automatically apply to God. What is a perfection for created beings may not be suitable for the Creator. For example, pleasure and pain are perfections in humans but impossible for God.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the purely rational argument for God's hearing, sight, and speech?
A) Because reason cannot prove any divine attributes.
B) Because the perfection of these attributes is only established in the witnessed realm, and does not necessarily transfer to the unseen.
C) Because the Quran explicitly denies these attributes.
D) Because the attributes are not perfections at all.

Key Takeaway
Faith in God's attributes of hearing, sight, and speech is based on revealed texts, not purely on rational necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes, do you find yourself unconsciously measuring Him by human standards of perfection? Where in your life has this assumption led you to rely on your own logic over what God has revealed? What would it cost you to submit fully to revelation on matters where reason is silent?
?
Consider a time you assumed that something 'good' or 'perfect' by human measure must be good for God – like pleasure, power, or knowledge as we know it. How does this lesson challenge that assumption? Are you willing to let revelation define what is perfect for God, even if it goes against your instinct?
?
The passage says God's essence is not known to us. How does that make you feel – uncomfortable or liberated? Does your prayer life reflect a God whose nature is known only through His words, or one you have constructed from your own ideals? What step can you take today to let scripture shape your understanding of God more than your own reasoning?

Transmitted Proofs for God's Hearing and Sight
Sanusi cites Quranic verses as transmitted proofs for God's attributes of hearing and sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses Quranic verses and the argument of Abraham as transmitted proofs for God's attributes of hearing and sight.

Core Idea
Revelation, not reason, is the primary proof for God's hearing and sight.

In the previous lesson, Sanusi explained that rational arguments alone cannot establish that God hears and sees. Now he turns to the Quran itself, citing verses that explicitly affirm these attributes. He also invokes the story of Abraham, who argued that true divinity must hear and see, and Allah confirmed his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the flow of Sanusi's argument.
Notice the structure: first a list of Quranic verses, then the Abrahamic argument.
2
Identify the Quranic verses
Note each verse Sanusi cites, and consider how each directly attributes hearing or sight to Allah.
The verses are from Surah Ta-Ha, Ash-Shura, Al-Alaq, and Ash-Shu'ara.
3
Understand the Abrahamic argument
See how Abraham's question to his father implies that a true God must hear and see, and how Allah endorses this reasoning.
Allah confirms that this was His argument given to Abraham.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Consider why Sanusi emphasizes transmitted proofs for these particular attributes.
Rational proofs are insufficient, so revelation is necessary.

فمنه في إثباتِ كونه تعالى سميعاً بصيراً : قوله تعالى : ( إِنَّنِي مَعَكُمَا أَسْمَعُ وَأَرَى ) : [ طه : ٤٦] ، وكقوله تعالى : ﴿ وَهُوَ السَّمِيعُ الْبَصِيرُ ﴾ [الشورى : ١١] ، وكقوله جلَّ وعلا : أَلَمْ يَعْلَمْ بِأَنَّ اللَّهَ يَرَى [العلق : ١٤] ، وكقوله جلَّ اسمُهُ : الَّذِي يَرَاكَ حِينَ تَقُومُ ﴾ [الشعراء : ۲۱۸] ، واحتجاج إبراهيم عليه السلام في نفي اللهيَّة الأصنام بقوله تعالى : لِمَ تَعْبُدُ مَا لَا يَسْمَعُ وَلَا يُبْصِرُ ) : [مريم : ٤٢] ، فلو كانَ معبوده كذلك لم تتم لهُ حُجَّةٌ ، وقد قال تعالى : وَتِلْكَ حُجَّتُنَا ءَاتَيْنَهَا إِبْرَاهِيمَ عَلَى قَوْمِهِ ﴾ [الأنعام : ٨٣] وإذا ثبت أنَّ الاتصاف بهاتين الصفتين لا يتوقف عقلاً على
So from it [the transmitted evidence] in proving that He, exalted, is Hearing, Seeing: His statement, exalted: 'Indeed, I am with you; I hear and I see' [Ta-Ha 20:46]; and His statement, exalted: 'And He is the Hearing, the Seeing' [Ash-Shura 42:11]; and His statement, glorified and exalted: 'Does he not know that Allah sees?' [Al-Alaq 96:14]; and His statement, glorified be His name: 'Who sees you when you arise' [Ash-Shu'ara 26:218]; and the argument of Abraham, peace be upon him, in negating divinity from idols with His statement, exalted: 'Why do you worship that which does not hear and does not see?' [Maryam 19:42]; for if his worshipped were like that, his argument would not be complete, and He, exalted, said: 'And that is Our argument which We gave to Abraham against his people' [Al-An'am 6:83]. And if it is established that the description with these two attributes does not depend, by reason, on
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage provides the transmitted proofs (al-sam'ī) for God's attributes of hearing and sight from the Quran. After establishing in the previous lesson that rational argument alone is insufficient, the author now cites several verses. First, from Surah Ta-Ha (20:46), where Allah tells Moses and Aaron: 'Indeed, I am with you; I hear and I see.' Second, from Surah Ash-Shura (42:11): 'And He is the Hearing, the Seeing.' Third, from Surah Al-Alaq (96:14): 'Does he not know that Allah sees?' Fourth, from Surah Ash-Shu'ara (26:218): 'Who sees you when you arise.' Fifth, the argument of Prophet Abraham in Surah Maryam (19:42), where he says to his father: 'Why do you worship that which does not hear and does not see?' The author notes that if Abraham's worshipped (idols) were indeed unable to hear or see, his argument would not be effective; but Allah confirms that this was His argument given to Abraham. Thus, the Quran itself affirms that God hears and sees, and the argument from Abraham's story reinforces that these attributes are essential for divinity. The passage ends mid-sentence: 'And if it is established that the description with these two attributes does not depend, by reason, on...' suggesting an ongoing argument that reason alone cannot establish them, but revelation does.

Critique of Rational Proof and Scriptural Affirmation of Hearing and Sight
Sanusi refutes rational perfection proof and affirms hearing and sight via scripture with literal interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why rational arguments alone are insufficient to prove divine attributes like hearing and sight, and how scripture provides definitive proof that must be interpreted literally.

Core Idea
Perfection in the witnessed does not guarantee perfection in the unseen; scripture is the final authority for hearing and sight.

Sanusi begins by challenging a common rationalist approach to divine attributes. He shows that simply labeling something a 'perfection' in our world does not make it applicable to God. Instead, he establishes a careful methodology: reason gives us some knowledge from God's actions, but for attributes like hearing and sight, we must turn to the Qur'an. This passage defends the literal meaning of scripture against metaphorical interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the flow from critique to scriptural evidence to methodological rule.
Pay attention to the example of pleasure and pain.
2
Identify the rational argument and its weakness
Find the statement of the rational perfection argument and Sanusi's critique using the shahid/gha'ib distinction.
He calls it 'weak' (ضعيف).
3
Understand the correct method
Notice the three-step method: reason from actions, then scripture, then stop if no evidence. This is key to avoiding speculative theology.
4
Review the scriptural proofs
Go through each Qur'anic verse Sanusi cites for hearing and sight. Consider how they are used as definitive evidence.
Note Abraham's argument against idols.
5
Learn the rule about apparent meanings
Understand why carrying a word to a remote meaning (majaz) requires a contextual indicator (qarina) and why literal interpretation is preferred when possible.
This rule applies to all future matters of the afterlife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعني : أنَّ الاعتماد في ثبوتِ تلكَ الأوصاف على الدليل العقليّ ؛ مِنْ كون تلك الأوصاف كمالاتٍ ، فيجب اتصافه بها ، وإلا لاتصف بأضدادها ، فيكون ناقصاً ؛ لأنَّه قد فاته الكمال ، وفوتُ الكمال نقصان .. ضعيفٌ ؛ لأنَّهُ إنَّما ثبت لتلك الأوصاف الكمال في الشاهد ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ كونِ الشيء كمالاً في الشاهد أنْ يكونَ في الغائبِ كذلك ألا ترى أنَّ اللذة والألم في الشاهدِ كمال ، وهما ممتنعان على الله تعالى ؛ لأنهما مِنْ عوارض الأجسام ، وذاته جلَّ وعلا لم تُعرَف حتى يُعلم أنَّ هذه الأوصاف كمالات في حقه تعالى يصح اتصافه بها بحيثُ يلزم إذا لم يتصف بها أنْ يتصف بأضدادها ، وإِنَّما نعرفُ مِنْ صفاتِهِ جلَّ وعلا بالعقل ما دلّتْ عليه أفعاله ، فإِنْ لم يدلَّ الفعلُ لجأنا إلى السمع ، فإن لم يَرِدْ وجبَ الوقف ولا شك أنَّ السمع وارد في هذه الصفاتِ الثلاثِ ؛ فمنه في إثباتِ كونه تعالى سميعاً بصيراً : قوله تعالى : ( إِنَّنِي مَعَكُمَا أَسْمَعُ وَأَرَى ) : [ طه : ٤٦] ، وكقوله تعالى : ﴿ وَهُوَ السَّمِيعُ الْبَصِيرُ ﴾ [الشورى : ١١] ، وكقوله جلَّ وعلا : ( أَلَمْ يَعْلَمْ بِأَنَّ اللَّهَ يَرَىٰ ) [العلق : ١٤] ، وكقوله جلَّ اسمُهُ : ( الَّذِي يَرَاكَ حِينَ تَقُومُ ) [الشعراء : ٢١٨] ، واحتجاج إبراهيم عليه السلام في نفي الألوهية عن الأصنام بقوله تعالى : ( يَا أَبَتِ لِمَ تَعْبُدُ مَا لَا يَسْمَعُ وَلَا يُبْصِرُ ) [مريم : ٤٢] ، فلو كان معبوده كذلك لم تتم لهُ حُجَّةٌ ، وقد قال تعالى : ( وَتِلْكَ حُجَّتُنَا آتَيْنَاهَا إِبْرَاهِيمَ عَلَى قَوْمِهِ ) [الأنعام : ٨٣] . وإذا ثبت أنَّ الاتصاف بهاتين الصفتين لا يتوقف عقلاً على الاتصالات الجسمانية ، ودلَّ التصريح بهما على أنهما صفتا كمال . وجب اعتقاد ما دلت عليه الآي ، ولا محوج للتأويل لا عقلاً ولا سمعاً، وحمل اللفظ على احتماله البعيد مجاز ، وشرطه القرينة ، ومع عدمِها لا يجوزُ المصيرُ إِليه ؛ لما فيهِ مِنْ إثباتِ المشروط بدونِ شرطِهِ ، فتعيَّنَ البقاء مع تلك الظواهر ، وهكذا القولُ ما وردَ مِنْ أحكام الآخرة ، متى كان ظاهره جائزاً وجب اعتقاده ، إلا أن يدل دليل على امتناعِهِ .
Meaning: that reliance in establishing those attributes is upon rational proof; that those attributes are perfections, so it is necessary for Him to be described with them, otherwise He would be described with their opposites, thus He would be deficient, because He would have missed perfection, and missing perfection is deficiency. [This is] weak, because perfection for those attributes is only established in the witnessed [world], and it does not necessarily follow from something being perfection in the witnessed that it is likewise in the unseen. Do you not see that pleasure and pain in the witnessed are perfection, while they are impossible for God Almighty, because they are accidents of bodies, and His Essence, glorified and exalted, is not known such that it is known that these attributes are perfections with respect to Him so that He may be described with them such that if He is not described with them He would necessarily be described with their opposites. Rather, we know from His attributes, glorified and exalted, through reason only what His actions indicate. So if the action does not indicate [something], we resort to scripture. If nothing comes [from scripture], stopping is obligatory. And there is no doubt that scripture is present regarding these three attributes. So from it regarding establishing His being Hearing and Seeing: His saying, 'Indeed I am with you, hearing and seeing' [20:46], and His saying, 'And He is the Hearing, the Seeing' [42:11], and His saying, 'Does he not know that Allah sees?' [96:14], and His saying, 'Who sees you when you stand' [26:218], and Abraham's argument against idolatry by saying, 'O my father, why do you worship what does not hear and does not see?' [19:42]; if his worshipped were like that, his argument would not be complete, and Allah said, 'And that is Our argument which We gave Abraham against his people' [6:83]. And if it is established that being described with these two attributes does not depend rationally on corporeal connections, and the explicit [scriptural] mention indicates that they are perfections, then it is obligatory to believe what the verses indicate, and there is no need for interpretation, neither rationally nor scripturally. Carrying the word to its remote probability is metaphor, and its condition is a contextual indicator. In its absence, it is not permissible to resort to it, because that would entail establishing the conditioned without its condition. So remaining with those apparent meanings is determined. And such is the case with whatever comes from rulings of the afterlife: whenever its apparent meaning is possible, it is obligatory to believe it, unless a proof indicates its impossibility.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a purely rational proof for God's attributes of hearing and sight, followed by the scriptural proof and a methodological principle for interpreting such texts. Sanusi begins by presenting and then critiquing the rational argument: some argue that these attributes are perfections, so God must possess them, otherwise He would have their opposite (deficiency). Sanusi calls this argument 'weak' because it assumes that what is a perfection in the witnessed world (shahid) is necessarily a perfection in the unseen (gha'ib). He gives the example of pleasure and pain, which are perfections in this world but impossible for God because they are accidents of bodies. Thus, we cannot simply transfer judgments from the witnessed to the unseen. He then states the correct method: reason only knows God's attributes from His actions; if actions do not indicate something, we resort to scripture (sam'), and if scripture is silent, we stop. For the attributes of hearing and sight, scripture is definitive. Sanusi cites several Qur'anic verses explicitly affirming that God hears and sees, including the story of Abraham who used the lack of hearing and sight in idols as an argument against their divinity. After establishing the scriptural evidence, he argues that since these attributes do not require a body (they are not corporeal), and scripture declares them perfections, we must believe them literally without ta'wil (interpretive distortion). He explains that carrying a word to a remote meaning is majaz (metaphor), which requires a contextual indicator (qarina); without it, it is invalid because that would be establishing the conditioned without its condition. Therefore, we must remain with the apparent meanings (zawahir). He extends this principle to all matters of the afterlife: if the apparent meaning is possible (jai'z), we must believe it unless a proof shows its impossibility. This passage thus defends the literal affirmation of attributes as revealed, against both rational speculation and arbitrary metaphorical interpretation.

Key Terms
الشاهد (the witnessed)
الغائب (the unseen)
الكمال (perfection)
السمع (scriptural proof)
التأويل (interpretive distortion)
المجاز (metaphor)
القرينة (contextual indicator)
الظواهر (apparent meanings)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is a perfection in humans, it must be a perfection in God, so God must have it.
Correction: Sanusi shows that perfection in the witnessed world does not automatically apply to the unseen, as with pleasure and pain which are perfections for humans but impossible for God.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is the rational argument from perfection insufficient to prove God's hearing and sight?
A) Because perfection in the witnessed does not necessarily apply to the unseen.
B) Because hearing and sight are corporeal attributes.
C) Because scripture prohibits rational inquiry into divine attributes.
D) Because the argument is not mentioned in the Qur'an.

Key Takeaway
Scriptural texts about God's attributes must be taken in their apparent meaning unless a clear proof prevents it, and rational perfection arguments alone are insufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself explaining away Qur'anic descriptions of God's hearing and sight because they seem too anthropomorphic? How does Sanusi's principle challenge you to submit to the text even when it makes you uncomfortable?
?
When you think about God's attributes, do you rely more on what seems 'perfect' to your own mind, or on what scripture explicitly says? What would it cost you to truly let scripture shape your understanding even in areas that feel uncertain?
?
Sanusi extends his principle to the afterlife: believe the apparent meaning unless impossible. Is there a verse about Paradise or Hell that you struggle to take literally? Will you commit to trusting its apparent meaning today, and ask God to increase your certainty?

Proof from God's Kingship
Abu Ishaq argues that God must be mutakallim because He is a king.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Ishaq's rational proof that God must possess speech because He is a king and kingship entails command and prohibition.

Core Idea
God's kingship logically requires command and prohibition, which demand speech.

This lesson introduces a rational argument for divine speech rooted in the attribute of kingship (malik). Unlike scriptural proofs, this argument reasons from the very concept of being a king to the necessity of speech for issuing commands and prohibitions. The passage is dense and incomplete, but the logical structure is sufficient to grasp the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation once to get the overall argument. Notice that the passage is cut off, but the main premises are present.
The argument is incomplete in the source, but the key reasoning is clear.
2
Identify the premises
List the two core premises: (1) God is a king, (2) kingship is only complete with command and prohibition. Then note the implied conclusion: God must be mutakallim (speaker).
The third part about creatures oscillating is cut off but suggests an additional rational ground.
3
Reflect on the logic
Ask yourself why command and prohibition require speech. This is the hidden premise: commands are communicated through speech. Therefore, God must have speech to be a true king.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد احتج الأستاذ أبو إسحاق على أنه تعالى متكلم: بأنه سبحانه ملك، ولا يتم الملك إلا بأمر ونهي، ولجواز تردد الخلائق بين أمر مطاعِ
And Abu Ishaq argued that He, exalted, is mutakallim: because He, glorified, is a King, and kingship is only complete with command and prohibition, and because of the possibility of creatures oscillating between an obeyed command...
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from divine sovereignty (malik). The argument is: God is a king (malik), and the function of kingship requires giving commands and prohibitions. Therefore, God must possess speech (kalam) to issue commands. The argument is cut off at the page break, but the premise is clear. This is a rational proof distinct from the scriptural one.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof anthropomorphizes God by comparing Him to earthly kings.
Correction: The proof does not compare God to creation; rather, it reasons from the necessary implications of the attribute of kingship. It is a rational deduction based on what kingship logically entails, not a likeness to human kings.

Abu Ishaq deduces that God must possess speech from the logical necessity of kingship requiring command and prohibition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
If God's kingship necessitates command and prohibition, then your life is not your own. Are you living as if you are accountable to a King who speaks? Write down one area of your life where you have been acting as if you are self-governing, and commit tonight to submitting it to God's command.

Defending the Kingship Proof: Circularity and Infinite Regress
Sanusi presents an objection against Abu Ishaq's proof and provides a response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defends Abu Ishaq's kingship proof against the objection of circularity and infinite regress by distinguishing between necessary and possible chains.

Core Idea
Not every regress is vicious; only necessary regress undermines a proof.

In the previous lesson, Sanusi presented Abu Ishaq's second proof based on the necessity of command and prohibition among creatures. Here, he anticipates a powerful objection: that this reasoning leads to circularity or infinite regress. The answer clarifies the logic of possibility versus necessity, a key distinction in kalam methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement
Read the Arabic passage first, then the translation. Note how the objector frames the challenge: the need for commands could simply come from humans commanding each other, not from divine speech.
The objection targets the second method (Abu Ishaq's proof).
2
Understand the regress argument
The objector argues that if we trace commands back to other commands, we either end in circularity or an infinite chain, both unacceptable. Identify where 'circularity' and 'infinite regress' appear in the text.
This mirrors classical kalam arguments about causation.
3
Examine Sanusi's answer
Sanusi replies that such regress would only be problematic if it were necessary—i.e., if every person had to be both commander and commanded. But the proof only relies on possibility, not necessity.
Possibility suffices to ground the proof; no actual infinite chain is required.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why distinguishing 'necessary regress' from 'possible regress' matters. This shows how kalam carefully sets the logical conditions for theological arguments.
This guards against a common misunderstanding about regress arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاعتراض على الثانيةِ أنْ يُقالَ : لا مانع أنْ يكونَ هذا الجواز لتردُّدِ الخلائق بينَ أمرٍ مطاع ونهي متَّبع يستند إلى صحة أمرِ بعضنا لبعض . فإن قيل : يلزمُ عليهِ الدورُ أو التسلسل ; لأنَّا ننقل الكلام إلى الأمر منا الذي استند إليه المأمور المطيع له ، فإنَّه يجوز أن يكون ذلك الأمرُ أيضاً مأموراً مطيعاً لغيره ، فإنْ كانَ الغير مأمورَهُ لزم الدورُ ، وإلا لزمَ التسلسل . قلنا : لا يلزمُ ذلك إلا لو كان يجب أنْ يكونَ كلُّ شخص آمراً ومأموراً ، أمَّا مطلقُ الجواز فيكفي في صحتِهِ ما سبق
And the objection against the second (method) is that it may be said: There is no prevention that this possibility of creatures being in a state of needing an obeyed command and a followed prohibition be based on the correctness of some of us commanding others. For if it is said: This would necessitate circularity or infinite regress because we transfer the speech to the command from us on which the commanded obedient person relied, for it is possible that that command is also a commanded person obedient to another. If the other is his commander, circularity follows, otherwise infinite regress follows. We say: That would only be necessary if every person had to be a commander and commanded. As for absolute possibility, the aforementioned is sufficient for its correctness.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the second method (Abu Ishaq's kingship argument). The objection states that the need for command and prohibition among creatures could be based on human commands, not necessarily on divine speech. The objector argues that if we trace commands back, we would get either circularity or infinite regress. The answer rejects this: the regress only applies if every person must be both commander and commanded, but the mere possibility of commands existing does not require an infinite chain. So the proof from kingship remains valid.

Key Terms
دور (circularity)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
جواز (possibility)
أمر (command)
مأمور (commanded)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any regress in a chain of commands invalidates a proof.
Correction: Only a necessary regress (where each step must be actual) is invalid; a regress that is merely possible does not undermine the argument.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection of circularity?
A) By denying that the objection applies
B) By saying that only a necessary chain of commands would lead to regress, and possibility is sufficient
C) By introducing a new proof
D) By accepting the objection

Key Takeaway
The objection of circularity or infinite regress fails because it assumes necessity, whereas the proof only requires possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You obey commands from various sources—teachers, employers, laws. Where does that chain end for you? Does it end in a command you accept as ultimately authoritative, or do you stop at human opinion? If you stopped at human opinion, could that chain ever satisfy your need for certainty? If not, what does this lesson demand you to do about it?
?
The objector thought any regress is destructive. But Sanusi shows only necessary regress is problematic. Are you comfortable living with possibilities that may not be actualized? In matters of belief, do you demand certainty or is possibility enough? What does your answer reveal about your trust in God's speech?
?
This passage distinguishes between possibility and necessity. When it comes to your own need for guidance, do you rely on what is merely possible or do you seek what is necessarily true? Where have you settled for possibility when you needed certainty? What will you change after this lesson?

Proof for Divine Speech from Permissible Commands
Sanusi presents a proof for kalam nafsi from permissibility of commands.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the permissibility of commanding and prohibiting leads to the necessity of an eternal attribute of speech in Allah.

Core Idea
Permissible commands require an eternal basis, which is divine speech.

Sanusi begins establishing the divine attribute of speech (kalam nafsi) by arguing from the observed fact that commanding and prohibiting are permissible. He argues that this permissibility cannot be grounded in other attributes like will or knowledge, so a distinct eternal attribute of speech must exist. This lesson presents that foundational proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
Read the first line: 'Every permissible attribute must be based on an eternal attribute.' This is the key premise.
Notice the logical structure: if not, permissibility would be impossible.
2
Apply to command and prohibition
Sanusi applies the principle to command and prohibition, stating they cannot be reduced to will, knowledge, or other attributes except internal speech.
The phrase 'as we will explain' indicates a later elaboration.
3
Note the conclusion and side remark
The conclusion is that an eternal attribute of speech must be affirmed for Allah. The mention of 'first method' is a brief note on an alternative approach.
The first method (negation of deficiencies) is less reliable according to Sanusi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال : كلُّ صفةٍ جائزة لا بد وأن تستند إلى صفةٍ أزليَّةِ ، وإلا استحالَ ما عُلِمَ جوازُهُ ، ويستحيلُ ردُّ الأمر والنهي إلى الإرادة أو العلم وسائر الصفاتِ غيرِ الكلام النفسي على ما سنبينه عند إثباتِ صفة الكلام في فصلِ إثباتِ صفاتِ المعاني (٢) ، فيجب إثباتُهُ للهِ تعالى . والطريقة الأولى تؤول إلى نفي النقائص ، وقد عرفت ما في الاستناد في نفيها إلى العقل . والاعتراض على الثانيةِ أنْ يُقالَ : لا مانع أنْ يكونَ هذا الجواز لتردُّدِ الخلائق بينَ أمرٍ مطاع ونهي متَّبع يستند إلى صحة أمرِ بعضنا لبعض . فإن قيل : يلزمُ عليهِ الدورُ أو التسلسل ؛ لأنَّا ننقل الكلام إلى الأمر منا الذي استند إليه المأمور المطيع له ؛ فإنَّه يجوز أن يكون ذلك الأمرُ أيضاً مأموراً مطيعاً لغيره ، فإنْ كانَ الغير مأمورَهُ لزم الدورُ ، وإلا لزمَ التسلسل . قلنا : لا يلزمُ ذلك إلا لو كان يجب أنْ يكونَ كلُّ شخص آمراً ومأموراً ، أمَّا مطلقُ الجواز فيكفي في صحتِهِ ما سبق
And he said: Every permissible attribute must be based on an eternal attribute, otherwise what is known to be permissible would be impossible. And it is impossible to refer command and prohibition to will or knowledge and the rest of the attributes other than internal speech (al-kalam al-nafsi), as we will explain when establishing the attribute of speech in the chapter on establishing the attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani). So it is necessary to establish it for Allah Almighty. And the first method (al-tariqa al-ula) leads to the negation of deficiencies, and you have known what is in relying on the intellect for negating them. And the objection to the second [method] is that it may be said: There is no impediment to this permissibility being due to the alternation of creatures between a obeyed command and a followed prohibition, based on the soundness of some of us commanding others. For if it is said: This leads to circularity (al-dawr) or infinite regress (al-tasalsul), because we transfer the discussion to the command from us to which the obedient commanded person referred; for it is permissible that that command also be a commanded person obedient to another. If that other is his commander, circularity follows, otherwise infinite regress follows. We say: That only follows if it were necessary that every person be both commander and commanded; as for absolute permissibility, what preceded suffices for its validity.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the divine attribute of internal speech (kalam nafsi) and addresses an objection. The proof begins with the premise that any attribute whose permissibility is known (like commanding and prohibiting) must be grounded in an eternal attribute; otherwise, its permissibility would be impossible. The author then argues that it is impossible to reduce command and prohibition to attributes like will or knowledge, as will be clarified later. Therefore, one must affirm an attribute of speech for God. The author notes that the first method (negation of deficiencies) has problems when relying on reason alone. He then presents an objection to the second method: the permissibility of command could arise from creatures commanding one another, not requiring an eternal attribute. The objector raises the problem of circularity or infinite regress if we trace back the chain of commands. The author replies that this problem only occurs if every person must be both commander and commanded, but for absolute permissibility, the previous discussion suffices.

Key Terms
صفة أزلية
جواز
الأمر والنهي
الكلام النفسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument presupposes the existence of kalam nafsi before proving it.
Correction: The argument does not presuppose kalam nafsi; it proceeds from the observed permissibility of commands to the necessity of an eternal attribute.

Checkpoint
What is the general principle Sanusi uses in this proof?
A. All attributes must be eternal.
B. Every permissible attribute must be based on an eternal attribute.
C. Command and prohibition are impossible.
D. The intellect is sufficient for negating deficiencies.

Key Takeaway
The proof for divine speech starts from the observed permissibility of commanding and prohibiting, which must be grounded in an eternal attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that every permissible command you give or receive is only possible because it is grounded in Allah's eternal speech, how does that change the weight you give to obeying a single command from the Quran today? What would it take for you to treat that command with the reverence it deserves?
?
Sanusi warns that the first method (negation of deficiencies) is unreliable when based solely on reason. Is there an area of your faith where you have relied on your own reasoning instead of submitting to revelation? What would it cost you to surrender that to Allah?
?
The proof eliminates will and knowledge as the basis for commands—meaning Allah's speech is something distinct and personal. Do you truly believe that the Quran is not just a reflection of Allah's will but His very speech addressing you? If so, what would change in how you read it tonight?

Objection and Answer: Circularity or Infinite Regress
Sanusi presents and refutes an objection to the earlier proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the permissibility of commands could arise from human interactions and Sanusi's reply showing it avoids circularity and infinite regress.

Core Idea
The objection of circularity fails because absolute permissibility does not require every commander to be commanded.

After presenting the proof for divine speech, Sanusi anticipates an objection: perhaps the permissibility of commanding is simply due to humans commanding each other, not requiring an eternal attribute. The objector then raises a problem of circularity or infinite regress. Sanusi provides a concise answer. This lesson examines that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector claims that the permissibility of command can be explained by human interactions without an eternal attribute.
The phrase 'no impediment' signals the objection.
2
Understand the objector's dilemma
The objector argues that tracing the chain of commands leads to either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul).
Dawr is a logical circle; tasalsul is an unending chain.
3
Study Sanusi's reply
Sanusi replies that the problem only arises if every commander must also be commanded. For absolute permissibility, a single instance suffices.
The reply hinges on the distinction between universal necessity and absolute possibility.

Source Text

Proof of Divine Speech from Internal Speech and Its Critique
Argument and objection regarding divine speech from internal experience

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that every person's internal experience of speech proves divine speech, and why Sharaf al-Din objects that universal truths cannot be derived from particular experiences.

Core Idea
Universal truths cannot be derived from individual experiences alone.

Al-Ustadh presents a proof for divine speech based on the universal human experience of internal speech. Sharaf al-Din objects that this reasoning mistakenly derives a universal from particulars. This lesson examines both sides and the role of revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm and the interplay between argument and objection come through.
Notice that the passage has three parts: the proof, the objection, and the author's concluding remark.
2
Understand Al-Ustadh's argument
Identify the claim: every rational person finds internal speech in themselves. How does this serve as a proof for divine speech?
This is an inductive argument from universal human experience.
3
Analyze Sharaf al-Din's objection
See how he argues that this inductive leap from particular experiences to a universal truth about God is invalid. Understand the role of customary induction and why revelation is the proper basis.
His critique highlights a key methodological issue in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج الأستاذ أيضاً على إثباتِ الخبرِ للهِ تعالى : بأنَّ كلَّ عالم يجدُ في نفسه حديثاً مطابقاً لمعلومه بالضرورة ، ولا معنى لكلام النفس إلا ذلك . واعترضَهُ شرفُ الدين بنُ التِّلِمْساني : بأَنَّهُ إثباتُ قضية كليَّةِ عامَّةٍ تشملنا وتشمل البارى جلَّ وعلا مِنْ قضايا جزئية وجدانية قد لا يساعدُ الخصم على تسليمِهِ، وأخذُ القضايا الكليَّةِ مِنَ المحسوسات والوجدانيات لا يتم إلا باستقراء عاداتِ ، وإثبات أحكام الله تعالى وصفاتِهِ لهُ لا تُؤخَذَ مِنَ القضايا العاديَّاتِ ، فالوجه الاعتماد على السمع ، وسننقحُ إن شاء الله تعالى معنى الكلام القديم الموصوف به جل وعلا
And the professor (al-Ustadh) also argued for establishing the report (al-khabar) for Allah Almighty: that every rational person necessarily finds in himself a discourse (hadith) corresponding to his known object, and internal speech (kalam al-nafs) means nothing but that. And Sharaf al-Din ibn al-Tilimsani objected to him: that it is an attempt to establish a universal general proposition (qadiyya kulliyya 'amma) that includes us and includes the Creator, may He be exalted, from particular experiential judgments (qadaya juz'iyya wijdaniyya) which the opponent may not accept. And deriving universal propositions from sense perceptions (al-mahsusat) and experiential matters (al-wijdaniyyat) is only achieved through customary induction (istiqra' 'adat), and establishing the rulings of Allah and His attributes from customary matters (al-qadaya al-'adiyyat) is not valid. Therefore, the correct approach is to rely on revelation (al-sam'), and we will refine, Allah willing, the meaning of eternal speech (al-kalam al-qadim) with which He, may He be exalted, is described.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage presents another proof for divine speech and a critique. Al-Ustadh argues that every rational person necessarily finds within themselves an internal discourse that matches their knowledge, which is the essence of internal speech. Sharaf al-Din al-Tilimsani objects that this attempts to derive a universal general truth (covering both humans and God) from particular experiential judgments. He argues that universal principles cannot be derived from sense perception and experience without customary induction, and that God's attributes should not be based on such customary matters. Therefore, the proper method is to rely on revelation. The author then expresses his intention to clarify the meaning of eternal speech.

Key Terms
خبر
كلام النفس
قضية كلية
قضايا وجدانية
استقراء عادات
السمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection denies the existence of internal speech in humans.
Correction: Sharaf al-Din does not deny internal speech; he objects to using it as a universal proof for God's attribute of speech.

Checkpoint
What is Sharaf al-Din ibn al-Tilimsani's objection to the proof from internal speech?
A) That internal speech is not found in all people
B) That the proof relies on deriving a universal from particular experiences
C) That God's speech is not like human speech
D) That the proof is circular

Key Takeaway
The proof from internal speech is rejected because universal truths for God cannot be derived from particular human experiences without revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life have you assumed that your personal experience defines what is true about God? How does the objection in this passage challenge you to repent from building your theology on your own feelings rather than revelation?
?
When have you considered your internal thoughts or spiritual experiences as direct proof of God's nature? Are you willing to submit your experiences to the Qur'an and Sunnah even when they seem to contradict?
?
The passage says universal truths about God cannot be derived from human experiences. How does this humble your approach to knowing Allah? Will you commit to seeking knowledge of His attributes solely from the revealed sources?

Distinction Between Knowledge and Hearing/Sight
Sanusi distinguishes hearing/sight from knowledge and refutes al-Jubba'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attributes of hearing and seeing are distinct from knowledge, and why al-Jubba'i's reduction of them to mere life is rejected.

Core Idea
Hearing and seeing are not reducible to knowledge or mere life; they are distinct divine attributes.

Sanusi begins a new section to clarify that Allah's attributes of hearing and seeing cannot be collapsed into His knowledge. We naturally experience a difference between knowing something and perceiving it. The Mu'tazili theologian al-Jubba'i attempted to equate them with the attribute of life, but Sanusi shows why this is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Understand the distinction from necessary experience
Sanusi argues that we necessarily find a difference between knowledge of something in its absence and the relationship of hearing/sight to it before. Reflect on your own experience: knowing about a sound is different from hearing it.
This is a self-evident distinction that supports the reality of separate attributes.
3
Examine and refute al-Jubba'i's position
Al-Jubba'i and his followers claim that 'hearing' and 'seeing' simply mean 'alive without defect'. Sanusi refutes this by stating that life is not among the attributes of perception. Consider why life alone cannot entail hearing or seeing.
This is a key refutation of a Mu'tazili view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: كونُهُ عليماً لا يُغني عن كونه سميعاً بصيراً ولا يُستغنى بكونه تعالى عالماً عن كونه سميعاً بصيراً : لما نجده ضرورةً مِنَ الفرق بينَ علمنا بالشيء حالَ غيبتِهِ عنا، وبينَ تعلُّق سمعنا وبصرنا بهِ قبل. اعلم : أنَّ العقلاء قد اختلفوا في معنى هاتين الصفتينِ ؛ فذهب الجبائي وابنهُ ومَنْ تبعَهما إلى أنَّ معنى السميع والبصير شاهداً وغائباً هو الحي الذي لا آفةَ بهِ ، وهذا باطل ؛ فإنَّ الحياة ليست مِنَ الصفات
Chapter: That His being Knowing does not suffice for His being Hearing and Seeing. And His being, may He be exalted, Knowing does not dispense with His being Hearing and Seeing: because we necessarily find a difference between our knowledge of a thing when it is absent from us, and the relation of our hearing and sight to it before. Know: that the rational people have disagreed about the meaning of these two attributes; al-Jubba'i and his son and those who followed them held that the meaning of 'Hearing' and 'Seeing', whether present or absent, is 'the living one who has no defect' (al-hayy alladhi la afata bihi). And this is false; for life is not among the attributes [that are related to perception].
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the distinction between knowledge and the attributes of hearing and sight. The author states that we necessarily experience a difference between knowing something in its absence and perceiving it through hearing or sight. He then notes the disagreement among scholars about the meaning of these two attributes. Al-Jubba'i and his followers claim that 'Hearing' and 'Seeing' simply mean being alive without any defect, whether the object is present or absent. The author rejects this view as false, arguing that life is not among the attributes that constitute perception; rather, hearing and sight are distinct attributes related to sensory perception.

Key Terms
سميع
بصير
حي لا آفة به
صفات
عالم
سمع
بصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hearing and seeing are just knowledge, or they simply mean being alive without defect.
Correction: Knowledge is distinct from perception, and life does not entail hearing or seeing.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jubba'i, what is the meaning of 'hearing' and 'seeing'?
A) Perception of sounds and sights
B) Being alive without defect
C) Knowledge of the object
D) A combination of life and knowledge

Key Takeaway
Knowledge does not replace hearing and seeing; they are distinct attributes, and life alone does not entail perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim that Allah is سميع بصير (All-Hearing, All-Seeing). But do you live as if He truly hears every word you speak and sees every action you take—even when no one else is watching? When you sin in private, do you forget that His hearing and sight are distinct from His knowledge, meaning He perceives your deed directly? How will you remind yourself today that Allah's hearing and sight are ever-present, not merely His knowledge?
?
Sanusi says we necessarily find a difference between knowing something in its absence and hearing/seeing it. Do you experience a difference in your own awareness of Allah? Is there a gap between 'Allah knows' and 'Allah sees me right now'? What would change in your daily life if you truly internalized that Allah's hearing and sight are active and distinct attributes, not just abstract knowledge?
?
Al-Jubba'i reduced hearing and sight to mere life, but Sanusi refuted that. How often do you reduce Allah's attributes to concepts that are comfortable for you? Do you ever treat His hearing as a passive awareness rather than an active, personal engagement with your voice? What step will you take today to speak to Allah (in du'a) with the conviction that He is actually listening—not just knowing—your words?

God's Knowledge Does Not Suffice for Hearing and Seeing
Sanusi argues for the distinction of hearing/seeing from knowledge and refutes al-Jubbai's definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's knowledge does not replace His hearing and seeing, and how the correct definition of these attributes is established by refuting al-Jubbai's view.

Core Idea
Knowledge of something absent is not the same as perceiving it directly.

Sanusi begins by asserting a necessary distinction: knowing a thing when it is absent is not the same as hearing or seeing it. He then turns to a historical disagreement about the meaning of 'hearing' and 'seeing' as divine attributes, refuting a definition that would collapse them into mere life without defect. This passage lays the groundwork for understanding that God's attributes are distinct and each has its own object.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Note the two-part structure: a proof and a refutation.
The passage moves from a general thesis to a specific refutation.
2
Identify the proof
Find the sentence that gives the proof for why knowledge does not suffice. It appeals to our necessary experience of the difference between knowing something absent and perceiving it directly.
The proof is experiential, not purely logical.
3
Understand al-Jubbai's definition
Sanusi introduces the disagreement: al-Jubbai and his followers defined 'hearing' and 'seeing' as 'the living being with no defect'. Identify this definition.
4
Examine the refutation arguments
Sanusi gives four arguments against al-Jubbai's definition. List each argument and see how it undermines the definition.
The arguments involve relational attributes, specificity, self-awareness, and consistency.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters for understanding God's attributes. What would be lost if hearing and seeing were merely life without defect?
This connects to the broader topic of divine attributes being distinct and meaningful.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
1 كونُهُ عليماً لا يُغني عن كونه سميعاً بصيراً ولا يُستغنى بكونه تعالى عالماً عن كونه سميعاً بصيراً : لما نجده ضرورةً مِنَ الفرق بينَ علمنا بالشيء حالَ غَيبَتِهِ عنا ، وبينَ تعلُّق سمعنا وبصرنا بهِ قبل اعلم : أنَّ العقلاء قد اختلفوا في معنى هاتين الصفتينِ ؛ فذهب الجبائي وابنهُ ومَنْ تبعَهما إلى أنَّ معنى السميع والبصير شاهداً وغائباً هو الحي الذي لا آفةَ بهِ ، وهذا باطل ؛ فإنَّ الحياة ليسَتْ مِنَ الصفاتِ المتعلقة ، والسمع والبصرُ مِنَ الصفات المتعلقة ، وسلب الآفة لا اختصاص لهُ بغيرِ مَنْ سُلِبَتْ عنه ، ولأنَّ الإنسانَ يُحِسُّ مِنْ نفسِهِ كونه سميعاً بصيراً ، والعدمُ لا يُحِسُّ ، ولأنه لو صح ذلك لصح أن يُقالَ : العالم والقادرُ هو الحيُّ الذي لا آفةَ بهِ ، ولم يقولوا بهِ
His being knowledgeable does not suffice for His being hearing, seeing. And it is not sufficient that He, Most High, is knowledgeable for His being hearing and seeing: because we find necessarily a difference between our knowledge of a thing when it is absent from us and between the attachment of our hearing and sight to it before. Know that the rational people have differed regarding the meaning of these two attributes: al-Jubbai, his son, and those who followed them held that the meaning of 'hearing' and 'seeing' for both present and absent is the living being with no defect, and this is false; for life is not among the relational attributes, while hearing and sight are among the relational attributes, and negation of defect has no specificity to those whom it is negated, and because a human perceives from himself that he is hearing and seeing, while non-existence does not perceive, and because if that were correct, it would be correct to say: the knowing and powerful is the living being with no defect, but they did not say that.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage contains a proof and a refutation. The author first states the thesis: God's being knowing does not make hearing and seeing redundant. The proof is the necessary experiential difference between knowing a thing when it is absent and hearing/seeing it directly. Then the author introduces a disagreement among rational beings about the meaning of sami' and basir. He refutes al-Jubbai's view that they mean simply 'living without defect.' Arguments: (1) Life is not a relational attribute, while hearing and sight are. (2) Negation of defect is not specific to those negated. (3) If it were true, one could call the knowing and powerful just 'living without defect,' but they do not. Thus the definition is false.

بصير
صفات متعلقة
حي لا آفة به

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge makes hearing and seeing redundant; if He knows everything, He doesn't need to hear or see.
Correction: Knowledge and perception are distinct. Knowing something absent is different from perceiving it directly. Hearing and seeing are relational attributes that add meaning beyond knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument that God's knowledge does not suffice for His hearing and seeing?
A) Knowledge is not an attribute of God.
B) There is a necessary difference between knowing something in its absence and perceiving it directly with the senses.
C) Hearing and seeing are impossible for God.
D) Knowledge and perception are identical in God.

Key Takeaway
God's hearing and seeing are distinct from His knowledge and cannot be reduced to mere life without defect.

Philosophers' Views on Vision and Hearing
Sanusi presents and refutes the philosophers' theories of vision and hearing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' physical explanations for vision and hearing and why they are inadequate for describing divine perception.

Core Idea
The philosophers reduce perception to physical processes, incompatible with God's transcendence.

This passage presents the philosophers' theories of vision and hearing as a background for refutation. Understanding these mechanical explanations helps clarify why they cannot apply to God, who is free from material limitations. The philosophers propose two opinions on vision and a detailed mechanism for hearing, both leading to a common sense in the brain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source once, then the English translation. Let the structure of the philosophers' argument become clear.
The passage reports the philosophers' views, not Sanusi's own position.
2
Identify the two opinions on vision
Notice the two opinions: one says we perceive a mental impression; the other says we perceive the external object itself via an impression in the vitreous humor.
Both opinions involve a physical impression process.
3
Understand the hearing process
Read the description of sound waves, the ear canal, and the nerve. Note that there are also two opinions about how the sound reaches the perceiving power.
The common sense is likened to a basin receiving input from five pipes (the senses).
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Ask yourself: if perception requires physical organs and processes, how can an immaterial God perceive? This is why the philosophers' views are refuted later.
The passage sets up the problem; the refutation follows in subsequent lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهبتِ الفلاسفة إلى أنَّ معنى الرؤية تأثر الحدقة بسبب ارتسام صورة المبصر فيها ، ولهما قولان : أحدهما : أنَّ المدرَكَ لنا نفس المثال المنطبع ، وهو الشيء المطابق لما في الخارج ، الخالي عن المادة والثاني : أنَّ المدرَكَ لنا عين ذلكَ الشيء بواسطة المثال المنطبع في الرطوبة الجليديّةِ المؤدِّيةِ إلى الحس المشترك ؛ الذي هو مُركَّبٌ مِنْ عضلتين مجوّفتين على صورة صليب في مُقدَّم الدماغ قالوا : وأما السمعُ : فإنَّ الصوت وما يتركَّبُ مِنَ الحروفِ والأصواتِ إذا صادمَتِ الهواء الراكد في الصماخ المجاور للعصبة المفروشة في أقصى الصماخ ، الممدودة عليه كالجلدِ على الطبل . . حصل فيها طنين ، فتشعرُ بهِ القُوَّةُ المُدرِكَةُ المُودعةُ في تلكَ العصبة على رأي ، أو تؤدِّيهِ إلى الحس المشترك على رأي ، والحس المشترك على هذا الرأي كحوض تصب فيه خمسة أنابيب ؛ وهي الحواس
And the philosophers held that the meaning of vision is the effect on the pupil due to the impression of the image of the visible object in it, and they have two opinions: One: that what is perceived by us is the impression itself, which is the thing conforming to what is in external existence, stripped of matter. The second: that what is perceived by us is the very thing itself by means of the impression in the vitreous humor leading to the common sense; which is composed of two hollow muscles in the form of a cross in the front of the brain. They said: As for hearing: sound and what is composed of letters and sounds, when they strike the stagnant air in the ear canal adjacent to the nerve spread in the deepest part of the ear canal, stretched over it like skin on a drum... a ringing occurs in it, and the perceiving power deposited in that nerve feels it according to one opinion, or it is conveyed to the common sense according to another opinion, and the common sense according to this opinion is like a basin into which five pipes pour; these are the senses.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' views on vision and hearing as background for refutation. For vision, they give two opinions: one says we perceive the mental impression itself (a form stripped of matter); the other says we perceive the external object itself via an impression in the vitreous humor that reaches the common sense (located in the brain). For hearing, they describe sound waves striking air in the ear canal, causing a ringing felt by a perceiving power in a nerve, or transferred to the common sense. The common sense is likened to a basin receiving input from the five senses. The context implies these physical explanations are inadequate for attributing perception to God, who is not material.

Correction: The passage is presenting the philosophers' theories as a prelude to their refutation. Sanusi does not accept these material explanations, especially for God's attributes.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The philosophers explain vision and hearing through physical processes, but these explanations cannot apply to God, who is immaterial and transcendent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Would your trust in His all-knowledge be shaken? Commit today to reflecting on one attribute of God that is beyond your own experience, and let that awe transform how you speak to Him.
?

If your own soul transcends the material, how much more does God's attribute of hearing transcend any physical ear? Hearing and Sight: Positive Relational Attributes
Sanusi defines hearing and sight as relational attributes and refutes their being mere negation of defect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why hearing and sight are considered positive relational attributes of Allah, not merely the absence of defects.

Core Idea
Hearing and sight are positive perceptions, not just absences of deafness or blindness.

In this passage, Sanusi defines hearing and sight as relational attributes (الصفات المتعلقة) and refutes the view that they are merely the negation of defect. He provides three arguments to establish that they are positive, real attributes. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the category
Read the opening line to see how Sanusi categorizes hearing and sight as relational attributes.
Relational attributes relate to creation, such as hearing and seeing.
2
First argument: not specific
The negation of defect is not specific; it could apply to anyone. But hearing and sight are specific to the one who has them.
This shows they are not mere negation.
3
Second argument: self-awareness
A person perceives himself as actually hearing and seeing. Non-existence does not perceive, so these are positive experiences.
Direct evidence from human experience.
4
Third argument: reductio ad absurdum
If the opponent's definition were correct, then 'knowing and powerful' could be reduced to 'living without defect', but they don't accept that.
Shows inconsistency in their position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتعلقة ، والسمع والبصرُ مِنَ الصفات المتعلقة ، وسلب الآفة لا اختصاص لهُ بغيرِ مَنْ سُلِبَتْ عنه ، ولأنَّ الإنسانَ يُحِسُّ مِنْ نفسِهِ كونه سميعاً بصيراً ، والعدمُ لا يُحِسُّ ، ولأنه لو صح ذلك لصح أن يُقالَ : العالم والقادرُ هو الحيُّ الذي لا آفةَ بهِ ، ولم يقولوا بهِ
The relational [attributes], and hearing and sight are among the relational attributes. And the negation of defect is not specific to the one from whom it is negated. And because a person perceives from himself that he is hearing and seeing, and non-existence does not perceive. And because if that were valid, it would be valid to say: The knowing and powerful [being] is the living one who has no defect, but they did not say that.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage identifies hearing and sight as relational attributes (الصفات المتعلقة) and refutes the claim that they are merely the absence of defect (سلب الآفة). The author first states that the negation of defect is not specific to the one from whom it is negated; it could be applied to anyone lacking a defect, not necessarily to a specific being. Next, he argues from self-awareness: a person directly perceives himself as hearing and seeing, whereas non-existence (العدم) does not perceive, so these are positive perceptions. Finally, he uses a reductio ad absurdum: if the definition were correct, one could say that the knowing and powerful being is merely the living one without defect, but the opponents themselves do not accept that. Thus, hearing and sight must be positive, relational attributes.

Key Terms
السمع
البصر
الصفات المتعلقة
سلب الآفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think hearing and sight are merely the absence of deafness and blindness.
Correction: Sanusi shows they are positive perceptions: a person directly perceives himself as hearing and seeing, which is not mere negation.

Checkpoint

A) That absence of defect is specific to God
B) That a person perceives himself as hearing and seeing, while non-existence does not perceive
C) That hearing and sight are not attributes
D) That the philosophers agree

Key Takeaway
Hearing and sight are positive, relational attributes of Allah, not mere absences of defect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What would change in your behavior today if you internalized that His hearing and sight are positive, real perceptions—not just the absence of deafness?

The author argues that a person directly perceives himself as hearing and seeing. When you make du'a, do you feel that Allah is genuinely listening to your voice, or does it sometimes feel like talking to the air? How can you remind yourself before du'a that Allah's hearing is an active, relational attribute?
?
If the opponents were correct and hearing/sight were merely negations, your accountability before Allah might be different. What does the fact that Allah has real, positive attributes of hearing and sight demand from you in terms of sincerity and awareness in your daily life? Commit to one specific action this week that shows you believe He is truly watching and listening.

Sunni Position on Hearing and Sight
Sanusi states the Sunni school's view and gives a proof from essential attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Sunni school holds that hearing and sight depend only on a locus and not on physical conditions, based on the proof from essential attributes.

Core Idea
Perception requires only a locus; God's custom, not physical causes, explains it.

In this passage, Sanusi presents the Sunni school's view on two of God's attributes: hearing and sight. He explains that they are perceptions that need only a locus to exist in, and that in our experience, which things perceive is a matter of God's habitual custom. He then gives a philosophical proof that the locus's capacity to receive perception is essential to it, so no external condition can be required.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice that Sanusi first states the doctrine, then gives the proof.
The proof is a concise logical argument.
2
Identify the two parts: doctrine and proof
The passage has two clear sections: the Sunni position (doctrine) and the proof (ḥujja). Mark where each begins.
3
Understand the proof's premise
The proof says that the locus's acceptance of perception is essential (nafsī). What does 'essential' mean here? It means intrinsic, not dependent on anything else.
This is key to the argument.
4
Follow the logical consequence
If the capacity is essential, then requiring a condition would make an essential attribute depend on something external, which is impossible (muḥāl). Therefore, no condition is needed.
This refutes the philosophers who require physical organs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومذهب أهل السنَّةِ : أنَّ السمع والبصر إدراكان لا يتوقفانِ إلا على وجود محلّ يقومانِ بهِ ، واختصاص بعض الأشياء بالإدراك في حقنا إنَّما هو بإجراءِ اللهِ عادتَهُ بخَلْقِ ذلكَ فيهِ أو عندَهُ ، وحُجَّتُهم : أَنَّ قَبول المحل للإدراك نفسي له ، فلوِ اسْتُرِطَ فيه شرط لزم توقف الصفة النفسيَّة على شرط ، وهو محال
And the doctrine of the Sunna is that hearing and sight are two perceptions that depend only on the existence of a locus in which they subsist. And the specification of some things with perception in our case is only by God's custom in creating that in them or with them. And their proof: that the locus's acceptance of perception is essential (nafsī) to it. So if a condition were required in it, it would entail the dependence of an essential attribute on a condition, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage presents the Sunni school's (أهل السنة) position on hearing and sight. They define them as two perceptions (إدراكان) that require only a locus (محل) in which they inhere. The fact that certain objects in our experience are capable of perception is due to God's habitual custom (عادة الله) of creating perception in them or in connection with them. Their proof (حجة) is that the capacity of a locus to receive perception is essential (نفسي) to it; it is an intrinsic property. If that capacity required an external condition, then an essential attribute would depend on a condition, which is impossible. This argument refutes the philosophers' view that perception requires specific physical mechanisms. Instead, God directly creates perception in the locus without necessary intermediaries.

Key Terms
أهل السنة
السمع
البصر
إدراكان
محل
الصفة النفسية
عادة الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think perception requires physical causes like eyes or ears as necessary conditions.
Correction: The Sunni proof shows that perception depends only on the locus's essential capacity and God's custom; physical organs are not necessary causes, only customary occasions.

Checkpoint
According to the Sunni proof, why cannot a condition be required for the locus to perceive?
A) Because God's custom is arbitrary
B) Because the capacity for perception is essential to the locus
C) Because perception is a miracle
D) Because the philosophers already proved it

Key Takeaway
Hearing and sight are perceptions that depend only on a locus with an essential capacity; no external condition is needed because that would make an essential attribute contingent, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If perception depends only on God's custom and not on physical conditions, do you truly rely on God for your ability to see and hear, or do you take them for granted as natural? Will you thank Him today with a specific act of worship that engages your senses?
?
The proof shows that essential attributes cannot depend on conditions. Does your faith in God's attributes rest on such sure logical grounding, or is it merely inherited? What would it take for you to know with certainty that God's hearing and sight are not contingent?
?
Your own hearing and sight are gifts from God's custom. Have you ever considered them as the direct creation of God each moment? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed that your perceptions are sustained by God without any necessary physical cause?
