The Imam's Objections to Impression Theory of Vision and Ibn al-Tilimsani's Response
Sanusi records the Imam's two objections against impression theory and Ibn al-Tilimsani's partial response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two objections raised by Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi against the impression theory of vision and the beginning of Ibn al-Tilimsani's response.

Core Idea
Vision by impression cannot explain seeing large objects.

In this passage, Sanusi records a key dialectical exchange on the nature of vision. The Imam raises two objections against the theory that vision occurs by impression of the object's form in the eye. Ibn al-Tilimsani responds to the second objection, offering a nuanced distinction. This text shows how kalam arguments are refined through critique and counter-argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument flow.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the response is incomplete.
2
Identify the two objections
Find the first objection (great in small) and note against whom it is valid. Then locate the second objection (lengths/widths in a point).
The Imam distinguishes between two versions of the impression theory.
3
Analyze Ibn al-Tilimsani's response
He distinguishes between 'impression with quality of greatness' and 'absolute impression'. Understand how this responds to the second objection.
The response continues beyond the available text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد اعترض الإمامُ على مَنْ قالَ : إِنَّ الرؤية بسبب الانطباع : بأنَّا نرى نصف كرةِ العالم ، وانطباعُ العظيم في الصغير محال ، وهذا الإلزام صحيح على مَنْ يقولُ : إِنَّ المُدرَكَ المثالُ المنطبع ، لا مطابقهُ الخارجي ، لا بالنسبةِ لِمَنْ يقولُ : إِنَّ المنطبع واسطة للإدراك وألزمهم الإمامُ أيضاً عدم رؤية الأطوالِ والعُروض ؛ لاستحالة ارتسامِ هذه الأبعادِ في نقطةِ الناظرِ واعترضَهُ ابنُ التِّلِمْساني : بأَنَّهُ إِنْ أرادَ الانطباع بكيفيةِ العِظَم .. فهو مِنْ معنى ما قبلَهُ وإنْ أراد مطلق الانطباع ، لأنَّ الناظر نقطةٌ والنقطة لا امتداد لها ، فكيفَ ينطبع فيها ما له امتداد . . فيُقالُ : إِنَّما يمتنع لو كانَتْ كرة حقيقة بحيثُ لا يقابلُ البسيط منها إلا نقطة ، أمَّا
And the Imam objected against one who said that vision is due to impression: that we see half the sphere of the world, and impression of the great in the small is impossible. And this objection is valid against one who says that what is perceived is the impressed form, not its external counterpart, but not with respect to one who says the impressed is a medium for perception. And the Imam also argued against them: the non-seeing of lengths and widths, due to the impossibility of the impression of these dimensions in the point of the viewer. And Ibn al-Tilimsani objected to him: that if he meant impression with the quality of greatness, then it is from the meaning of what preceded; and if he meant absolute impression, because the viewer is a point and a point has no extension, so how can what has extension be impressed in it? So it is said: It is impossible only if it were a real sphere such that only a point of its surface faces the simple [substance]; but as for...
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage presents two objections by the Imam (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) against the impression theory of vision, and a response by Ibn al-Tilimsani. The first objection: if vision occurs by impression, then seeing half the celestial sphere would require the impression of something huge in the tiny eye, which is impossible. The Imam notes this refutes those who say the perceived object is the impressed form itself (not the external object), but it does not refute those who say the impressed form is merely a medium. The second objection: we cannot see lengths and widths because their dimensions cannot be impressed in the point of the viewer's eye. Ibn al-Tilimsani responds by distinguishing: if 'impression' means with the quality of greatness, that is already covered by the first objection; if it means absolute impression, the viewer's eye is a point with no extension, so an extended thing cannot be impressed in it. The response continues that the impossibility holds only if the seen object were a real sphere such that only a point of its surface faces the simple substance (atom); but as for... (the passage cuts off). This shows the dialectical refinement of arguments in kalam.

Key Terms
الإمام
الرؤية
الانطباع
المثال المنطبع
نصف كرة العالم
ابن التلمساني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impression theory of vision is easily refuted by the size argument.
Correction: The Imam's first objection only refutes those who say the impressed form is the perceived object itself; it does not refute those who consider the impressed form a medium. The second objection is more nuanced and receives a response from Ibn al-Tilimsani.

Checkpoint
Against which view does the Imam's first objection (great impression in small) hold validity?
A) Against those who say the impressed form is a medium
B) Against those who say the perceived object is the impressed form itself
C) Against all philosophers
D) Against the Sunni school

Key Takeaway
The Imam's objections against impression theory are nuanced and target specific variants, illustrating the precision of kalam dialectics.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Imam’s objections target specific versions of a theory. Do you often reject ideas based on a simplified understanding, without considering nuanced distinctions? Think of a belief you hold strongly—can you articulate the precise version you accept and the versions you reject? Write down a clear distinction in one area of your faith where you have been imprecise.
?
Ibn al-Tilimsani’s response shows that an objection can be met by clarifying what exactly is meant by a term. When your understanding of Islam is challenged, do you rush to defend without first asking 'What do you mean by that?' Reflect on a recent discussion where clarifying definitions could have changed the outcome. Commit to asking for clarification before responding in future.

Ash'ari Doctrine on Hearing and Sight
Sanusi states the Ash'ari doctrine on hearing and sight and their proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of hearing and sight as perceptions that require only a locus, and the rational proof that their condition is intrinsic, not external.

Core Idea
Hearing and sight depend only on the soul's intrinsic capacity, not on physical organs.

Sanusi now turns to the divine attributes of hearing and sight. He presents the Ash'ari position clearly, distinguishing it from other views. The passage defines these attributes and provides a logical proof for their nature, emphasizing that they are perceptions intrinsic to the soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own phrasing introduce the doctrine.
The Arabic uses technical terms like 'idrāk' and 'maḥall'.
2
Identify the two parts of the passage
Notice that Sanusi first states the doctrine (definition and customary specification), then provides a proof introduced by 'wa-ḥujjatuhum'.
The proof is a concise logical argument.
3
Reflect on the proof's logic
Understand why requiring a condition for an intrinsic attribute is impossible. This solidifies the Ash'ari position that hearing and sight are not dependent on external conditions.
This is a key theological argument against reducing perception to physical organs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومذهب أهل السنَّةِ : أنَّ السمع والبصر إدراكان لا يتوقفانِ إلا على وجودِ محلّ يقومانِ بهِ، واختصاص بعض الأشياء بالإدراك في حقنا إنَّما هو بإجراءِ اللهِ عادتَهُ بخَلْقِ ذلكَ فيهِ أو عندَهُ، وحُجَّتُهم : أَنَّ قَبول المحل للإدراك نفسي له، فلوِ اسْتُرِطَ فيه شرط لزم توقف الصفة النفسيَّة على شرط، وهو محال
And the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunnah is that hearing and sight are two perceptions that do not depend except on the existence of a locus (maḥall) in which they subsist, and the specification of some things with perception in our case is only by God's running His custom by creating that in it or near it. And their proof is that the acceptance of the locus for perception is intrinsic (nafsī) to it, so if a condition were required for it, it would necessitate the dependence of an intrinsic attribute on a condition, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage presents the Ashʿari definition of hearing and sight (samʿ and baṣar) as two perceptions (idrākān) that require a locus (maḥall) in which they subsist, but do not depend on any other condition beyond that locus. The specification of certain things as objects of perception in our experience is due to God's habitual action (ʿāda) in creating perception in that locus or in relation to it. The proof (ḥujja) for this is that the locus's capacity to accept perception is intrinsic (nafsī) to it; if it required a further condition, then an intrinsic attribute would depend on an external condition, which is absurd. Thus, hearing and sight are not reducible to other faculties like knowledge.

Key Terms
السمع
البصر
إدراك
محل
الصفة النفسية
عادة الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the condition for perception is a physical organ (e.g., eye or ear) or external stimuli (light, sound waves).
Correction: The passage clarifies that perception requires only a locus (the soul or substance) and that physical organs are merely customary loci, not necessary conditions.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Sunnah, what is the condition for the existence of hearing and sight?
A) A healthy sense organ
B) A locus in which they subsist
C) External stimuli like light or sound waves
D) God's direct creation without any locus

Key Takeaway
Hearing and sight are perceptions intrinsic to the soul, requiring only a locus, and any additional condition would be logically impossible.

?
When you witness something unjust, do you consider that your perception of it is created by God in that moment? How should that affect your response and your trust in God's wisdom?

Fakhr al-Din's Objection to Imprinting in Vision
Fakhr al-Din's two objections against imprinting theory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's two objections to the theory of vision through imprinting and the scope of their validity.

Core Idea
Fakhr al-Din argues that imprinting the large in the small is impossible, especially for the celestial sphere.

Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a powerful critique of the imprinting theory of vision, which held that we see objects because their images are physically imprinted in the eye. His two objections target the impossibility of fitting large objects into a small eye and the impossibility of depicting extended dimensions in a point. This passage lays out those objections and notes their limited applicability.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage containing Fakhr al-Din's objections once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice how he structures his critique.
The passage includes a qualification about who the objection applies to.
2
Identify the two objections
The passage presents two distinct objections: one about the size of the celestial sphere, and one about lengths and widths in a point. Find both.
3
Understand the qualification
Fakhr al-Din notes that his objection is valid only against those who hold that the imprinted image itself is perceived, not those who view it as a medium. Reflect on why this distinction matters.
This shows the objection is not universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد اعترض الإمامُ على مَنْ قالَ : إِنَّ الرؤية بسبب الانطباع : بأنَّا نرى نصف كرةِ العالم، وانطباعُ العظيم في الصغير محال (۱)، وهذا الإلزام صحيح على مَنْ يقولُ : إِنَّ المُدرَكَ المثالُ المنطبع، لا مطابقهُ الخارجي، لا بالنسبةِ لِمَنْ يقولُ : إِنَّ المنطبع واسطة للإدراك
وألزمهم الإمامُ أيضاً عدم رؤية الأطوالِ والعُروض ؛ لاستحالة ارتسامِ هذه الأبعادِ في نقطةِ الناظرِ )
واعترضَهُ ابنُ التِّلِمْساني : بأَنَّهُ إِنْ أرادَ الانطباع بكيفيةِ العِظَم .. فهو مِنْ معنى ما قبلَهُ وإنْ أراد مطلق الانطباع، لأنَّ الناظر نقطةٌ والنقطة لا امتداد لها، فكيفَ ينطبع فيها ما له امتداد . . فيُقالُ : إِنَّما يمتنع لو كانَتْ كرة حقيقة بحيثُ لا يقابلُ البسيط منها إلا نقطة، أمَّا إذا كانَ فيها انبطاح مع استدارتها كالبيضة مثلاً .. فلا مانع من انطباع المثالِ الصغيرِ المطابق للكبير بحسَب العادة
And the Imam (Fakhr al-Din) objected against those who said that vision is due to imprinting (inṭibāʿ): that we see half of the celestial sphere, and the imprinting of the large in the small is impossible. And this objection is valid against those who say that the perceived thing is the imprinted image (mithāl munṭabiʿ), not its external correspondent, but not against those who say that the imprinted thing is a medium for perception. And the Imam also made obligatory against them the non-seeing of lengths and widths, due to the impossibility of the depiction of these dimensions in the point of the viewer. And Ibn al-Tilimsani objected against him: that if he meant imprinting with the quality of magnitude, then it is from the meaning of what preceded it; and if he meant absolute imprinting, because the viewer is a point and a point has no extension, so how can that which has extension be imprinted in it? So it is said: It is only impossible if it were a true sphere such that only a point of its surface contacts the simple (atom); but if it has flatness (inbiṭāḥ) along with its sphericity like an egg for example, then there is no obstacle to the imprinting of a small image corresponding to the large according to custom.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage presents Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's objection to the theory that vision occurs via physical imprinting (inṭibāʿ) of the object's image in the eye. His first objection is that we see half of the celestial sphere, but imprinting something large in a small space (the eye) is impossible. He adds that this objection is valid only against those who claim the perceived thing is the imprinted image itself, not against those who say the imprinted image is a medium (wāsiṭa) for perception. A second objection is that we see lengths and widths, but these dimensions cannot be depicted in the point (nāẓir) that is the eye. Ibn al-Tilimsani responds by distinguishing two meanings: if the Imam meant imprinting with the quality of magnitude (kaifiyat al-ʿiẓam), then this objection is redundant with the first; if he meant absolute imprinting, then because the viewer is a point with no extension, it is impossible to imprint extended things. However, the reply continues: this impossibility only holds if the object is a perfect sphere where only a point of its surface contacts the simple (atom, jawhar fard); but if the object has some flatness (inbiṭāḥ) like an egg, then a small image corresponding to the large can be imprinted by custom. Thus Ibn al-Tilimsani defends the imprinting theory against Fakhr al-Din's objection.

Key Terms
الانطباع
المثال المنطبع
نقطة الناظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fakhr al-Din's objection refutes all versions of imprinting theory.
Correction: Fakhr al-Din himself notes that his objection only applies to a specific version where the imprinted image is the direct object of perception.

Checkpoint
According to Fakhr al-Din, against whom is his objection valid?
A) Those who say the imprinted image is a medium for perception
B) Those who say the perceived thing is the imprinted image itself
C) Those who deny vision occurs through imprinting
D) Those who believe vision is due to rays emitted from the eye

Key Takeaway
Fakhr al-Din's objections challenge the physical possibility of imprinting large objects and extended dimensions in a point, but only refute the version where the image itself is perceived.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you insist that God must fit into your mental models (like the imprinting theory), are you not committing the same error Fakhr al-Din points out—trying to contain the infinite in the finite? Reflect on a belief you hold about God that might be limited by your own capacity to conceive. Are you willing to let go of that mental image?
?
Fakhr al-Din shows that physical analogy fails for vision. How often do you assume your physical senses can grasp spiritual realities? Is there an area of faith where you demand physical proof? What does that say about your trust in revelation?
?
Consider that the failure of imprinting theory doesn't negate the reality of vision. Similarly, when your understanding of a theological truth is challenged, do you abandon the truth itself or just your mental model? Which have you done in the past?

Ibn al-Tilimsani's Defense of Imprinting
Ibn al-Tilimsani's reply to Fakhr al-Din's objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn al-Tilimsani's response to Fakhr al-Din's objection, distinguishing between two meanings of imprinting and introducing the concept of flatness (inbiṭāḥ).

Core Idea
Ibn al-Tilimsani shows that Fakhr al-Din's objection only applies to perfect spheres, not to objects with flatness.

Ibn al-Tilimsani offers a nuanced reply to Fakhr al-Din's critique. He distinguishes between imprinting with magnitude and absolute imprinting, and argues that the impossibility of imprinting only holds for perfect spheres where only a point contacts the viewer. If the object has some flatness, like an egg, a small image can correspond to a large object by custom.

Guided Path
1
Read the response passage
Read the Arabic passage containing Ibn al-Tilimsani's reply. Notice the conditional structure and the distinction he makes.
The passage includes a hypothetical objection and a clarification.
2
Understand the two interpretations of imprinting
Ibn al-Tilimsani says if Fakhr al-Din meant imprinting with the quality of magnitude, the objection is redundant. If he meant absolute imprinting, then because the eye is a point, extended things cannot be imprinted. This sets up the main problem.
This is a dialectical step: Ibn al-Tilimsani considers both meanings.
3
Grasp the condition about flatness
Ibn al-Tilimsani then argues that impossibility only arises if the object is a perfect sphere contacting at a point. If it has flatness (inbiṭāḥ), like an egg, then a small image can be imprinted corresponding to the large object by custom.
This is the key defense: the objection does not rule out all imprinting.

Fakhr al-Din's Objections to Imprinting in Hearing
Fakhr al-Din raises two objections against imprinting theory in hearing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's two key objections to the theory of imprinting in hearing and why they are not considered conclusive.

Core Idea
Imprinting theory faces objections, but they require further examination.

Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi was a master of philosophical theology. He raised two objections against the theory that hearing occurs through physical imprinting of sound in the ear. However, as Sanusi notes, these objections are not decisive—they require further consideration and investigation. Let's examine each one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Let the objections sink in.
Notice the author's phrases 'fīhi naẓar' and 'fīhi baḥth'.
2
Identify the two objections
Find the two objections: the problem with direction of sound, and the problem with hearing behind a wall.
3
Understand the author's qualification
Note that the author does not accept the objections as final; he says there is consideration and investigation needed.
This is a sign of careful theological methodology.
4
Reflect on the broader context
Consider why these objections matter in the context of divine attributes and the nature of sound.
The passage concludes by tying this to the philosophers' view on hearing and sight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This is what pertains to hearing and sight according to the philosophers' statement.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage presents two objections by Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi against the theory that hearing occurs via physical imprinting of sound in the ear. The first objection: if hearing were due to imprinting, we would not be able to perceive the direction of sound. But the author notes 'there is consideration in this' (fīhi naẓar), meaning this objection is not decisive. The second objection: we would not hear sounds from behind a wall, since the imprint would be blocked. Again, 'there is investigation in this' (fīhi baḥth), indicating the point is arguable. The passage concludes by stating that these points are what pertain to hearing and sight according to the philosophers' opinion (i.e., the theory of imprinting).

The Mu'tazili Reduction of Hearing and Sight and Fakhr al-Din's Proof
Mu'tazili reduction, Fakhr's proof, and an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili claim that hearing and sight are reducible to knowledge, Fakhr al-Din's intuitive argument for their distinction, and the objection that mere distinction does not prove they are different types.

Core Idea
An intuitive distinction between knowing and perceiving proves they are distinct powers.

This passage presents a key debate in divine attributes: are hearing and sight really distinct from God's knowledge? The Mu'tazilites reduce them to knowledge, while Fakhr al-Din al-Razi offers an intuitive proof that they are separate. We will also examine an objection from Sharaf al-Din ibn al-Tilimsani, which questions whether a mere felt distinction suffices to establish a difference in essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Try to identify the three main parts: the Mu'tazili view, Fakhr's proof, and the objection.
The objection is incomplete here; the answer is not provided in this window.
2
Understand the Mu'tazili position
Notice how Abu al-Qasim al-Ka'bi and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri reduce hearing and sight to knowledge of visible and audible things, similar to how 'shahid' and 'khabir' are just specific forms of knowledge.
3
Analyze Fakhr al-Din's proof
Fakhr al-Din argues that when we know something and then perceive it, we find an intuitive (badihi) distinction between the two states. This indicates that seeing and hearing are other than knowledge. Consider why this distinction is considered a proof.
The author notes that the order (knowledge before or after perception) does not affect the argument.
4
Examine the objection
Ibn al-Tilimsani objects that a mere distinction does not prove a specific (naw'i) distinction; the difference could be accidental. Think about what this objection means for the validity of Fakhr's proof.
The objection is presented without its answer in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب أبو القاسم الكعبي وأبو الحسين البصري إلى ردّهما إلى العلم بالمبصراتِ والمسموعاتِ؛ كالشهيد والخبير، فإنَّهما يرجعان إلى تعلقِ العلم على وجه خاص
وقد احتج الفخرُ على ردّ هذه المقالة(٣) : بأنَّا إذا علمنا شيئاً ثم أبصرناه أو سمعناه.. وجدنا بين الحالتين تفرقةً بديهيةً، وذلك ممَّا يدلُّ أنَّ الإبصار والسماع مغاير للعلم، وإلى هذهِ الحُجَّةِ أشرتُ في أصل العقيدة بقولي : ( لما نجده ضرورةً . . . ) إلى آخرِهِ، إلا أنّي فرضتُ تأخُرَ العلم بالشيء عن تعلُّقِ السمع والبصرِ بهِ، والإمامُ فرضَ العكس، ولا فرق في الحُجَّةِ بينَ الوجهين .
واعترضَ شرفُ الدين بنُ التَّلِمْساني هذهِ الحُجَّةَ : بأَنَّ مُجرَّدَ التفرقة لا ينتج أن تكون التفرقة بينهما تفرقة نوعيَّةً، ولا أنَّهما نوعان
And Abu al-Qasim al-Kaʿbi and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri went to reduce them (hearing and sight) to knowledge (ʿilm) of visible and audible things; like the terms 'martyr' (shahīd) and 'expert' (khabīr), for they revert to the attachment of knowledge in a specific manner. And al-Fakhr argued for refuting this statement: that if we know something then we see it or hear it, we find between the two states an intuitive distinction, and that indicates that seeing and hearing are other than knowledge. And I alluded to this proof in the original Creed with my saying: 'Because we find necessarily…' etc., except that I supposed the knowledge of the thing to be posterior to the attachment of hearing and sight to it, while the Imam supposed the opposite, and there is no difference in the argument between the two aspects. And Sharaf al-Din ibn al-Tilimsani objected to this proof: that mere distinction does not result in the distinction between them being a specific distinction, nor that they are two types.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage presents the Muʿtazili position of Abu al-Qasim al-Kaʿbi and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, who reduce hearing and sight to knowledge (ʿilm) of visible and audible things, similar to how terms like 'shahīd' (witness) and 'khabīr' (expert) are just specific forms of knowledge. Then Fakhr al-Din al-Razi argues against this by pointing to an intuitive (badīhī) distinction: when we know something and then see or hear it, we clearly perceive a difference between the two states, proving that seeing and hearing are distinct from knowledge. The author (Sanusi or his commentator) notes that he himself used a similar argument in his original creed but with the order reversed (knowledge after perception), while Fakhr al-Din supposed knowledge before perception, but the argument works either way. Finally, Sharaf al-Din ibn al-Tilimsani objects that mere distinction does not prove they are different in kind (nawʿīyya); the distinction could be accidental. The objection is incomplete in this window, as the answer is not provided.

A) Hearing and sight are created by God
B) There is an intuitive distinction between knowing and perceiving
C) Knowledge always precedes perception

Fakhr al-Din's intuitive distinction argument is a powerful refutation of the Mu'tazili reduction, though it faces an objection about the nature of the distinction.

?

Critique of the Philosophers' Theory of Hearing and Sight
Sanusi presents the philosophers' theory of sensory imprinting and its critique on hearing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational objections raised by kalam scholars against the philosophers' theory of physical imprinting in sensory perception, particularly concerning hearing.

Core Idea
Physical imprinting cannot explain how we hear direction or through barriers.

The philosophers argued that sensory perception occurs through physical imprinting of forms on the sense organs. Here, Sanusi presents key objections raised by Imam Fakhr al-Razi that show the inadequacy of this theory for hearing. Let's examine the passage to see why the imprinting model fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with a conditional statement about vision.
2
Identify the condition for vision
The passage states that if the eye has a flattened spherical shape like an egg, then imprinting of a small image corresponding to the large one is possible. This is the philosophers' explanation for sight.
This condition is accepted by Sanusi as plausible.
3
Understand the two objections against hearing
The Imam raises two arguments: (1) if hearing were by imprinting, we would not know the direction of sound; (2) we would not hear sounds behind a wall. Both are contradicted by experience.
These objections demonstrate that hearing cannot be explained by physical imprinting.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
By showing that sensory perception involves non-physical qualities, the kalam tradition defends the possibility of Allah's attributes being non-material and distinct from creation.
This critique supports the Ash'ari view that attributes are not reducible to physical processes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا كانَ فيها انبطاح مع استدارتها كالبيضة مثلاً .. فلا مانع من انطباع المثالِ الصغيرِ المطابق للكبير بحسَب العادة (١) وألزم الإمامُ أيضاً على القول بالانطباع في السمع : ألا تُعرَفَ جهةً الصوتِ ، وفيه نظرُ ، وألا تُسمَعَ الحروفُ وراء الجدارِ ، وفيه أيضاً بحث (٢) هذا ما يتعلق بالسمع والبصر على قول الفلاسفة .
If it (the eye) has flattening with its roundness like an egg for example, then there is no obstacle to the imprinting of a small image corresponding to the large one according to habit (1). And the Imam also imposed on the claim of imprinting in hearing: that the direction of sound is not known, and there is consideration in it; and that letters are not heard behind a wall, and in it also is research (2). This is what pertains to hearing and sight according to the philosophers.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage presents a critique of the philosophers' theory of sensory perception based on physical imprinting. The author first states the condition: if the eye has a flattened spherical shape like an egg, then it is possible for a small image to be imprinted corresponding to the large one according to habit. This is a condition for vision. Then he mentions two objections raised by the Imam (Fakhr al-Razi) against extending the same theory to hearing. First, if hearing were based on imprinting of sound in the ear, then the direction of the sound would not be known, which is false. Second, letters (sounds) would not be heard through a barrier like a wall. Both objections show the inadequacy of the imprinting theory for hearing. The passage concludes that this is what applies to hearing and sight according to the philosophers' view.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' theory of imprinting implies that sensory perception is merely a physical impression.
Correction: Kalam argues that sensory perception is a distinct non-physical quality, as shown by the inability of physical imprinting to explain hearing direction and barrier penetration.

Checkpoint

D) The sound would be inverted

Key Takeaway
Imprinting cannot account for direction and barrier penetration in hearing, showing that hearing is a non-physical quality.

Write down one specific change you will make to trust in the non-physical reality of Allah's attributes as taught by the Ahl al-Sunnah.

The Basri View on Senses and Fakhr's Refutation
Sanusi presents the Basri view, Fakhr's proof, and Ibn al-Tilmisani's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Basri position that senses reduce to knowledge, Fakhr al-Razi's proof against it, and Ibn al-Tilmisani's objection that a mere distinction does not prove a generic difference.

Core Idea
Distinguishing sensory perception from knowledge does not automatically make them different types.

This passage presents a key debate in Islamic theology about the nature of sensory attributes like hearing and sight. The Basri school reduces them to knowledge of objects, while Fakhr al-Razi argues they are distinct. However, Ibn al-Tilmisani's objection challenges whether a simple distinction suffices to prove a difference in kind. Let's examine the arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Basri view
Read the part about al-Ka'bi and al-Basri reducing hearing and sight to knowledge of visible and audible things. Note how they use examples like 'martyr' and 'expert' to describe a particular kind of knowledge.
The Basri view collapses sensory attributes into knowledge.
2
Examine Fakhr's proof
Fakhr al-Razi argues that there is an obvious distinction between knowing something and then seeing or hearing it. This distinction, he claims, shows that seeing and hearing are different from knowledge.
The proof relies on a direct experiential distinction.
3
Analyze Ibn al-Tilmisani's objection
Ibn al-Tilmisani counters that a mere distinction does not prove a generic difference. The distinction could be due to other factors, such as the multiplicity of objects perceived by sight or the locus of knowledge (heart vs. eye).
This objection undermines the force of Fakhr's argument.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this debate illustrates the importance of precise reasoning in theology. Not every distinction implies a different essence; alternative explanations must be ruled out.
This is a common pitfall in theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب أبو القاسم الكعبي وأبو الحسين البصري إلى ردّهما إلى العلم بالمبصراتِ والمسموعاتِ ؛ كالشهيد والخبير ، فإنَّهما يرجعان إلى تعلقِ العلم على وجه خاص وقد احتج الفخرُ على ردّ هذه المقالة (٣) : بأنَّا إذا علمنا شيئاً ثم أبصرناه أو سمعناه.. وجدنا بين الحالتين تفرقةً بديهيةً ، وذلك ممَّا يدلُّ أنَّ الإبصار والسماع مغاير للعلم ، وإلى هذهِ الحُجَّةِ أشرتُ في أصل العقيدة بقولي : ( لما نجده ضرورةً . . . ) إلى آخرِهِ ، إلا أنّي فرضتُ تأخُرَ العلم بالشيء عن تعلُّقِ السمع والبصرِ بهِ ، والإمامُ فرضَ العكس ، ولا فرق في الحُجَّةِ بينَ الوجهين . واعترضَ شرفُ الدين بنُ التَّلِمْساني هذهِ الحُجَّةَ : بأَنَّ مُجرَّدَ التفرقة لا ينتج أن تكون التفرقة بينهما تفرقة نوعيَّةً ، ولا أنَّهما نوعان خارجان عن نوع العلم وهو محلُّ النزاع ، ولا مانع مِنْ رجوع التفرقة إلى كثرة المتعلقاتِ وقلَّتِها ؛ فإنَّ البصر يتعلَّق بالهيئات الاجتماعية ، ولا يتعلَّقُ العلمُ بذلكَ في حالِ الغَيبةِ ، ولذلك يُقالُ : ليس الخبرُ كالعِيانِ ، أو يُقالُ : ما المانعُ مِنْ رجوع التفرقة إلى اختلافِ محلّ العلمين ، فعند الرؤيةِ يكون العلم حاصلاً بالقلب والعين ، وعند الغَيبة يبقى في القلبِ بخلقِ أَمثالِهِ ، ويُعدَمُ مِنَ العينِ ؟
And Abu al-Qasim al-Ka'bi and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri went to reducing them (hearing and sight) to knowledge of visible things and audible things; like the martyr and the expert, for they both go back to the attachment of knowledge in a particular manner. And al-Fakhr argued against this assertion (3): that if we know something then we see it or hear it, we find between the two states an obvious distinction, and that indicates that seeing and hearing are distinct from knowledge. And to this argument I referred in the original creed with my saying: 'when we find necessarily...' to its end, except that I assumed the priority of knowledge of the thing over the attachment of hearing and sight to it, and the Imam assumed the opposite, and there is no difference in the argument between the two aspects. And Sharaf al-Din ibn al-Tilmisani objected to this argument: that mere distinction does not entail that the distinction between them is a generic distinction, nor that they are two types outside the type of knowledge, which is the point of dispute; and there is no preventing the distinction from being attributed to the multiplicity and paucity of objects; for sight attaches to social configurations, and knowledge does not attach to that in the state of absence, therefore it is said: 'news is not like eyewitnessing'; or it may be said: what prevents the distinction from being attributed to the difference of the locus of the two knowledges, so that at the time of vision knowledge occurs in the heart and the eye, and at the time of absence it remains in the heart by the creation of its likenesses, and is removed from the eye?
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage presents the view of the Basri school (al-Ka'bi and al-Basri) that hearing and sight reduce to knowledge of visible and audible objects, like the terms 'shahid' (martyr) and 'khabir' (expert) which refer to a particular kind of knowledge. Then Fakhr al-Razi argues against this by pointing out that there is an evident distinction between knowing something and then seeing or hearing it; this distinction shows that seeing and hearing are other than knowledge. The author notes that he presented a similar argument in his earlier work, with a slight variation in order. Then Ibn al-Tilmisani objects: a mere distinction does not prove that the two are generically different (different types) from knowledge. The distinction could be due to other factors, such as the number of objects (sight perceives social configurations which knowledge may not perceive in absence), or the locus of knowledge (during vision knowledge is in both heart and eye, during absence only in heart). These alternative explanations show that the distinction does not necessarily imply a difference in essence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any obvious distinction between two phenomena automatically proves they are different in essence (type).
Correction: The passage shows that a distinction may be due to other factors such as the multiplicity of objects or the locus of knowledge, not necessarily a difference in type.

Checkpoint

B) The distinction may be due to other factors like objects or locus
C) Knowledge and perception are identical
D) Fakhr's argument is based on false premises

Key Takeaway
The Basri view reduces senses to knowledge, but Fakhr's distinction argument is countered by Ibn al-Tilmisani's point that a distinction does not necessarily imply a difference in type.

?
Ibn al-Tilmisani warns that a mere distinction doesn't prove a difference in type. In your own life, where might you be mistaking a circumstantial difference for a fundamental one, especially in matters of faith or worship? Will you examine that assumption today?

Al-Ash'ari's Two Opinions on Hearing and Sight
Sanusi presents al-Ash'ari's two opinions on hearing and sight and Ibn al-Tilmisani's criticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's two positions on whether hearing and sight are distinct from knowledge, and the objection raised by Ibn al-Tilmisani.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari offers two views: hearing/sight are either generically different from knowledge or of the same genus, but both are additional attributes.

Al-Ash'ari, the founder of the Ash'ari school, did not hold a single fixed position on the nature of divine hearing and sight. His two opinions illustrate the theological struggle to affirm these attributes while distinguishing them from God's knowledge. This lesson presents those opinions and a subsequent critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the footnotes (2) and (3) as they clarify the second opinion.
2
Identify the two opinions
The passage presents two opinions from al-Ash'ari. The first says hearing and sight are two perceptions different in genus from knowledge. The second says they are from the genus of knowledge but have a narrower scope. Note the common ground: both are additional attributes.
3
Reflect on Ibn al-Tilmisani's criticism
Ibn al-Tilmisani points out that the earlier objection applies to al-Ash'ari's view, and that the Mu'tazila reduce hearing and sight to knowledge entirely. Consider how this critique challenges the Ash'ari position.
This connects to broader debates about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري قولان : أحدهما : أنَّهما إدراكان يخالفانِ العلم بجنسهما ، مع مشاركتهما للعلم في أنَّهما صفتان كاشفتان يتعلقان بالشيء على ما هو عليه (٢) . والقولُ الثاني (٢) : أنَّهما مِنْ جنس العلم (٣) ، إلا أنَّهما لا يتعلَّقانِ إلا بالموجودِ المعلومِ ، والعلم يتعلق بالموجودِ والمعدوم والمطلق والمقيد. وكلاهما معَ ذلكَ صفتانِ زائدتان على علمه تعالى ، واحتج على ذلك بما احتجَّ بِهِ الفخرُ . قالَ ابنُ التَّلِمْساني : ( وما ذكرناهُ مِنَ الإشكال وارد عليهِ ، ومَنْ قالَ مِنَ المعتزلة : إِنَّهُ سميعٌ بصير لنفسِهِ.. فهو يردُّهُما إلى العلم .
And for Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari there are two opinions: One of them: that they (hearing and sight) are two perceptions that differ from knowledge in their genus, while sharing with knowledge that they are two revealing attributes that attach to a thing as it is (2). And the second opinion (2): that they are from the genus of knowledge (3), except that they attach only to the existent known, and knowledge attaches to the existent, the non-existent, the absolute, and the restricted. And both, despite that, are two additional attributes to His knowledge, exalted is He; and he argued for that with what al-Fakhr argued with. Ibn al-Tilmisani said: 'And what we mentioned of the problem applies to it; and whoever of the Mu'tazila says that He is hearing, seeing by Himself, then he reduces them to knowledge.'
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage presents al-Ash'ari's two opinions on whether hearing and sight are distinct from knowledge. The first opinion: they are two perceptions (idrakan) that differ from knowledge in their genus (jins), but share with knowledge that they are 'kashifat' (revealing) attributes that disclose the thing as it is. The second opinion: they are from the same genus as knowledge (here interpreted as 'kashf' or disclosure), but they are more restricted in scope: hearing and sight only attach to existent known things, while knowledge can attach to existent, non-existent, absolute, and restricted. Despite this, both are additional attributes to God's knowledge. al-Ash'ari argued for this using Fakhr al-Razi's argument (mentioned in the previous lesson). Finally, Ibn al-Tilmisani notes that the objection he raised earlier (about the distinction not proving generic difference) also applies to al-Ash'ari's view, and that the Mu'tazila who say God is hearing and seeing by His essence also reduce them to knowledge.

Key Terms
قولان
إدراكان
جنس
كاشفتان
صفات زائدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ash'ari's two opinions contradict each other.
Correction: Both opinions agree that hearing and sight are additional attributes to knowledge; they differ only on whether they are of the same generic type as knowledge or not.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari's second opinion, what is the difference between hearing/sight and knowledge?
A) Hearing and sight attach only to existent known things
B) Hearing and sight attach to everything
C) Knowledge attaches only to existent things
D) Hearing and sight are not attributes

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's two opinions both affirm hearing and sight as additional attributes to God's knowledge, differing only on their generic relationship to knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that al-Ash'ari offers two opinions on whether hearing and sight are from the same genus as knowledge or different, do you feel a tension between trusting your intellect to analyze God's attributes and simply accepting what revelation says? Where does your personal reliance truly rest when the rational arguments become difficult—on the scholars’ reasoning or on the plain text? What will you do the next time you encounter a theological puzzle that feels unsettled?

If God's hearing and sight are truly additional attributes beyond His knowledge, does that make you feel more personally seen and heard, or does it raise doubts about how 'one' God can have multiple distinct faculties? Examine your heart: Do you secretly lean towards a simplified view where all attributes are the same, because it feels more comfortable? What would it mean for your prayer if God's hearing were genuinely distinct from His knowledge?
?
Ibn al-Tilmisani points out a problem with distinguishing attributes by their scope alone. When you face intellectual objections to your faith, do you tend to ignore them, rationalize them away, or does the difficulty drive you to seek deeper understanding? Are you willing to sit with the unsettledness of not having a perfect logical system for God's attributes, trusting that He is beyond full comprehension? What concrete step will you take this week to strengthen your awe of God’s attributes without reducing them to your own mental categories?

The Difference Between Knowledge and Perception
Sanusi presents an objection and two possible answers regarding the distinction between knowledge and perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defends the unity of divine attributes by showing that the difference between knowledge and sensory perception is not essential but attributive.

Core Idea
The difference between knowledge and perception is circumstance, not essence.

Sanusi addresses an objection: if hearing and sight are distinct from knowledge, how can they be divine attributes? He responds by showing the difference is merely in relations or loci, not in genus. This passage is crucial for understanding the unity of God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Pay attention to the two proposed explanations (relations and loci).
2
Identify the objection
The passage begins with the claim that hearing and sight are 'outside the genus of knowledge'. This is the objection being addressed.
The phrase 'وهو محلُّ النزاع' indicates this is the point of dispute.
3
Analyze the first answer: relations
Sanusi argues that the difference can be attributed to the multiplicity or paucity of the objects of these attributes. For example, sight relates to sensible forms, which knowledge does not perceive in absence.
The proverb 'Report is not like direct sight' illustrates this.
4
Analyze the second answer: loci
Alternatively, the difference may stem from the different loci (heart vs. eye) where knowledge resides during vision vs. absence.
During vision, knowledge is in both heart and eye; in absence, only in heart.
5
Reflect on the implications
Both explanations show that the difference is not in the essence of the attributes, preserving the unity of God's knowledge, sight, and hearing.
This is key for justifying divine attributes as identical with God's essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خارجان عن نوع العلم وهو محلُّ النزاع ، ولا مانع مِنْ رجوع التفرقة إلى كثرة المتعلقاتِ وقلَّتِها ؛ فإنَّ البصر يتعلَّق بالهيئات الاجتماعية ، ولا يتعلَّقُ العلمُ بذلكَ في حالِ الغَيبةِ ، ولذلك يُقالُ : ليس الخبرُ كالعِيانِ ، أو يُقالُ : ما المانعُ مِنْ رجوع التفرقة إلى اختلافِ محلّ العلمين ، فعند الرؤيةِ يكون العلم حاصلاً بالقلب والعين ، وعند الغَيبة يبقى في القلبِ بخلقِ أَمثالِهِ ، ويُعدَمُ مِنَ العينِ ؟ (۱) انظر ( شرح معالم أصول الدين ) ( ص ٣١٦ ) .
They are outside the genus of knowledge, and that is the point of dispute. There is no impediment to attributing the difference to the multiplicity or paucity of relations; for sight relates to sensible forms, while knowledge does not relate to that in the state of absence. Therefore it is said: 'Report is not like direct sight.' Or it is said: 'What prevents attributing the difference to the difference of the locus of the two knowledges? For at the time of vision, knowledge occurs in both heart and eye, and in the state of absence it remains in the heart by the creation of its likenesses, and is absent from the eye?' (1) See (Sharh Ma'alim Usul al-Din) (p. 316).
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection is that hearing and sight are not of the same genus as knowledge because they differ from it. The author states that there is no impediment to attributing the difference to the multiplicity or paucity of the objects of these attributes. For example, sight relates to sensible forms (al-hay'at al-ijtimaiyya) which knowledge does not perceive when the object is absent. Hence the saying 'Report is not like direct sight.' Alternatively, one may attribute the difference to the different loci (mahall) of knowledge: during vision, knowledge is in both heart and eye, while in absence it remains only in the heart through the creation of its likenesses, and disappears from the eye. So the difference is not in essence but in circumstance. The footnote references a commentary.

البصر
السمع
التفرقة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think hearing and sight are fundamentally different from knowledge in essence (i.e., they belong to a different genus).
Correction: Sanusi shows the difference is merely in the number of relations (objects) or in the locus where knowledge resides, not in the essence of the attributes.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the objection in this passage?
A) The difference between knowledge and perception is in genus.
B) The difference can be explained by varying relations or loci.
C) Sight is not a form of knowledge at all.
D) Knowledge is superior to perception.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi defends the unity of divine attributes by attributing the difference between knowledge and perception to relations or loci, not to essence.

Al-Ash'ari's Two Opinions on Hearing and Sight
Sanusi records two opinions of al-Ash'ari on hearing and sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opinions of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari regarding the nature of hearing and sight and their relationship to divine knowledge.

Core Idea
Hearing and sight are either a different genus or the same genus as knowledge, but always additional attributes.

In this passage, Sanusi records two positions attributed to the founder of the Ash'ari school, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, on the divine attributes of hearing and sight. The debate centers on whether these attributes are distinct in kind from knowledge or share its nature. This lesson explores both opinions and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation to grasp the two opinions. Pay attention to the footnotes that clarify technical terms and the more famous opinion.
The footnotes are part of the source text.
2
Understand the first opinion
The first opinion holds that hearing and sight are two perceptions differing from knowledge in genus, though they share the property of being revealing attributes.
Genus (jins) refers to the category of attribute.
3
Understand the second opinion
The second opinion holds that hearing and sight are of the same genus as knowledge (manifestation), but they relate only to existing known objects, whereas knowledge relates also to non-existent and restricted. They are still additional attributes to knowledge.
This opinion is the more famous one.
4
Reflect on the Mu'tazila position
Ibn al-Tilimsani notes that the Mu'tazila who say God is Hearing, Seeing by Himself reduce these attributes to knowledge, which contrasts with Ash'ari's view of additional attributes.
This highlights the sectarian context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري قولان : أحدهما : أنَّهما إدراكان يخالفانِ العلم بجنسهما ، مع مشاركتهما للعلم في أنَّهما صفتان كاشفتان يتعلقان بالشيء على ما هو عليهِ (۳) والقولُ الثاني (۲) : أنَّهما مِنْ جنس العلم (٣) ، إلا أنَّهما لا يتعلَّقانِ إلا بالموجودِ المعلومِ ، والعلم يتعلق بالموجودِ والمعدوم والمطلق والمقيد وكلاهما معَ ذلكَ صفتانِ زائدتان على علمه تعالى ، واحتج على ذلك بما احتجَّ بِهِ الفخرُ . قالَ ابنُ التَّلِمْساني : ( وما ذكرناهُ مِنَ الإشكال وارد عليهِ ، ومَنْ قالَ مِنَ المعتزلة : إِنَّهُ سميعٌ بصير لنفسِهِ.. فهو يردُّهُما إلى العلم ، (۱) انظر ( شرح معالم أصول الدين ) ( ص ٣١٦ ) . (۲) هذا القول هو المشهور عنده ، وهو مذهب الإسفرايني ومختار ابن الحاجب عكاري » ( ق ١٤٠ ) . (۳) جنس العلم هنا هو الكشف ، فكلُّ من العلم والسمع والبصر صفات كاشفة
And for the Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari there are two opinions: One of them: that they are two perceptions differing from knowledge in their genus, while sharing with knowledge that they are two revealing attributes that relate to a thing as it is. (3) And the second opinion (2): that they are of the genus of knowledge (3), except that they relate only to the existing known, while knowledge relates to the existing, the non-existent, the absolute, and the restricted. And both of them, despite that, are two additional attributes to His knowledge, may He be exalted. And he argued for that with what al-Fakhr argued. Ibn al-Tilimsani said: 'What we mentioned of the difficulty applies to it, and whoever among the Mu'tazila says that He is Hearing, Seeing by Himself, he reduces them to knowledge.' (1) See (Sharh Ma'alim Usul al-Din) (p. 316). (2) This opinion is the well-known one from him, and it is the doctrine of al-Isfarayini and the choice of Ibn al-Hajib al-'Ukbari. (3) The genus of knowledge here is manifestation (kashf), so all of knowledge, hearing, and sight are revealing attributes.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage records two opinions of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari on the nature of hearing (sam') and sight (basar). The first opinion holds that hearing and sight are two perceptions (idrakan) that differ from knowledge in genus (jins), though they share with knowledge the property of being revealing attributes (sifat kashifat) that disclose things as they are. The second opinion, which is the more famous one according to the footnote, holds that hearing and sight are of the same genus as knowledge, i.e., manifestation (kashf), but they are restricted to relating only to existing known objects (al-mawjud al-ma'lum), whereas knowledge relates to both existing and non-existing, absolute and restricted. Despite being of the same genus, they are still additional attributes to God's knowledge. Al-Ash'ari argued for this using the same proof as al-Fakhr al-Razi. Ibn al-Tilimsani remarks that the difficulty (al-ishkal) mentioned earlier applies to this argument, and that those Mu'tazila who say God is Hearing, Seeing by Himself (li-nafsihi) reduce these attributes to knowledge. The footnotes provide references and clarifications.

زيادة الصفات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that al-Ash'ari considered hearing and sight to be identical to knowledge, not additional attributes.
Correction: Even in the second opinion where they share the same genus, they are still additional attributes (za'idatan) to knowledge.

Checkpoint

B) They relate only to existing known things.
C) They relate only to nonexistent things.
D) They are not additional attributes.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari held two opinions on hearing and sight: they either differ from knowledge in genus or share its genus while being additional attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Can you accept your own limitations in understanding while still trusting the scholars? What one step can you take to deepen your reliance on qualified scholarship without demanding absolute uniformity?
?

What does this lesson teach you about the distinctness and additional nature of God's attributes, and how will that affect your du'a and worship this week?

The Attribute of Perception: Affirmation, Negation, or Suspension?
Sanusi investigates the attribute of idrak (perception) for God and presents three opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly opinions regarding whether God is described as a perceiver (mudrik) and why the correct position is suspension (waqf) according to Sanusi.

Core Idea
When revelation is silent, we suspend judgment — not affirm or deny.

Sanusi now turns to a controversial attribute: perception (idrak). Some theologians affirm it, others deny it because they think it implies connection with bodies. But Sanusi argues that the correct method is to rely on revelation, and since revelation does not mention idrak, the most appropriate stance is suspension. In this lesson, we will explore the three opinions and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand Sanusi's argument. Notice how he starts by stating the correct position is suspension.
Pay attention to the phrase 'الوقف' (suspension).
2
Understand the methodological principle
Sanusi says the method for negating deficiencies is reliance on revelation (sam'). This is a key principle in kalam.
Revelation mentions hearing, sight, and speech, but not idrak.
3
Identify the three opinions
The passage lists three opinions: affirmation, negation (because it implies connection with bodies), and suspension. Note the reasoning for each.
The author favors suspension as 'closest to correctness'.
4
Analyze the argument for suspension
Suspension is preferred because revelation does not mention idrak, and the truth is that idrak does not necessitate connection with bodies.
This avoids both unjustified affirmation and unfounded denial.
5
Reflect on the final clarification
The demonstrative 'this' refers to the proof that hearing and sight are perfections additional to knowledge, and the same applies to idrak, so it would be necessary if there were evidence.
But because no textual evidence exists, we suspend.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحقيق كونه تعالى مُدركاً ] وبهذا يثبتُ كونُهُ مُدْرِكاً عندَ مَنْ أثبته ، والتحقيق فيه : الوقفُ ؛ لما تقدَّمَ أنَّ التحقيق في نفي النقائص الاعتماد على السمع ، وقد ورد في السمع والبصر والكلام ، ولم يرد في الإدراك ، وجزم بعضُهم بنفيهِ لمَّا رآه ملزوماً للاتصال بالأجسام؛ يعني : ويدخل في العلم والحقُّ: أنَّه لا يستلزمه. وبالجملة : فمجموع ما فيه ثلاثة أقوال ، وأقربها الوقف كما قدمناه. الإشارة بـ ( هذا ( راجعة إلى دليل كونه تعالى سميعاً بصيراً ؛ وهو كونهما كمالين في حق الحي زائدين على العلم ؛ للتفرقة الضروريةِ بينَ العلم وبينهما ، وهذا المعنى ثابت للإدراك ، فيجب (۱) سيأتي ( ص ٤٨٢ ) .
Investigation of His being, exalted, a Perceiver (mudrik). And by this it is established that He is a Perceiver according to those who affirm it. And the correct position in it is suspension (waqf); because it has preceded that the correct method in negating deficiencies is reliance on revelation (sam'), and revelation has come regarding hearing, sight, and speech, but has not come regarding perception (idrak). And some of them positively denied it because they saw it as necessitating connection with bodies; meaning that it enters into knowledge. And the truth is that it does not necessitate that. In summary, the total concerning it is three opinions, and the closest is suspension as we have presented. The reference by 'this' goes back to the proof that He is Hearing, Seeing; which is that they are perfections for a living being additional to knowledge, due to the necessary distinction between knowledge and them. And this meaning is established for perception, so it is necessary. (1) It will come (p. 482).
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is an investigation (tahqeeq) of the attribute of idrak (perception) for God. It begins by stating that those who affirm idrak do so based on a proof, but the correct position is suspension (waqf) because the method for negating deficiencies in God is reliance on revelation (sam'). Revelation mentions attributes like hearing, sight, and speech, but does not mention idrak. Some scholars positively deny idrak because they think it requires connection with bodies (ittisal bi al-ajsam), but the truth is that it does not necessitate that. There are three opinions overall: affirmation, negation, and suspension. The author holds that suspension is closest to correctness. The final sentence clarifies that the demonstrative 'this' in the proof refers to the argument that hearing and sight are perfections additional to knowledge, and that same meaning applies to idrak, so it is necessary (i.e., logically required). The footnote promises more on page 482.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Idrak must be denied because it is not mentioned in revelation.
Correction: The correct position is suspension (waqf), not denial, because idrak is not necessarily a deficiency and revelation does not address it.

Checkpoint
What is the strongest opinion regarding whether God is described as mudrik (perceiver)?
A) Affirmation, because it is a perfection.
B) Negation, because it implies connection with bodies.
C) Suspension (waqf), because revelation does not mention it.
D) It is the same as knowledge.

Key Takeaway
When revelation does not address an attribute, the proper stance is suspension (waqf), not affirmation or denial, unless there is rational necessity.

Proof That Perception Is Additional to Knowledge
Sanusi provides the proof that perception is additional to knowledge and explains its nature separate from the five senses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that perception is a perfection additional to knowledge and why the sensory acts (smell, taste, touch) are not identical to perception.

Core Idea
Perception is not the same as the sensory act; the act can occur without perception.

Sanusi now turns to a key attribute of God: perception (idrāk). The Ashʿarī tradition distinguishes perception from knowledge and from the five physical senses. In this passage, he provides the rational proof that perception is an additional perfection, using the same logic applied to hearing and seeing. He then clarifies that sensory acts like smelling are merely customary causes, not the perception itself, and offers a simple linguistic proof to drive the point home.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the first few sentences to see that Sanusi is proving perception is additional to knowledge, just as hearing and seeing were proven additional. Notice the phrase 'necessary distinction'.
This builds on the earlier proof for hearing and seeing.
2
Follow the perfection-deficiency argument
The proof runs: perception is a perfection; if a living being lacks it, it has its opposite (deficiency); deficiency is impossible for God; therefore God must have perception in a way befitting Him.
The argument assumes that every living being is receptive to perception.
3
Understand the distinction from sensory acts
Sanusi explains that words like 'smell' and 'taste' cannot be applied to God because they imply physical contact and change. The real perception is something beyond these acts.
This avoids anthropomorphism.
4
Grasp the linguistic proof
The key evidence: we can say 'I smelled the apple but did not find any smell,' showing that the act (smelling) can occur without the perception (finding the smell). Thus perception is additional.
The argument is cut off in the text but the conclusion is clear: if perception were the same as the act, such a statement would be contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإشارة بـ ( هذا ( راجعة إلى دليل كونه تعالى سميعاً بصيراً ؛ وهو كونهما كمالين في حق الحي زائدين على العلم ؛ للتفرقة الضروريةِ بينَ العلم وبينهما ، وهذا المعنى ثابت للإدراك ، فيجب ثبوتهُ عندَ مَنْ سلك هذا الطريق العقلي ، وقد قدَّمنا ما في ذلك (١) ويعنون بالإدراك : إدراك الملموسات والمشموماتِ والمذوقاتِ فقولي : ( وبهذا يثبتُ كونُهُ مُدْرِكاً عندَ مَنْ أثبتهُ ( معناه : أنَّ دليل الإدراكِ عندَ القائلينَ بهِ أنْ قالوا : إنَّ الإدراكات المتعلقة بهذه الأشياء زائدة على العلم بها ؛ للتفرقة الضرورية بينهما كما سبق قبلُ في زيادةِ إدراك السمع والبصر على العلم ، وإذا كانت زائدة على العلم.. لا يُستغنى به عنها ، وهي كمالات ، وكلُّ حيّ فهو قابل لها ، فإذا لم يتّصف بها اتّصف بأضدادها ، وأضدادها نقص ؛ لأنَّ فيها فوتَ كمال ، والنقص في حقّهِ جلَّ وعلا محال ، فوجبَ أَنْ يَتَّصفَ بتلك الإدراكات زائدةً على علمه جلَّ وعلا ، لكن على ما يليقُ بهِ تعالى مِنْ نفي الاتصال بالأجسام ، ونفي اللذَّاتِ عن ذاتِهِ العليَّةِ والآلام . ولهذا أجمعوا : أنَّ لفظ الشم والذوقِ واللمس لا يصح إطلاقه حقِّهِ تعالى ؛ لما يؤذنُ بهِ مِنَ الاتصالات وتجدُّدِ الكيفياتِ ، وكلُّ ذلك في حقٌّ مَنْ تنزَّةَ عن الحدوثِ في ذاتِهِ وصفاتِهِ . . محال ، وإِنَّما الإدراك المتنازعُ في إثباتِهِ في حقه تعالى أمر وراء الشم والذوق واللمس وليست هذه الثلاثةُ نفس الإدراكاتِ ولا لازماً عقليّاً لها ، وإنَّما هي في حقنا أسباب عاديةٌ، يخلق الله جل وعلا معها الإدراك غالباً . ويدلُّ على أنَّ الإدراكَ أمرٌ زائد عليها : أَنَّكَ تقولُ : شَمِمْتُ التفاحة فلم أجد لها ريحاً ، وكذا : لمستُ وذقتُ فلم أجد ، ولو كانَ
The reference by 'this' goes back to the proof of His being all-hearing and all-seeing, which is that they are perfections in the living being additional to knowledge, due to the necessary distinction between knowledge and them. And this meaning is established for perception, so its affirmation is necessary for whoever follows this rational path, and we have presented what is in that. And they intend by 'perception': perception of tangible things, smells, and tastes. So my saying: 'And by this His being perceiver is established for those who affirm it' means that the proof of perception according to those who affirm it is that they said: the perceptions related to these things are additional to the knowledge of them, due to the necessary distinction between them as preceded earlier concerning the addition of perception of hearing and sight over knowledge. And since they are additional to knowledge, it is not sufficient without them, and they are perfections, and every living being is receptive of them, and if it is not characterized by them, it becomes characterized by their opposites, and their opposites are deficiency, because in them is the loss of perfection, and deficiency with respect to Him, exalted, is impossible. Therefore it is necessary that He be characterized by those perceptions additional to His knowledge, exalted, but in a manner befitting Him, exalted, by negating connection to bodies, and negating pleasures and pains from His lofty essence. And for this reason they all agreed that the word 'smell', 'taste', and 'touch' cannot be applied to Him, exalted, because they imply connections and renewal of qualities, and all of that with respect to the One who is transcendent above origination in His essence and attributes is impossible. Rather, the perception disputed about its affirmation concerning Him, exalted, is something beyond smell, taste, and touch, and these three are not themselves perceptions nor a necessary concomitant of them, but they are in our case customary causes, with which God, exalted, generally creates perception. And evidence that perception is additional to them is that you say: 'I smelled the apple and did not find any smell for it,' and similarly: 'I touched and tasted and did not find,' and if it were
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage provides the rational proof that perception (idrāk) is a perfection additional to knowledge and explains its true nature. The author first clarifies that the argument for God being hearing and seeing relies on them being perfections additional to knowledge, due to a necessary distinction between knowledge and these attributes. The same reasoning applies to perception. Then he states that by 'perception' they mean perception of tangible things, smells, and tastes. The proof for it goes as follows: perceptions of these things are additional to knowledge of them; if they were not additional, the knowledge would suffice, but it does not. They are perfections; any living being that lacks them possesses their opposites, which are deficiencies. Since deficiency is impossible for God, He must possess these perceptions in a manner befitting Him, free from bodily connection and from pleasures and pains. For this reason, the terms 'smell', 'taste', and 'touch' cannot be applied to God because they imply physical connections and changing qualities. Rather, the perception in question is something beyond these sensory acts; they are merely customary causes in us, not identical to perception nor necessary for it. A proof that perception is additional: one may say 'I smelled the apple but did not find any smell,' or 'I touched and tasted but did not find,' showing that the sensory act can occur without the corresponding perception. The argument is cut off here, but the intended conclusion is that if perception were the same as the act, such statements would be contradictory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that sensory acts like smelling are themselves the perception, or that if an act occurs, perception necessarily occurs.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the act can occur without the perception (e.g., 'I smelled but did not find a smell'), so they are distinct.

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence given in this passage that perception is additional to the sensory acts of smell, taste, and touch?
A) Because God cannot be described with such acts.
B) Because one can say 'I smelled but did not find a smell,' showing the act can occur without the perception.
C) Because the Quran separates them.
D) Because the Mu'tazilites denied it.

Key Takeaway
Perception is a perfection additional to knowledge, distinct from the five sensory acts, and is proven by the linguistic observation that the sensory act can occur without the corresponding perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You say you know God is All-Perceiving, but do you live as if He truly perceives your innermost thoughts and intentions? If perception is not limited to the senses, how does that change the reality of His awareness of you? Pause and ask yourself: Am I truly aware that every hidden deed is perceived by One who sees without eyes and hears without ears? Commit today to acting with that awareness.
?
The passage shows that you can smell something yet not perceive its odor. Where in your worship are you 'going through the motions'—performing the act but failing to perceive the reality of the One you are standing before? What steps can you take tonight to transform your rituals into genuine perception?
?
If sensory acts are merely causes, not the essence of perception, then your physical senses can deceive you. Your heart may be numb to the signs around you. When you read the Quran or observe creation, do you truly perceive the hand of Allah, or are you just going through the act of looking? Ask yourself: What is one sign of Allah that you have been overlooking, and how will you train your heart to truly perceive it starting tomorrow?

Allah's Perception Beyond Knowledge
Sanusi proves Allah's perception of smells, tastes, and tangibles as additional attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that Allah's perception of smells, tastes, and tangible things is an attribute additional to His knowledge, and why sensory terms are not applied literally to Him.

Core Idea
Allah's perception is a perfection beyond knowledge, free from bodily limitations.

This passage from Sanusi addresses a subtle point: how can we affirm that Allah perceives smells, tastes, and touches without attributing bodily senses to Him? The key is that perception (idrak) is a perfection additional to knowledge, and its opposite is deficiency, which cannot be in Allah. But the sensory acts we experience are only customary causes, not the perception itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the flow: it starts with the claim, then gives the proof, then clarifies the manner and rejects sensory terms.
The author uses a rational argument based on perfection and deficiency.
2
Understand the proof
The proof shows that perception is additional to knowledge due to a necessary distinction. Since it is a perfection and its opposite is deficiency, Allah must possess it.
This parallels the earlier proof for hearing and sight.
3
Note the qualification
Sanusi emphasizes that this perception is in a manner befitting Allah, negating physical contact, pleasure, and pain.
This prevents anthropomorphism.
4
Examine the rejection of sensory terms
Terms like 'smell', 'taste', and 'touch' are not used for Allah because they imply contact and change, which are impossible for the Eternal.
The perception is something beyond these acts.
5
Learn the linguistic evidence
Sanusi gives an example: 'I smelled the apple and did not find any scent' shows that perception is not identical to the act of smelling.
This is a clear proof that perception is additional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثبوتهُ عندَ مَنْ سلك هذا الطريق العقلي ، وقد قدَّمنا ما في ذلك ويعنون بالإدراك : إدراك الملموسات والمشموماتِ والمذوقاتِ فقولي : ( وبهذا يثبتُ كونُهُ مُدْرِكاً عندَ مَنْ أثبتهُ ) معناه : أنَّ دليل الإدراكِ عندَ القائلينَ بهِ أنْ قالوا : إنَّ الإدراكات المتعلقة بهذه الأشياء زائدة على العلم بها ؛ للتفرقة الضرورية بينهما كما سبق قبلُ في زيادةِ إدراك السمع والبصر على العلم ، وإذا كانت زائدة على العلم لا يُستغنى به عنها ، وهي كمالات ، وكلُّ حيّ فهو قابل لها ، فإذا لم يتّصف بها اتّصف بأضدادها ، وأضدادها نقص ؛ لأنَّ فيها فوتَ كمال ، والنقص في حقّهِ جلَّ وعلا محال ، فوجبَ أَنْ يَتَّصفَ بتلك الإدراكات زائدةً على علمه جلَّ وعلا ، لكن على ما يليقُ بهِ تعالى مِنْ نفي الاتصال بالأجسام ، ونفي اللذَّاتِ عن ذاتِهِ العليَّةِ والآلام . ولهذا أجمعوا : أنَّ لفظ الشم والذوقِ واللمس لا يصح إطلاقه حقِّهِ تعالى ؛ لما يؤذنُ بهِ مِنَ الاتصالات وتجدُّدِ الكيفياتِ ، وكلُّ ذلك في حقٌّ مَنْ تنزَّةَ عن الحدوثِ في ذاتِهِ وصفاتِهِ محال ، وإِنَّما الإدراك المتنازعُ في إثباتِهِ في حقه تعالى أمر وراء الشم والذوق واللمس وليست هذه الثلاثةُ نفس الإدراكاتِ ولا لازماً عقليّاً لها ، وإنَّما هي في حقنا أسباب عاديةٌ، يخلق الله جل وعلا معها الإدراك غالباً . ويدلُّ على أنَّ الإدراكَ أمرٌ زائد عليها : أَنَّكَ تقولُ : شَمِمْتُ التفاحة فلم أجد لها ريحاً ، وكذا : لمستُ وذقتُ فلم أجد ، ولو كانَ الإدراك غيرَ زائد عليها لكان هذا اللفظ متناقضاً
Its affirmation for those who follow this rational path, and we have already presented what is in that. And they mean by perception: perception of tangible things, smells, and tastes. So my saying: 'And by this, His being perceptive is affirmed for those who affirm it' means that the proof for perception according to those who affirm it is that they said: The perceptions related to these things are additional to knowledge due to the necessary distinction between them, as preceded before in the addition of perception of hearing and sight to knowledge. And if they are additional to knowledge, it is not sufficed with it [knowledge] instead of them, and they are perfections. And every living being is capable of them, so if it is not characterized by them, it is characterized by their opposites, and their opposites are deficiency because they involve loss of perfection, and deficiency with respect to Him, exalted, is impossible. So it is necessary that He be characterized by those perceptions in addition to His knowledge, but in a manner befitting Him, exalted, by negating contact with bodies and negating pleasures and pains from His exalted essence. And for this reason they unanimously held that the terms smell, taste, and touch are not validly applied to Him, exalted, because of the contacts and renewal of qualities that they imply, and all that is impossible with respect to the One who is transcendent of temporal origination in His essence and attributes. Rather, the perception under dispute regarding its affirmation for Him, exalted, is something beyond smell, taste, and touch, and these three are not themselves the perceptions nor a rational concomitant of them. Rather, in our case they are customary causes with which Allah, exalted, usually creates perception. And it indicates that perception is additional to them: that you say: 'I smelled the apple and did not find any scent,' and similarly: 'I touched and tasted and did not find [anything],' and if perception were not additional to them, this utterance would be contradictory.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the affirmation of Allah's perception (idrak) of smells, tastes, and tangible things as an attribute additional to His knowledge. The author begins by stating that this perception is affirmed by those who follow the rational method. He clarifies that the perception in question is of the three types: tangible items, smells, and tastes. Then he explains the reasoning of the affirmers: these perceptions are additional to knowledge because there is a necessary distinction between them, as previously established for hearing and sight. Since they are perfections, and every living being is capable of them, if Allah were not characterized by them, He would be characterized by their opposites, which are deficiencies. Deficiency in Allah is impossible, so He must possess these perceptions additionally to His knowledge, but in a manner befitting Him—free from physical contact and pleasure/pain. Therefore, the scholars agree that the terms 'smell,' 'taste,' and 'touch' are not to be used for Allah because they imply contact and change, which are impossible for the Eternal. The perception at issue is something else beyond those sensory acts; they are not identical nor rationally necessary concomitants. In our human context, sensory acts are merely customary causes through which Allah usually creates perception. The author then provides a linguistic proof: one can say 'I smelled the apple and did not find any scent,' proving that the perception of scent is not the smelling act itself. If perception were identical to the act, such a statement would be contradictory. Therefore, perception is additional to the sense organs and their activities.

Correction: The author explicitly denies this: perception is something beyond sensory acts, and terms like smell, taste, and touch are not applied literally to Allah.

Allah's perception of smells, tastes, and tangible things is an attribute additional to knowledge, affirmed as a perfection, but sensory terms are not applied literally because they imply physicality.
