Definition of Attributes of Acts
Sanusi defines attributes of acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of attributes of acts as the emanation of effects from Allah's power and will.

Core Idea
Attributes of acts are Allah's causing creation to happen.

Sanusi now defines a key category of divine attributes: those related to actions. This passage gives a precise definition, showing that attributes of acts are not about Allah's essence but about what He does through His power and will.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the key terms: صفات الأفعال, قدرة, إرادة.
The definition is concise; every word matters.
2
Identify the core idea
What does 'صدور الآثار' mean? It means the emanation of effects—i.e., creation.
3
Connect to power and will
Understand that attributes of acts are not separate from power and will; they are the effects of these two attributes.
This corrects a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما صفاتُ الأفعالِ : فهي عبارةٌ عن صدور الآثارِ عن قدرته وإرادته جل وعلا وأمَّا الصفاتُ الجامعة لجميع الأقسام : فهي عبارة عن كل صفةٍ تدل على معنى يندرج فيه سائر الأقسام الستة .
And as for the attributes of acts (صفات الأفعال): they are expressions for the emanation of effects from His power and will, majestic and exalted. And as for the comprehensive attributes for all the categories (الصفات الجامعة): they are expressions for every attribute that indicates a meaning that includes the remaining six categories.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage defines two more categories. First, attributes of acts (صفات الأفعال) are defined as the emanation of effects from Allah's power and will. Second, comprehensive attributes (الصفات الجامعة) are defined as attributes that indicate a meaning encompassing all the other six categories (referring to the earlier divisions of attributes). This shows a systematic classification of divine attributes.

Correction: No, they are precisely the effects emanating from power and will.

Sanusi defines comprehensive attributes.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Comprehensive attributes are umbrella categories covering all attribute types.

Having defined the individual categories, Sanusi now presents a broader classification: comprehensive attributes (الصفات الجامعة). These are attributes that encompass all the previous six categories, such as life, knowledge, power, etc.

This is a meta-category.
3

Examples of Meaningful Attributes and Attributes of Meanings
Sanusi enumerates examples for two attribute categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concrete examples Sanusi gives for the categories of meaningful attributes and attributes of meanings, and how these examples illustrate the distinction between the two categories.

Core Idea
Examples make the categories concrete: meaningful attributes are descriptions, meanings are the concepts themselves.

Sanusi now provides specific examples to clarify the earlier theoretical division of attributes. By seeing what 'meaningful attributes' and 'attributes of meanings' look like in practice, the student can better grasp the conceptual difference. This passage lists the classic examples for each category.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to see the examples listed for meaningful attributes and attributes of meanings.
Notice the ellipsis indicating that the list continues.
2
Distinguish the categories
Observe that meaningful attributes are verbal descriptions (e.g., 'knowing') while attributes of meanings are nouns (e.g., 'knowledge').
3
Identify the seven or eight attributes
Sanusi mentions that the attributes of meanings are seven or eight. This hints at a scholarly difference about the exact count.
Traditionally, the seven attributes of meanings are knowledge, power, will, life, hearing, sight, speech. Some add persistence.
4
Reflect on significance
Ask yourself why this distinction matters for understanding divine attributes. It helps in discussing whether attributes are identical to essence or distinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثالُ الصفاتِ المعنويَّةِ : كونه عالماً قادراً مريداً حياً . . . إلى آخرها . ومثالُ صفاتِ المعاني : العلمُ والقدرة والإرادة والحياة ... إلى آخر الصفاتِ السبع أو الثمانِ ومثالُ صفاتِ الأفعالِ : خَلْقُ اللهِ جلَّ وعلا ورَزْقُهُ وإحسانه ، ومنهم مَنْ يمثْلُها بالأسماء الدالَّة عليها ؛ كالخالقِ والرازق والمحيي والمميت ومثالُ الصفاتِ الجامعةِ : عزَّةُ اللهِ تعالى وجلاله وعظمته وكبرياؤه ، ونحو ذلكَ ومِنَ المحققينَ (١) مَنْ يقسمُ صفاتِ الباري تعالى باعتبار آخر غيرِ
And the example of the meaningful attributes: His being knowing, powerful, willing, living... to the end. And the example of the attributes of meanings: knowledge, power, will, life... to the end of the seven or eight attributes. And the example of the attributes of acts: Allah's creation, provision, and beneficence, and some exemplify them by the names indicating them, such as the Creator, the Provider, the Giver of life, the Giver of death. And the example of the comprehensive attributes: Allah's might, majesty, greatness, and pride, and similar. And among the verifiers (1) are those who divide the attributes of Allah, exalted, according to another consideration...
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage provides concrete examples for each category. For meaningful attributes, examples are being knowing, powerful, willing, living. For attributes of meanings, examples are knowledge, power, will, life — listed as seven or eight attributes. For attributes of acts, examples are creation, provision, beneficence, and also the names like Creator, Provider, Giver of life, Giver of death. For comprehensive attributes, examples are might, majesty, greatness, pride. The passage ends with a note that some verifiers divide divine attributes according to another consideration (perhaps cutting off). The footnote identifies these as Imam al-Razi and his followers.

Meaningful attributes describe states, while attributes of meanings are the conceptual entities they refer to.

Divine Attributes
advanced

Classification of Divine Attributes: Meanings, Acts, and Comprehensive
Sanusi classifies divine attributes with definitions, examples, and an alternative division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold classification of divine attributes according to Sanusi, including the distinction between ṣifāt al-maʿānī and ṣifāt maʿnawiyya, and the alternative division into relational and essential attributes.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are classified into meaning-based, act-related, comprehensive, and also relational vs. essential.

Sanusi presents a detailed classification of divine attributes, distinguishing between the attributes themselves (meaning-attributes) and the descriptions they produce (meaning-based attributes). He then introduces the attributes of acts and comprehensive attributes, and finally an alternative twofold division that highlights changeable relations versus unchangeable realities. This passage provides the essential framework for understanding how theologians organize God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalysing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own structure come through.
2
Identify the three main categories
Find the definitions of ṣifāt al-maʿānī, ṣifāt al-afʿāl, and al-ṣifāt al-jāmiʿa. Notice how each is defined and what examples are given.
Pay special attention to the relation between ṣifāt al-maʿānī and al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya.
3
Understand the cause-effect relationship
The text states a necessary concomitance like cause and effect between ṣifāt al-maʿānī and al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya. Ask yourself why this distinction matters.
This is a key point in Ashʿarī theology.
4
Examine the alternative division
Consider the twofold division into relational (iḍāfa) and essential (ḥaqīqiyya). Note how the relational aspects change while the essential realities do not.
This division comes from scholars like al-Rāzī.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما صفات المعاني : فهي عبارة عن كل صفة قائمة بموصوف موجبة له حكماً وقيل : هي المعاني الموجبة للأحوال . فبين المعاني والمعنوية تلازم عند أهل السنة تلازم العلة ومعلولها . وأما صفات الأفعال : فهي عبارة عن صدور الآثار عن قدرته وإرادته جل وعلا وأما الصفات الجامعة لجميع الأقسام : فهي عبارة عن كل صفة تدل على معنى يندرج فيه سائر الأقسام الستة . ومثال الصفات المعنوية : كونه عالماً قادراً مريداً حياً . . . إلى آخرها . ومثال صفات المعاني : العلم والقدرة والإرادة والحياة ... إلى آخر الصفات السبع أو الثمان ومثال صفات الأفعال : خلق الله جل وعلا ورزقه وإحسانه ، ومنهم من يمثلها بالأسماء الدالة عليها ؛ كالخالق والرازق والمحيي والمميت ومثال الصفات الجامعة : عزة الله تعالى وجلاله وعظمته وكبرياؤه ، ونحو ذلك ومن المحققين من يقسم صفات الباري تعالى باعتبار آخر غير ما سبق إلى قسمين : إلى إضافة لا وجود لها في الأعيان ؛ كتعلق العلم والقدرة والإرادة ، وهي متغيرة متبدلة ، وإلى حقيقية ؛ كنفس العلم والقدرة والإرادة ، وهذه قديمة لا تتغير ولا تتبدل
As for the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī): they are every attribute subsisting in a described object, necessitating a ruling for it. And it is said: they are the meanings that necessitate the states (aḥwāl). So between the meanings and the meaning-based attributes (al-maʿnawiyya) there is a necessary concomitance according to the Ahl al-Sunna, like the concomitance of cause and effect. And as for the attributes of acts (ṣifāt al-afʿāl): they are the issuing of effects from His power and will, exalted and high. And as for the attributes comprehensive of all divisions (al-ṣifāt al-jāmiʿa): they are every attribute that indicates a meaning under which the other six divisions are subsumed. An example of the meaning-based attributes (ṣifāt maʿnawiyya): His being knowing, powerful, willing, living, etc. An example of the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī): knowledge, power, will, life, etc., up to the seven or eight attributes. An example of the attributes of acts: God's creation, provision, and beneficence, and some exemplify them by the names indicating them, such as the Creator, the Provider, the Giver of life, and the Causer of death. An example of the comprehensive attributes: God's might, majesty, greatness, pride, and the like. And among the verifying scholars (al-muḥaqqiqīn) are those who divide the attributes of the Exalted Creator according to another consideration besides the foregoing into two divisions: into relational (iḍāfa) having no existence in concrete entities, like the relation of knowledge, power, and will, and these are changing and variable; and into essential (ḥaqīqiyya), like knowledge, power, and will themselves, and these are eternal, not changing nor varying.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage is a classification of divine attributes. The author first defines four types. The attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) are those descriptions that subsist in an entity and necessitate a ruling; they are the very entities (like knowledge, power) that are the causes of the meaning-based attributes (ṣifāt maʿnawiyya), which are the descriptions of being knowing, powerful, etc. The relationship between the attributes of meanings and the meaning-based attributes is like that of cause and effect according to Ahl al-Sunna. Next, the attributes of acts (ṣifāt al-afʿāl) are those that involve the production of effects from divine power and will. Then, the comprehensive attributes (ṣifāt jāmiʿa) are those that subsume all the previous six categories under a single meaning, such as majesty. The author gives concrete examples for each: for meaning-based attributes, the descriptions of being knowing, powerful, willing, living; for attributes of meanings, the actual entities of knowledge, power, will, life (the seven or eight essential attributes); for attributes of acts, God's creating, providing, and acts like giving life and death; for comprehensive attributes, qualities like might, majesty, greatness. Finally, the author mentions an alternative division by some scholars (like al-Razi and followers) who split attributes into two: the relational aspects that are non-existent and changeable (like the relation of knowledge to objects) and the essential realities (like knowledge itself) that are eternal and unchangeable.

Key Terms
صفات المعاني
الصفات المعنوية
صفات الأفعال
الصفات الجامعة
إضافة
حقيقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that ṣifāt al-maʿānī and al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya are the same thing.
Correction: They are distinct: ṣifāt al-maʿānī are the entities (e.g., knowledge, power) that cause the descriptions (e.g., being knowing, being powerful). They are related as cause and effect.

Checkpoint
What is the relationship between ṣifāt al-maʿānī and al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya according to Ahl al-Sunna?
A) They are identical.
B) They are like cause and effect.
C) They are contradictory.
D) They are unrelated.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's classification provides a systematic way to understand divine attributes, distinguishing between entities, descriptions, actions, and overarching qualities, with a further division into changeable relations and eternal realities.

Which one makes you feel closer to Him in your daily life? Commit to meditating on one essential attribute this week that you usually overlook.
?
The scholars say that the relation of God's knowledge to changing events is itself changeable, but His knowledge is eternal. ?
God's comprehensive attributes like majesty and greatness subsume all others. Do you truly sense awe and reverence for God in your worship? If not, what one comprehensive attribute—might, majesty, greatness, or pride—can you contemplate deeply tonight before you sleep, so that your heart trembles before Him?

Proof for the Existence of States as Intermediates
Sanusi presents two arguments for the existence of states as intermediaries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam proof that states (ahwal) are intermediate between existence and non-existence, and the problems that arise if they are considered fully existent or non-existent.

Core Idea
States are neither existent nor non-existent but an intermediate reality.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a key argument from those who affirm states (ahwal) as real attributes. He shows that existence itself cannot be a fully existent attribute added to essence, as this leads to infinite regress. Instead, existence must be a state — an intermediary between being and non-being. The second argument uses the example of blackness and whiteness to further illustrate the need for an intermediate category.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Notice the thesis that states are intermediaries between existence and non-existence.
2
Analyze the first argument
Focus on the reasoning: why existence cannot be fully existent. Identify the two consequences mentioned: infinite regress (tasalsul) and describing the non-existent by its opposite.
The first argument concludes that existence must be an intermediary.
3
Consider the second argument
Examine the comparison of blackness and whiteness. Note how they share a genus (color-ness) but differ by specific differences. Ponder the two problematic alternatives that arise.
The second argument is incomplete, but the direction is clear: the differences must be states.
4
Reflect on the overall proof
Understand the via media approach: showing the inadequacy of both extremes (existent or non-existent) and positing an intermediate category.
This method is common in kalam for establishing concepts like states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على ثبوت الأحوال عند القائلين بها . احتج القائلون بإثبات الأحوال وأنها واسطة بين الوجود والعدم : بأن الوجود مشترك زائد على الماهية ليس بموجود ، وإلا لساوى وجوده وجود غيره ، فيزيد وجوده ، فننقل الكلام إلى هذا الوجود الثاني ، ثم كذلك ويتسلسل ، ولا ، وإلا لاتصف معدوم الشيء بنقيضه ؛ إذ المعدوم نقيض الموجود ، فكيف يكون صفة له ؟! فإذاً تعين أنه واسطة ، وهو المطلوب . وأيضاً السواد يشارك البياض في اللونية ، ويخالفه في السوادية فيتغايران ضرورة مخالفة ما به التمايز لما به التشارك ، فإما أن يوجد هذان الوصفان للسواد فيلزم قيام العرض بالعرض ، أو يعدما فيتركب
On the establishment of states (aḥwāl) according to those who affirm them. Those who affirm states argued that they are an intermediary (wāsiṭa) between existence and non-existence: because existence (al-wujūd) is a common attribute (mushtarak) superadded to quiddity (al-māhiyya) and is not existent (laysa bi-mawjūd), otherwise its existence would equal the existence of another, resulting in an increase of existence, so we would transfer the discussion to this second existence, and so on leading to infinite regress (tasalsul). And if not (i.e., if existence were not an intermediary), then a non-existent thing would be described by its opposite (naqīḍ), for the non-existent is the opposite of the existent; so how could it be an attribute of it? Thus it is determined that it is an intermediary, which is what was sought. Also, blackness shares with whiteness in color-ness (al-lawniyya) and differs in black-ness (al-sawādiyya), so they are necessarily distinct because what differentiates is different from what is shared. So either these two descriptions exist in the blackness, entailing the inherence of an accident in an accident, or they are non-existent, leading to composition from non-existents...
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a proof (argument) for the reality of states (aḥwāl) as intermediate entities between existence and non-existence. The author first states the thesis: states are intermediaries. Then he gives two arguments. The first argument: Existence is a common attribute added to quiddity (essence). If existence itself were existent, then we would have an existence of existence, leading to an infinite regress. Moreover, if existence were not an intermediary, then a non-existent entity would be described by its opposite, which is impossible. Therefore existence must be an intermediary state. The second argument (incomplete in the source): Blackness and whiteness share the genus of color but differ by specific differences. These differences must either be existent (leading to accident inhering in accident, which is problematic) or non-existent (leading to composition from non-existents). The argument is cut off, but the reasoning implies that they must be states—neither fully existent nor non-existent. The passage follows the standard kalam method of showing the inadequacy of two opposite positions and positing a third intermediate option.

Key Terms
أحوال
واسطة
وجود
ماهية
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: States are either completely existent or completely non-existent.
Correction: The proof demonstrates that states are neither fully existent nor non-existent; they are an intermediate reality.

Checkpoint
In Sanusi's first argument for states, what problem does he identify if existence (wujud) were considered fully existent?
A) It would lead to infinite regress.
B) It would cause the attribute to be separate from the essence.
C) It would make existence a non-attribute.
D) It would require existence to be a substance.

Key Takeaway
States (ahwal) are proven as an intermediate category between existence and non-existence through arguments showing the impossibility of either extreme.

Classification of Divine Attributes into Relational and Real
Sanusi divides divine attributes into relational and real types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between relational attributes (idafa) and real attributes (haqiqiyya) in divine attributes, and why relational attributes are changeable while real attributes are eternal.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are of two kinds: relational and real; only the latter are eternal.

Imam Sanusi introduces a key classification in the study of divine attributes. He distinguishes between attributes that are purely relational, having no external existence, and those that are real and inherent to God. This passage lays the groundwork for understanding how God's attributes relate to His creation without implying change in His essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice the two-part structure signaled by 'ila qismayn' (into two divisions).
2
Identify the two types
The passage names two types: idafa (relational) and haqiqiyya (real). Identify the examples given for each type.
The examples are taluq (relation) for the first and the essence of the attributes for the second.
3
Understand why relational attributes change
Relational attributes, like the relation of knowledge to an object, change because the objects of knowledge change. Real attributes, like knowledge itself, are eternal and unchanging.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
This distinction protects God's immutability. God's real attributes are eternal and unaffected by creation, while relational aspects reflect creation's dependence on God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما سبق إلى قسمين: إلى إضافة لا وجود لها في الأعيانِ، كتعلق العلم والقدرة والإرادة، وهي متغيرة متبدلة، وإلى حقيقية، كنفس العلم والقدرة والإرادة، وهذه قديمة لا تتغير ولا تتبدل.
What preceded is divided into two divisions: into an addition that has no existence in external entities, like the relation of knowledge, power, and will, and these are changing and varying; and into a real [addition], like the essence of knowledge, power, and will, and these are eternal, not changing nor varying.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a classification. The author divides the attributes (or perhaps the relations of attributes) into two types. The first type is the 'idafa' (relational addition), which has no external existence; examples are the taluq (relation) of God's knowledge, power, and will to their objects. These relations are changing because the objects change. The second type is the 'haqiqiyya' (real addition), which are the actual attributes themselves, like the essence of knowledge, power, and will. These are eternal and unchanging. This classification helps distinguish between what is inherent in God and what is merely relational.

Key Terms

حقيقية
تعلق
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might mistakenly think that the relational attributes (like taluq) have real existence in the external world.
Correction: The author clarifies they have no existence in external entities; they are merely relational aspects that change with the objects.

Checkpoint

A) The real addition
B) The relational addition
C) Both types
D) Neither type

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes are classified into relational (changing) and real (eternal); only the real attributes are inherent to God.

The First Proof for States: Avoiding Infinite Regress
First proof for states using infinite regress and contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's first argument for the existence of states (ahwal) as intermediaries between existence and non-existence.

Core Idea
Existence cannot be existent nor non-existent; it must be an intermediary state.

Sanusi begins his case for states (ahwal) by showing that existence itself cannot be a fully existent attribute nor a non-existent one. If existence were existent, an infinite regress would follow. If it were non-existent, then non-existence would be described by its opposite. Therefore, existence must be an intermediary state – a 'hal'.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof passage
Read the first argument from 'احتج القائلون بإثبات الأحوال' to 'وهو المطلوب'. Pay attention to the two possibilities considered.
2
Identify the two possibilities
Note that Sanusi considers whether existence is existent or non-existent. Trace the consequences of each.
The argument uses reduction to absurdity.
3
Understand the necessity of the intermediary
Realise why both possibilities lead to contradiction, necessitating an intermediary state.
This is the core of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج القائلون بإثبات الأحوال وأنها واسطة بين الوجود والعدم: بأن الوجود مشترك زائد على الماهية ليس بموجود، وإلا لساوى وجوده وجود غيره، فيزيد وجوده، فننقل الكلام إلى هذا الوجود الثاني، ثم كذلك ويتسلسل. ولا، وإلا لاتصف المعدوم بنقيضه؛ إذ المعدوم نقيض الموجود، فكيف يكون صفة له؟! فإذاً تعين أنه واسطة، وهو المطلوب.

وأيضاً: السواد يشارك البياض في اللونية، ويخالفه في السوادية، فيتغايران ضرورة مخالفة ما به التمايز لما به التشارك، فإما أن يوجد هذان الوصفان للسواد فيلزم قيام العرض بالعرض، أو يعدما فيتركب الموجود من المعدوم.
The proponents of establishing the states argued that they are intermediate between existence and non-existence: because existence is common and additional to quiddity, not existing; otherwise its existence would equal the existence of another, so its existence would increase, and we would transfer the discussion to this second existence, and so on leading to infinite regress. And [if existence were not existing], then the non-existent would be described by its opposite; since the non-existent is the opposite of the existent, how can it be a description of it?! So it is determined that it is an intermediary, which is what is sought.

Also: Blackness shares with whiteness in color-ness and differs from it in blackness, so they necessarily differ due to the contrast of what differentiates from what is shared. So either these two descriptions exist in blackness, leading to the subsistence of an accident in an accident, or they do not exist, resulting in the existent being composed of the non-existent.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs for the existence of states (ahwal) as intermediary entities between existence and non-existence. The first proof argues that existence is a common element added to quiddity; if it were existent, there would be an infinite regress, and if it were non-existent, then non-existence would be described by its opposite (existence), which is contradictory. Therefore, existence must be an intermediary state. The second proof uses the example of blackness and whiteness: they share the genus color and differ in specific difference. If both the generic and specific descriptions were existent in blackness, then an accident (the description) would subsist in another accident (blackness), which is problematic. If they were non-existent, then an existent (blackness) would be composed of non-existents. Thus, these descriptions must be states intermediate between existence and non-existence.

Key Terms
حال
واسطة
وجود
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite regress is not a valid argument in kalam.
Correction: Sanusi uses infinite regress to show impossibility, which is a standard logical tool in kalam to reject an infinite chain of causes.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, what would happen if existence were existent?
A) Existence would be unnecessary
B) Infinite regress would follow
C) Non-existence would be described
D) Quiddity would be denied

Key Takeaway
Existence must be an intermediary state (hal) because calling it existent leads to infinite regress and calling it non-existent leads to contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that existence cannot be simply classified as existent or non-existent. How comfortable are you with having concepts in your theology that are neither fully real nor fully unreal? Does this feel like a necessary precision or an uncomfortable abstraction?
?
This argument relies on infinite regress—a logical tool used to avoid an endless chain. Where in your own beliefs about Allah's attributes might you have assumed a simple answer, when in fact a nuanced 'intermediary' is required? Will you accept the complexity, or will you resist it?
?
If existence is an intermediary state, then your own existence as a human being is not a simple fact—it is a state between being and non-being. How does that affect your sense of dependence on Allah every moment? Are you living as if your existence is a given, or as a constant gift?

The Second Proof for States: Blackness and Whiteness
Second proof for states using the example of blackness and whiteness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's second argument for states using the example of blackness and whiteness.

Core Idea
Generic and specific descriptions cannot both be existent or non-existent; they must be intermediary states.

Sanusi's second argument uses a concrete example: blackness shares color-ness with whiteness and differs in blackness. These two descriptions must either both exist, both not exist, or be intermediate. The first leads to an accident subsisting in an accident, the second to an existent composed of non-existents. Thus, they must be states.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof passage
Read from 'وأيضاً: السواد يشارك البياض' to the end. Focus on the example of blackness and whiteness.
2
Analyse the two descriptions
Identify the generic description (color-ness) and the specific description (blackness). Note how they differ.
3
Consider the two problematic outcomes
If both descriptions exist, an accident subsists in another accident; if both are non-existent, an existent is composed of non-existents. Both are impossible.
Thus, they must be intermediary states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج القائلون بإثبات الأحوال وأنها واسطة بين الوجود والعدم: بأن الوجود مشترك زائد على الماهية ليس بموجود، وإلا لساوى وجوده وجود غيره، فيزيد وجوده، فننقل الكلام إلى هذا الوجود الثاني، ثم كذلك ويتسلسل. ولا، وإلا لاتصف المعدوم بنقيضه؛ إذ المعدوم نقيض الموجود، فكيف يكون صفة له؟! فإذاً تعين أنه واسطة، وهو المطلوب.

وأيضاً: السواد يشارك البياض في اللونية، ويخالفه في السوادية، فيتغايران ضرورة مخالفة ما به التمايز لما به التشارك، فإما أن يوجد هذان الوصفان للسواد فيلزم قيام العرض بالعرض، أو يعدما فيتركب الموجود من المعدوم.
The proponents of establishing the states argued that they are intermediate between existence and non-existence: because existence is common and additional to quiddity, not existing; otherwise its existence would equal the existence of another, so its existence would increase, and we would transfer the discussion to this second existence, and so on leading to infinite regress. And [if existence were not existing], then the non-existent would be described by its opposite; since the non-existent is the opposite of the existent, how can it be a description of it?! So it is determined that it is an intermediary, which is what is sought.

Also: Blackness shares with whiteness in color-ness and differs from it in blackness, so they necessarily differ due to the contrast of what differentiates from what is shared. So either these two descriptions exist in blackness, leading to the subsistence of an accident in an accident, or they do not exist, resulting in the existent being composed of the non-existent.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs for the existence of states (ahwal) as intermediary entities between existence and non-existence. The first proof argues that existence is a common element added to quiddity; if it were existent, there would be an infinite regress, and if it were non-existent, then non-existence would be described by its opposite (existence), which is contradictory. Therefore, existence must be an intermediary state. The second proof uses the example of blackness and whiteness: they share the genus color and differ in specific difference. If they were non-existent, then an existent (blackness) would be composed of non-existents. Thus, these descriptions must be states intermediate between existence and non-existence.

سوادية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossibility of accident subsisting in accident is a physical principle.
Correction: It is a logical principle in kalam; an accident cannot inhere in another accident because accidents require substances.

Checkpoint
What is the problem if both the generic and specific descriptions exist in blackness?
A) Infinite regress

Are you willing to accept that some realities (like states) are neither fully existent nor non-existent, even if your mind wants a clear yes/no?
?
The proof uses logic to affirm that even in a simple blackness, there are distinct descriptions that cannot be merely mental. Look at your own beliefs: Are there theological concepts you've dismissed as 'just mental' or 'just words' because they made you uncomfortable? What would it cost you to take them as real states that demand a response from your heart?

Objections to States and Their Refutation
Sanusi refutes objections to states and presents a supporter's argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi addresses objections to the theory of states (ahwal) and why the issue of states is crucial for causal reasoning, definitions, and universal premises in theological proofs.

Core Idea
Denying states blocks causal explanation, definitions, and universal premises.

Having presented the proof for states, Sanusi now refutes two objections raised by opponents. He also reports a powerful argument from supporters of states: without states, the very possibility of causal reasoning and definitions collapses. This demonstrates the high stakes of the debate over states.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice how the passage is structured into three parts: refutations, a supporter's argument, and the chapter's purpose.
Pay attention to the technical terms like 'تعليل' (causation) and 'حدود' (definitions).
2
Identify the two objections and their refutations
The first objection says that existence is identical to essence, so no additional attribute (state) exists, avoiding regress. The second says that accidents can subsist in accidents. Find these in the text and see how Sanusi refutes them.
Sanusi notes that the second refutation has 'نظر' (consideration/weakness).
3
Understand the supporter's argument
A shaykh argues that denying states would close the door of causation (تعليل), definitions (حدود), and universal premises (مقدمات كلية) in proofs. This shows how central the theory of states is to rational theology.
This argument links states to broader methodological concerns in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورد الأول بأن الوجود عين ذات الموجود، وتميزه عن غيره سلب، فلا تسلسل، والثاني بتجويز القيام، وفيه نظر.

قال بعض الشيوخ ممن نصر القول بثبوت الأحوال: إن القول بنفيها يسد باب التعليل والحدود والمقدمات الكلية في الأدلة، وهو ظاهر، والمسألة لها تعلق بمسألة أصولية؛ وهي كون العموم هل هو من عوارض المعنى أم لا؟ فعليك بها.

وإذا عرفت هذا كله فمقصودنا من هذا الفصل: إقامة البراهين على ثبوت صفات المعاني له جل وعلا، والرد على المعتزلة المنكرين لها مع موافقتهم على وجوب كونه جل وعلا قادراً مريداً عالماً حياً.
The first [argument] was refuted by [saying] that existence is identical to the essence of the existent, and its distinction from others is negation, so there is no infinite regress. The second [was refuted] by permitting subsistence [of accident in accident], but there is consideration in that.

Some shaykhs who supported the establishment of states said: The denial of them closes the door of causation, definitions, and universal premises in proofs, and that is apparent. And this issue is connected to an usuli issue: whether generality is among the accidents of meaning or not? So refer to it.

And if you have understood all this, our purpose in this chapter is to establish proofs for the existence of attributes of meanings for Him, Exalted and Majestic, and to refute the Mu'tazila who deny them, despite their agreement on the necessity of His being powerful, willing, knowing, and living.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage contains three parts. First, objections to the two proofs for states: the first proof is refuted by stating that existence is identical to the essence of the existent, so there is no additional attribute leading to regress; the second proof is refuted by allowing the subsistence of an accident in an accident (qiyam al-'ard bil-'ard), but the author notes that this has problems. Second, a supporter of states argues that denying states would block the possibility of causal explanations (ta'lil), definitions (hudud), and universal premises (muqaddimat kulliyya) in proofs, which would be detrimental. He also links the issue to a principle of jurisprudence: whether generality (umum) is an accident of meaning or not. Third, the author concludes by stating that the purpose of this chapter is to prove the attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani) for God and to refute the Mu'tazila who deny them, even though the Mu'tazila agree that God is powerful, willing, knowing, and living.

Key Terms

مقدمات كلية

Correction: The Mu'tazila agree on the necessity of God being powerful, willing, knowing, and living, but they deny the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-ma'ānī) which are the subject of this chapter.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi shows that the denial of states would undermine the very foundations of theological reasoning, including causation, definitions, and universal premises.

Rebuttal of Objections and Purpose of the Chapter on Attributes
Author rebuts two objections about existence and ahwal, then states chapter purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi rebuts two objections against the doctrine of states (ahwal) and how this sets the stage for proving God's attributes of meaning.

Core Idea
Denying states (ahwal) undermines the very logic of theological proof.

Sanusi now tackles two objections that have been raised against the concept of states (ahwal). He shows that denying states would cripple rational theology, and then clarifies that the chapter's real goal is to prove the divine attributes of meaning against the Mu'tazila. This passage is a crucial methodological pivot.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once through
Read the Arabic passage and its translation without stopping. Let the flow of the argument settle in your mind.
The passage combines rebuttals and a purpose statement.
2
Identify the two objections and their refutations
The first objection concerns existence and regress. The second involves subsistence of accidents. Note how Sanusi refutes each and his reservation on the second.
The first refutation is decisive; the second has 'scrutiny' — it's not fully settled.
3
Analyze the shaykh's warning
The shaykh argues that denying ahwal blocks ta'lil, hudud, and universal premises. Consider why these are essential for rational theology.
This shows the high stakes of the ahwal debate.
4
Connect the purpose statement
Sanusi states his purpose: to prove attributes of meaning and refute the Mu'tazila. See how the ahwal discussion leads into this.
The Mu'tazila agree that God is described by these attributes but deny their reality.
5
Reflect on the method
Think about how this passage demonstrates the interdependence of kalam and usul al-fiqh, and why logical rigor matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموجودُ مِنَ المعدوم ، ورُدَّ الأَوَّلُ بأنَّ الوجودَ عينُ ذاتِ الموجودِ ، وتميزه عن غيره سلبُ ، فلا تسلسل ، والثاني بتجويز القيام ، وفيهِ نظر (۱). قال بعضُ الشيوخ ممَّنْ نصرَ القول بثبوتِ الأحوالِ (۲) : إِنَّ القولَ بنفيها يسدُّ بابَ التعليلِ والحدودِ والمقدمات الكلية في الأدلة ، وهو ظاهر ، والمسألة لها تعلُّق بمسألة أصولية ؛ وهي كونُ العموم هل هو مِنْ عوارض المعنى أم لا ؟ فعليك بها (۳) وإذا عرفت هذا كلَّه فمقصودنا مِنْ هذا الفصل : إقامة البراهين على ثبوتِ صفاتِ المعاني له جلَّ وعلا ، والردُّ على المعتزلة المنكرينَ لها مع موافقتهم على وجوب كونِهِ جلَّ وعلا قادراً مريداً عالماً حيّاً إلى آخرها .
The existent from the non-existent, and the first [objection] is refuted by [the fact] that existence is identical to the essence of the existent, and its distinction from others is by negation, so there is no vicious regress. And the second [is refuted] by allowing subsistence [of one accident in another], and there is scrutiny in it. Some of the shaykhs among those who supported the statement affirming states said: 'The statement negating them closes the door of causal explanation, definitions, and universal premises in the proofs.' And it is apparent. And the issue is connected to an usuli issue: whether generality is among the accidents of the meaning or not? So look into it. And when you have understood all this, our purpose from this chapter is to establish proofs for the affirmation of attributes of meaning for Him, Exalted and High, and to refute the Mu'tazila who deny them, despite their agreement on the necessity that He, Exalted and High, is powerful, willing, knowing, living, etc.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage combines a rebuttal of two objections with a preview of the chapter's goal. First, the author refutes two objections against the doctrine of states (ahwal): the first objection argued that if existence is an attribute added to the essence, it would lead to a regress; the author replies that existence is identical to the essence of the existent, and its distinction from other essences is by negation (salb), so there is no regress. The second objection involved allowing the subsistence of an accident in an accident (qiyam al-'arad bil-'arad); the author notes there is scrutiny (nazar) in this—implying the objection is not fully settled. Then a quote from some shaykhs who support the affirmation of states: denying states blocks the door of ta'lil (causal explanation), hudud (definitions), and universal premises (al-muqaddimat al-kulliyya) in proofs—meaning that the concept of states is essential for rational theology. The author adds that this issue relates to an usuli question: whether generality ('umum) is an accident of meaning or not, directing the reader to study that. Finally, the author states his purpose for this chapter: to prove the attributes of meaning (sifat al-ma'ani) for God—attributes like power, will, knowledge, life—and to refute the Mu'tazila who deny them, despite agreeing that God is described by these attributes. This sets the stage for the detailed arguments that follow.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying states (ahwal) is a minor technicality without major consequences for theology.
Correction: Denying states closes the door to causal explanation (ta'lil), definitions (hudud), and universal premises (muqaddimat kulliyya) in proofs — essential tools for rational theology. Therefore affirming states is methodologically necessary.

Key Takeaway

Mu'tazilite View on Divine Attributes and Created Speech
Author explains Mu'tazilite position that attributes are essential except speech, which is created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite position on divine attributes, especially their unique theory that God's speech is created, and why they distinguish speech from other attributes.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilites affirm most attributes as essential but deny that speech is eternal, claiming it is created in a body.

The Mu'tazila, a major theological school, agreed with Ahl al-Sunna that God possesses attributes, but they departed sharply on the nature of divine speech. While they held that attributes like power and knowledge are necessary by God's essence, they argued that speech cannot be an eternal attribute because it consists of temporal letters and sounds. This lesson presents their reasoning and the flaw the author identifies.

Guided Path
1
Learn the Mu'tazilite general position on attributes
Read the first part of the passage which states that attributes are wajiba li-dhatihi (necessary by essence) and not due to a meaning inhering in God.
Contrast this with the orthodox view that attributes are eternal and subsist in God.
2
Understand their exception for speech
Notice that they make an exception for the attribute of speech. They agree God is a speaker but define speech as created letters and sounds.
This exception is the core of the controversy.
3
Examine their reasoning about created speech
Follow their argument: speech must be temporal, temporal can't subsist in God, so God creates speech in a body. Reflect on why the author calls this a 'corruption'.
The author promises a fuller discussion later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : وهذه الأوصافُ واجبةٌ لهُ تعالى لذاته ، لا لمعنى ملازم لها يقوم بذاتِهِ جلَّ وعلا كما هو في حقنا ، واستثنوا مِنْ ذلكَ كونَهُ تعالى مُتكلّماً ، فوافقوا على أنه تعالى متكلم بكلام ، لكن خالفوا أهل السنَّةِ في معنى الكلام الثابت له تعالى ، فهم جعلوه حروفاً وأصواتاً يخلقها جلَّ وعلا في محل مِنَ الأجرام يتكلم بها ، ولا يقوم به هذا الكلام عندهم ؛ لأنَّه لا يكون إلا حادثاً ، وقيام الحوادث بذاتِهِ محال ؛ فمعنى كونِهِ مُتكلّماً عندَهم : أنَّه خالق للكلام في غيرِه ، وجاءَهم هذا الفسادُ مِنْ حصرِهم الكلام في الحروفِ والأصوات وسيأتي تحقيق القولِ معهم في ذلكَ إِنْ شاءَ الله تعالى (١)
They said: These attributes are obligatory for Him, Exalted, by His essence, not due to a meaning inhering in His essence, Exalted, as is the case with us. And they excepted from that His being, Exalted, a speaker. So they agreed that He, Exalted, is a speaker with speech, but they opposed the Ahl al-Sunna regarding the meaning of the speech established for Him, Exalted. For they made it letters and sounds which He, Exalted, creates in a place among bodies, speaking thereby, and this speech does not subsist in Him according to them, because it is only temporal, and the subsistence of temporal [accidents] in His essence is impossible. So the meaning of His being a speaker according to them is that He is the creator of speech in something else. And this corruption came to them from their restriction of speech to letters and sounds. And the investigation of the discussion with them regarding that will come, if God wills.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite position on divine attributes and their specific theory of speech. The Mu'tazila assert that all divine attributes (such as power, will, knowledge, life) are necessary by God's essence (wajiba li-dhatihi) and not due to any meaning (ma'na) inhering in Him, unlike attributes in created beings which derive from an inherent quality. However, they make an exception for the attribute of speech (kalam). They agree with Ahl al-Sunna that God is mutakallim (speaker), but they define His speech as created letters and sounds that He creates in some bodily locus, not as an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence. Their reasoning: speech can only be temporal (hadith), and temporal accidents cannot subsist in God's essence. Therefore, the meaning of God being a speaker is that He is the creator of speech in something other than Himself. The author then criticizes this view, stating that this corruption stems from restricting speech to letters and sounds—implying that divine speech is not limited to such created entities. The author promises a fuller investigation later in the work.

Misconception: Mu'tazila deny all divine attributes.
Correction: They affirm most attributes as essential but deny that speech is eternal; their disagreement is on the mode of speech.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila restricted divine speech to created letters and sounds, leading them to deny that speech subsists in God's essence.

Basran Mu'tazilite View on Divine Will and Its Impossibilities
Author presents Basran Mu'tazilite view on God's will and two impossibilities it entails.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Basran Mu'tazilite position on God's will and the two logical impossibilities that arise from it.

Core Idea
A temporal will without a locus forces God into absurdities.

The Basran Mu'tazila made an exception for the attribute of will, claiming God wills by a temporal will not located in Him. Imam Sanusi shows that this position leads to three impossibilities—though only two are recorded here. This lesson examines those impossibilities and why they are rationally untenable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the Basran position and the two impossibilities listed.
Note that the author claims three impossibilities but only two appear; the third is lost.
2
Understand the first impossibility
The first impossibility is the renewal of temporal states upon the Eternal, which would imply God's origination.
The author refers to an earlier discussion on the world's origination.
3
Understand the second impossibility
The second impossibility is the subsistence of a meaning (the temporal will) by itself without a locus, which is impossible for accidents.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how these impossibilities refute the Basran position and affirm the Sunni doctrine that God's will is eternal and not temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستثنى معتزلة البصرة أيضاً كونَهُ جلَّ وعلا مريداً ، فقالوا : مريد بإرادة حادثة لا في محلّ ، فالتزموا في ذلك ثلاثة أمورٍ كلُّها مستحيلة : أحدها : تجدُّدُ الأحوالِ الحادثة على الأزلي جلَّ وعلا ، وذلكَ يفضي إلى حدوثِ مَنْ وجبَ قدمُهُ ، وقد تقدَّمَ بسْطُ ذلك في حدوثِ العالم (٢) الثاني : قيام المعنى بنفسِهِ ، وهو محال
And the Mu'tazila of Basra also excepted His being, Exalted and High, willing, and they said: [He is] willing by a temporal will not in a locus. So they committed in that three matters, all of which are impossible: First: the renewal of temporal states upon the Eternal, Exalted and High, and that leads to the origination of the One whose pre-eternality is necessary, and the exposition of that has preceded in the origination of the world. Second: the subsistence of a meaning by itself, and it is impossible.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage exposes the Basran Mu'tazilite position on the divine attribute of will (irada). Like with speech, they except will from their general principle that attributes are necessary by essence. They assert that God is willing (murid) by a temporal will (irada haditha) that is not located in any substrate (la fi mahall). The author points out that this commits them to three impossible consequences (though only two are listed here). The first impossibility: the renewal of temporal states (ahwal haditha) upon the Eternal—meaning that God would be subject to new accidents over time, leading to His originating existence—which contradicts His necessary pre-eternality. The author references an earlier discussion on the origination of the world for more detail. The second impossibility: the subsistence of a meaning (ma'na) by itself—the temporal will is conceived as a meaning that subsists without a locus, but accidents require a substrate to exist. This is deemed impossible. The missing third impossibility is not provided in this passage. The passage thus argues that the Basran view is untenable because it entails absurdities.

Key Terms
معتزلة البصرة
إرادة حادثة
لا في محل
أحوال حادثة
قيام المعنى بنفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Basran Mu'tazila only differ on will without introducing absurdities.
Correction: The text shows their position forces multiple logical impossibilities, refuting their view.

Checkpoint

A) That God does not have will.
B) That temporal states are renewed in the Eternal leading to His origination.
C) That will is identical to power.
D) That will is created in a body.

Key Takeaway
The Basran Mu'tazilite view of a temporal will without a locus is refuted because it entails impossible consequences: God would be subject to change and accidents would subsist without a substrate.

Divine Attributes
intermediate
4 min
The Third Objection: Ruling Without Attribute
Sanusi presents the third objection on the return of a ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical problem of attributing a ruling to an essence that does not have the corresponding attribute subsisting in it, and why Sanusi considers this arbitrary.

Core Idea
Arbitrary attribution undermines the consistency of the Ash'ari doctrine.

Sanusi continues his critique of the Ash'ari theory of will by presenting a logical impossibility: if the attribute of will does not subsist in the essence, how can the ruling 'being willing' be attributed to that essence? He argues this is an arbitrary distinction not observed in other attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the third objection. Note the structure: it begins with a general statement, then a clarification, then a refutation.
2
Understand the logical structure
The objection says it's impossible for the ruling of an attribute (being willing) to return to an essence that does not have the attribute (will) subsisting in it, unless the ruling is specific to that essence. The Ash'aris deny specificity.
Specificity would mean only that essence can receive the ruling.
3
Analyze the refutation
Sanusi calls this 'arbitrary' (تحكم) and gives a counterexample: many attributes subsist in things without their rulings applying elsewhere.
E.g., the attribute of blackness subsists in a body, but its ruling (being black) doesn't apply to other bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثُ : عود حكمِهِ إلى ما لم يقم بهِ معَ نفْيِ اختصاصِهِ بهِ وهو محال . (١) يعني : عود حكم المعنى - وهو الكون مريداً - إلى الذات التي لم يقم فيها المعنى - وهو الإرادة - مع كون ذلك ليس مختصاً بالذات العلية ، وهذا تحكم؛ فكثير من المعاني قامت بغيرها ولم تقم أحكامها بها . والزموا أيضاً : مخالفة أصلهم في نفي صفاتِ المعاني ؛ مِنْ حيثُ لم يقولوا : مريد لنفسِهِ كما قالوا : قادرٌ لنفسه وعالم لنفسه ، بل بإرادة ، فأجابوا بالفَرْقِ ؛ قالوا : إنَّهُ لو كانَ مريداً لنفسه كما نقولُ : عالم مثلاً لنفسِهِ .. لعمَّ بمريديَّتِهِ كلَّ ممكن ، وأصلُهم : خروج كثير مِنَ الممكناتِ ـ كالمعاصي ونحوها ـ عن كونها مرادة الله ، تعالى أن يكون في ملكه ما لا يريد ، وما تخيَّلوه في ذلك باطل ؛ إذْ إرادته تعالى عامة التعلقِ بكل ممكن على ما يأتي برهانه (٢) وتحكمهم بأنَّ النفسي هو الذي يعم . . لا يخفى فساده ، وهم قد نقضوه في القادريَّةِ ؛ فإنَّهم زعموا أنَّه تعالى قادر لنفسِهِ ، مع أنَّ أفعال العبادِ الاختياريَّة غيرُ مقدورةٍ عندَهم للهِ ، تعالى عما يقولُ الظالمونَ علوّاً كبيراً .
The third [objection]: the return of its ruling to that which does not subsist in it, along with the negation of its being specific to it, and it is impossible. (1) Meaning: the return of the ruling of the attribute—which is being willing—to the essence in which the attribute—which is will—does not subsist, while that is not specific to the Exalted Essence, and this is an arbitrary judgment; for many attributes subsist in other things and their rulings do not subsist by those other things. And they also obligated [a contradiction against themselves]: the violation of their principle in negating the attributes of meanings; because they did not say 'willing by His essence' as they said 'powerful by His essence' and 'knowing by His essence', but rather by a will. So they replied with a distinction: they said that if He were willing by His essence, as we say, for example, knowing by His essence, then His being willing would encompass every possible thing, while their principle is that many possibles—like sins and the like—are outside being willed by God, exalted is He above having in His kingdom what He does not will. And what they imagined in that is false; for His will, exalted, has universal relation to every possible, as its proof will come. And their arbitrary judgment that the essential is that which is universal—its corruption is not hidden, and they have already violated it in the case of power; for they claimed that He is powerful by His essence, while the voluntary acts of servants are, according to them, not within God's power, exalted is He above what the wrongdoers say, a great exaltation.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage presents two objections against the Ash'ari doctrine that God's will is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence. The first objection (third in a series) argues that it is impossible for the ruling of an attribute—i.e., being willing—to return to an essence that does not have that attribute subsisting in it, unless the ruling is specific to that essence, which the Ash'aris deny. The footnote explains: if the attribute of will does not subsist in the essence, then the ruling of being willing cannot be attributed to the essence without arbitrariness, because many attributes subsist in things without their rulings applying elsewhere. The second objection is that the Ash'aris contradict their own principle of denying attributes of meanings (like power and knowledge as eternal entities). They affirm that God is powerful and knowing by His essence without additional attributes, but for will they posit an additional attribute (a meaning). When asked about this inconsistency, they reply with a distinction: if He were willing by essence, His will would encompass everything, but they believe sin is not willed by God. The author refutes this by stating that God's will actually encompasses everything (universal), and that their rule that the essential is universal is self-contradictory because they apply it inconsistently: they say God is powerful by essence yet deny that He has power over human voluntary acts. The author concludes that their position is absurd.

Key Terms
عود الحكم
المعنى
الحكم
اختصاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an attribute subsists in an essence, its ruling must apply to all that is attributed to that essence.
Correction: Many attributes subsist in things without their rulings applying elsewhere, as Sanusi states: 'many attributes subsist in other things and their rulings do not subsist by those other things.'

Checkpoint
What is the core of the third objection in this passage?
A) It is impossible for a ruling to return to an essence without the attribute subsisting in it unless the ruling is specific to that essence.
B) The Ash'aris deny that God has will.
C) The ruling of an attribute always applies to all entities.
D) The attribute of will is identical to the essence.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari view that God is willing by an eternal attribute leads to an arbitrary attribution of the ruling of being willing to the essence that does not contain that attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to defend the Ash'ari position against this objection, how would you avoid making an arbitrary judgment about God? Would you accept that the ruling of being willing is attributed to God without the attribute will subsisting in His essence? What does that reveal about the limits of human rational comparison with God?
?
Sanusi uses a mundane example (blackness in a body) to challenge a theological objection. How often do you apply analogies from created things to understand God? Are you willing to let that analogy be broken when it conflicts with divine transcendence? Commit to examining your own use of analogy in your beliefs about God's attributes.

The Fourth Objection: Contradiction on 'By Essence'
Sanusi presents the fourth objection on essential attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari inconsistency in not saying 'willing by essence' while saying 'powerful by essence' and 'knowing by essence', and Sanusi's refutation of their answer.

Core Idea
Inconsistency in attributing essential qualities undermines the Ash'ari system.

Sanusi levels a second objection: the Ash'aris violate their own principle of negating attributes of meanings (like power and knowledge as eternal entities) because they say God is powerful and knowing by His essence, but for will they posit an additional attribute. Their attempted distinction fails, as Sanusi shows.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth objection
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the contradiction: the Ash'aris say 'powerful by essence' but not 'willing by essence', instead saying 'by a will'.
2
Understand the Ash'ari reply
They reply with a distinction: if He were willing by essence, then His will would encompass everything, but they believe sins are not willed by God.
They argue that essential attributes are universal.
3
Analyze Sanusi's refutation
Sanusi refutes by stating that God's will is actually universal (as will be proved later), and that their rule that the essential is universal is contradicted by their own affirmation that God is powerful by essence yet they deny He has power over human voluntary acts.
Thus, their distinction leads to inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثُ : عود حكمِهِ إلى ما لم يقم بهِ معَ نفْيِ اختصاصِهِ بهِ وهو محال . (١) يعني : عود حكم المعنى - وهو الكون مريداً - إلى الذات التي لم يقم فيها المعنى - وهو الإرادة - مع كون ذلك ليس مختصاً بالذات العلية ، وهذا تحكم؛ فكثير من المعاني قامت بغيرها ولم تقم أحكامها بها . والزموا أيضاً : مخالفة أصلهم في نفي صفاتِ المعاني ؛ مِنْ حيثُ لم يقولوا : مريد لنفسِهِ كما قالوا : قادرٌ لنفسه وعالم لنفسه ، بل بإرادة ، فأجابوا بالفَرْقِ ؛ قالوا : إنَّهُ لو كانَ مريداً لنفسه كما نقولُ : عالم مثلاً لنفسِهِ .. لعمَّ بمريديَّتِهِ كلَّ ممكن ، وأصلُهم : خروج كثير مِنَ الممكناتِ ـ كالمعاصي ونحوها ـ عن كونها مرادة الله ، تعالى أن يكون في ملكه ما لا يريد ، وما تخيَّلوه في ذلك باطل ؛ إذْ إرادته تعالى عامة التعلقِ بكل ممكن على ما يأتي برهانه (٢) وتحكمهم بأنَّ النفسي هو الذي يعم . . لا يخفى فساده ، وهم قد نقضوه في القادريَّةِ ؛ فإنَّهم زعموا أنَّه تعالى قادر لنفسِهِ ، مع أنَّ أفعال العبادِ الاختياريَّة غيرُ مقدورةٍ عندَهم للهِ ، تعالى عما يقولُ الظالمونَ علوّاً كبيراً .
The third [objection]: the return of its ruling to that which does not subsist in it, along with the negation of its being specific to it, and it is impossible. (1) Meaning: the return of the ruling of the attribute—which is being willing—to the essence in which the attribute—which is will—does not subsist, while that is not specific to the Exalted Essence, and this is an arbitrary judgment; for many attributes subsist in other things and their rulings do not subsist by those other things. And they also obligated [a contradiction against themselves]: the violation of their principle in negating the attributes of meanings; because they did not say 'willing by His essence' as they said 'powerful by His essence' and 'knowing by His essence', but rather by a will. So they replied with a distinction: they said that if He were willing by His essence, as we say, for example, knowing by His essence, then His being willing would encompass every possible thing, while their principle is that many possibles—like sins and the like—are outside being willed by God, exalted is He above having in His kingdom what He does not will. And what they imagined in that is false; for His will, exalted, has universal relation to every possible, as its proof will come. And their arbitrary judgment that the essential is that which is universal—its corruption is not hidden, and they have already violated it in the case of power; for they claimed that He is powerful by His essence, while the voluntary acts of servants are, according to them, not within God's power, exalted is He above what the wrongdoers say, a great exaltation.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage presents two objections against the Ash'ari doctrine that God's will is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence. The first objection (third in a series) argues that it is impossible for the ruling of an attribute—i.e., being willing—to return to an essence that does not have that attribute subsisting in it, unless the ruling is specific to that essence, which the Ash'aris deny. The footnote explains: if the attribute of will does not subsist in the essence, then the ruling of being willing cannot be attributed to the essence without arbitrariness, because many attributes subsist in things without their rulings applying elsewhere. The second objection is that the Ash'aris contradict their own principle of denying attributes of meanings (like power and knowledge as eternal entities). They affirm that God is powerful and knowing by His essence without additional attributes, but for will they posit an additional attribute (a meaning). When asked about this inconsistency, they reply with a distinction: if He were willing by essence, His will would encompass everything, but they believe sin is not willed by God. The author refutes this by stating that God's will actually encompasses everything (universal), and that their rule that the essential is universal is self-contradictory because they apply it inconsistently: they say God is powerful by essence yet deny that He has power over human voluntary acts. The author concludes that their position is absurd.

Key Terms
الصفات المعنوية
مريد لنفسه
قادر لنفسه
التعلق العام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God were willing by essence, His will would have to encompass all things, including sins.
Correction: Sanusi argues that God's will does encompass all things (universal), so the objection's premise is false.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi consider the Ash'ari reply about 'willing by essence' logically inconsistent?
A) Because they also say 'powerful by essence' yet deny power over human acts, showing their rule is not universal.
B) Because they reject the attribute of will entirely.
C) Because they claim God's will is limited to good things only.
D) Because they affirm that God is willing by essence.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position on will is inconsistent because their own principle about essential attributes is contradicted by their view on divine power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that nothing happens in your life without God's will? If so, how do you reconcile that with the existence of sin and evil in the world? Does your heart accept God's universal will, or do you secretly resist it when things don't go your way?
?
The Ash'aris claimed God is powerful by essence but denied His power over human acts. Examine your own beliefs: do you ever limit God's power to protect your own sense of freedom or control? Is your 'free will' a comfortable excuse to avoid surrendering to His absolute authority?
?
Sanusi exposes inconsistency in a theological system. Where in your own faith practice are you inconsistent? Claim one thing with your tongue but live another? What would it cost you to be fully consistent with what you profess about God's attributes?

The Infinite Regress Argument Against an Originated Will
Sanusi presents the infinite regress argument against an originated divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an originated divine will leads to an infinite regress, and how this argument is used in Islamic theology to affirm the eternity of God's will.

Core Idea
An originated will needs a cause just like any other created thing, leading to an endless chain.

This lesson presents a classical kalam argument against the idea that God's will is created (hadith). If God's will were created, it would require specifiers for its existence and time, each needing another will, ad infinitum. This is a key proof for the eternality of God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
2
Identify the key terms
Find and note the terms: التسلسل (infinite regress), الإرادة الحادثة (originated will), and التخصيص (specification).
3
Understand the logical chain
The argument is: an originated will is a contingent thing that needs specification → specification requires another will → that will, if also originated, requires another → infinite regress. Ask yourself why specification requires a will.
In kalam, every contingent thing needs a cause for its specification.
4
Reflect on theological implications
Reflect on why infinite regress is considered impossible in Islamic theology, and how this argument supports the doctrine of God's eternal attributes.
Infinite regress would entail an endless chain of causes, which is seen as a logical absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً يلزمهم في حدوثِ الإرادة : التسلسل ؛ مِنْ حيث إنَّها حادث مختص بوجود بدلاً عن العدم، وزمان معيَّن بدلاً عن غيره ، فتفتقر إلى إرادة حادثةٍ كما افتقرَتْ سائر الحوادثِ إليها ، ثم ننقل الكلامَ إلى تلكَ الإرادةِ ، فيلزمُ فيها ما لزمَ في الأولى وهكذا أبداً . ولهذا قالَ مشايخنا : إِنَّ كلَّ صفةٍ لهُ تعالى يتوقَّفُ الفعلُ عليها ، ولا يصح ثبوته بدونِها .. فالقولُ بحدوثها يؤدّي إلى التسلسل وما أجابوا بهِ مِنَ الهوس الذي لا ينتحله عاقل ؛ وهو أنَّ الإرادة لا تُرادُ كما أنَّ الشهوة لا تُشتهى .. ظاهرُ الفسادِ لكل ناظر (١) ، فإِنَّ الإرادة الحادثةَ وُجِدَ فيها دليلُ الافتقار إلى إرادة أخرى ، والدليل العقلي يستحيل وجوده بدونِ مدلوله ، والشهوة لا دليل على افتقارها إلى شهوة أخرى ، بل يجوز أن تُشتهى وألا تشتهى ، وقد وقع في العادة الأمرانِ ، فالشهوة ممَّا يجوز أن تتعلَّق بها الشهوة ، والإرادة الحادثة ممَّا يجب أن تتعلَّق بها الإرادة ، فظهر الفرق .
And also they are obligated, regarding the origination of will: infinite regress; because it is an originated thing specified with existence instead of non-existence, and a particular time instead of other times, so it needs an originated will just as other originated things need it. Then we transfer the discourse to that will, and what applied to the first applies to it, and so on forever. For this reason our masters said: 'Every attribute of His, upon which an action depends, and its existence is not valid without it... so the statement that it is originated leads to infinite regress.' And their reply—which is a delirium that no rational person would adopt—is that will is not willed, just as appetite is not desired. That is clearly corrupt to every observer (1). For the originated will contains evidence of need for another will, and the rational proof is impossible to exist without its implication, while appetite has no evidence of need for another appetite; rather it may be desired or not desired, and both have occurred in habit. So appetite is something that appetite may relate to, while originated will is something that will must relate to. Thus the difference appears.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage presents a classic argument against the origination of God's will: If God's will were originated (hadith), then it would require specification (takhsis) for its existence at a particular time rather than another, and for its particular nature. Such specification requires another will, which would then itself require specification, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). The author notes that his predecessors (mashayikhuna) taught that any attribute of God upon which an action depends cannot be originated, because that would entail regress. The Ash'ari response—that 'will is not willed' just as 'appetite is not desired'—is dismissed as absurd. The author refutes the analogy: unlike appetite, which has no evidence of needing a further appetite, an originated will clearly shows marks of contingency (need for specification), and a rational proof cannot exist without its implication. Therefore appetite may or may not be desired, but an originated will must be willed by another, yielding infinite regress. Thus the distinction fails.

Key Terms
التسلسل
الإرادة الحادثة
التخصيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the need for specification only applies to physical things, not to attributes like will.
Correction: Sanusi argues that the originated will is itself a contingent entity (hadith) and therefore requires a cause for its specification, just like any other originated thing.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why does an originated will require another will?
A) Because it is a mental concept.
B) Because it is a specific accident that needs a cause to specify it.
C) Because God cannot create directly.
D) Because will is eternal by definition.

Key Takeaway
An originated divine will would entail an infinite regress of wills, which is impossible; therefore God's will must be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will were subject to change based on newly arising causes, how would that affect your certainty in His promises and decrees? What does the eternity of His will demand of your trust in Him today?
?
Consider a time when you became impatient waiting for an answer from God. How might understanding that His will is eternal—not needing a cause for its timing—reshape your response to waiting?
?
The argument shows that an originated will would require an infinite chain of causes. In your own decisions, how often do you attribute your choices to external factors? How does God's uncaused, eternal will challenge you to rely on His wisdom alone?

Refuting the Appetite Analogy
Sanusi refutes the analogy between originated will and appetite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why comparing originated will to appetite is fallacious, and how the evidence of need for a cause distinguishes them.

Core Idea
Originated will shows clear need for a cause; appetite does not, so the analogy collapses.

The opponents of the infinite regress argument analogize divine will to human appetite: just as appetite is not desired by another appetite, will is not willed by another. Sanusi demonstrates why this analogy fails: originated will bears rational evidence of need for a cause, while appetite does not.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage where Sanusi dismisses the reply
Read the Arabic passage from 'وما أجابوا به' to the end. Note the strong language: 'الهوس الذي لا ينتحله عاقل'.
2
Compare the two cases
Compare the case of originated will and appetite. What evidence does each have?
Originated will has 'dalil al-iftiqar' (evidence of need), appetite does not.
3
Note the key difference
The rational proof (dalil 'aqli) for will cannot exist without its implication (madlul). For appetite, there is no such proof; it may or may not be desired.
4
Understand the conclusion
Sanusi concludes that appetite may be desired, but originated will must be willed. This shows the analogy is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then we transfer the discourse to that will, and what applied to the first applies to it, and so on forever. For this reason our masters said: 'Every attribute of His, upon which an action depends, and its existence is not valid without it... so the statement that it is originated leads to infinite regress.' And their reply—which is a delirium that no rational person would adopt—is that will is not willed, just as appetite is not desired. That is clearly corrupt to every observer (1). For the originated will contains evidence of need for another will, and the rational proof is impossible to exist without its implication, while appetite has no evidence of need for another appetite; rather it may be desired or not desired, and both have occurred in habit. So appetite is something that appetite may relate to, while originated will is something that will must relate to. Thus the difference appears.

Commentary
This passage presents a classic argument against the origination of God's will: If God's will were originated (hadith), then it would require specification (takhsis) for its existence at a particular time rather than another, and for its particular nature. Such specification requires another will, which would then itself require specification, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul).
