The Problem of Originated Accidents and the Denial of Will by Ka'bi and Najjar
Sanusi presents an objection against Ash'aris and introduces Ka'bi and Najjar's denial of will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari dilemma regarding originated accidents in God's essence and the alternative position of al-Ka'bi and al-Najjar who deny the attribute of will entirely.

Core Idea
If will is originated, even its states imply change in God; Ka'bi and Najjar avoid this by denying will as an attribute.

This lesson examines a critical objection against the Ash'ari view that originated states (ahwal) subsist in God's essence, implying accidents in God. It then introduces the radical solution of al-Ka'bi and al-Najjar, who deny the attribute of will altogether and reinterpret God's being 'willing' in scripture. Understanding this debate clarifies the stakes in classical kalam over divine attributes and origination.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection against Ash'aris
Begin by reading the first part where Sanusi argues that even if will is not directly in God's essence, its originated states are, and that is enough to imply change.
Notice the key term 'قيام الحوادث' (subsistence of accidents).
2
Understand the reasoning
Sanusi claims there is no difference between the renewal of states and the renewal of meanings—both indicate origination. Ask yourself: why would this be a problem for Ash'ari theology?
3
Examine Ka'bi and Najjar's denial
Read how al-Ka'bi and al-Najjar deny the attribute of will entirely. They reinterpret 'willing' as creating (for God's actions) and presumably commanding (for servants' actions).
The passage cuts off, so we only have part of their view.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why denying an attribute might be seen as problematic. How does this relate to the broader debate on divine attributes?
This shows the diversity of thought in early kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يلزمهم أيضاً : قيامُ الحوادثِ بذاتِهِ تعالى ؛ إذ الإرادة الحادثة وإن لم يقولوا بقيامها بذاتِهِ جلَّ وعلا .. فقد قالوا بقيام أحوالها الحادثة بذاته تعالى ، ولا فرق في الدلالة على الحدوثِ بين تجدُّدِ أحوالِ المعنوية الحادثة على الذاتِ وبين تجدُّدِ معانيها . مذهبُ الكعبي والنجار في الإرادة : وذهب الكعبي والنجَّارُ وأتباعُهما إلى إنكار هذه الصفة أصلاً وتأوَّلوا كونه مريداً لما ورد السمعُ بإطلاقِهِ ، فقال الكعبي : معناه بالنسبة إلى أفعالِهِ : أَنَّهُ خالقها ومنشتُها ، وبالنسبة إلى أفعال عبيده :
Then they are also obligated: the subsistence of originated accidents in His essence; for the originated will, even if they do not say it subsists in His essence, they have said that its originated states subsist in His essence. And there is no difference in indicating origination between the renewal of originated meaningful states upon the essence and the renewal of their meanings. The doctrine of al-Ka'bi and al-Najjar regarding will: And al-Ka'bi and al-Najjar and their followers went to the denial of this attribute entirely and interpreted His being willing according to what the revelation has declared. So al-Ka'bi said: its meaning with respect to His actions is that He is their creator and originator, and with respect to the actions of His servants:
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage first presents another objection: if will is originated, even if it does not subsist in God's essence, its originated states (ahwal) do subsist in His essence, and that is sufficient to attribute change to God, which is impossible. Then it introduces the view of al-Ka'bi and al-Najjar, who deny the attribute of will altogether. They reinterpret God's being 'willing' in revealed texts: for His own actions it means He creates them; for the actions of servants, the passage is cut off but likely means He commands or decrees them. This shows an alternative approach that avoids the problems of an eternal or originated attribute while still affirming the scriptural descriptions. However, the passage is incomplete, so the full doctrine is not presented.

Key Terms
قيام الحوادث
الأحوال
الكعبي
النجار
إنكار الصفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that denying the attribute of will solves the problem of origination without any consequences.
Correction: Sanusi implies that this denial leads to figurative interpretation (ta'wil) of scriptural texts, which many theologians consider problematic as it departs from the literal meaning.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ka'bi, what does 'willing' mean regarding God's own actions?
A) He intends them from eternity.
B) He is their creator and originator.
C) He commands them.
D) He approves of them.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position that originated states subsist in God's essence implies change, leading some like Ka'bi and Najjar to deny the attribute of will entirely.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Ka'bi and al-Najjar denied the attribute of will to avoid saying God changes. Have you ever been tempted to reject a difficult belief because it seemed to lead to an uncomfortable conclusion? What does that tell you about your own intellectual honesty?
?
This passage shows that every theological position comes with costs. When you study aqeedah, are you willing to accept the implications of the Ash'ari position, including ta'wil? Or do you find yourself wanting a simpler solution? Examine your heart: are you seeking truth or comfort?

Refuting the Claim that Will is Not Willed
Sanusi refutes the objection that will is not willed and distinguishes it from desire.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why originated will necessarily requires another will, unlike desire, and how this refutes the objection of infinite regress.

Core Idea
Will must be willed; desire may be desired or not.

Sanusi addresses an objection: if every originated will requires another will, this leads to infinite regress. The objectors claim that will is not willed, just as desire is not desired. In this passage, Sanusi refutes this by showing the rational proof for will and the absence of such proof for desire, clarifying the fundamental difference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the argument's structure emerge.
Notice the objection is stated first, then the refutation.
2
Identify the objection
Find the objectors' claim that will is not willed, analogizing to desire. Understand why they think this avoids infinite regress.
The key phrase is 'will is not willed just as desire is not desired'.
3
Analyze the refutation
Sanusi points out that originated will contains evidence (dalil) of need for another will, but desire does not. Explain why this difference is crucial.
The rational proof cannot exist without its conclusion (another will).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالقولُ بحدوثها يؤدّي إلى التسلسل وما أجابوا بهِ مِنَ الهوس الذي لا ينتحله عاقل ؛ وهو أنَّ الإرادة لا تُرادُ كما أنَّ الشهوة لا تُشتهى .. ظاهرُ الفسادِ لكل ناظر ، فإِنَّ الإرادة الحادثةَ وُجِدَ فيها دليلُ الافتقار إلى إرادة أخرى ، والدليل العقلي يستحيل وجوده بدونِ مدلوله ، والشهوة لا دليل على افتقارها إلى شهوة أخرى ، بل يجوز أن تُشتهى وألا تشتهى ، وقد وقع في العادة الأمرانِ ، فالشهوة ممَّا يجوز أن تتعلَّق بها الشهوة ، والإرادة الحادثة ممَّا يجب أن تتعلَّق بها الإرادة ، فظهر الفرقُ
So the statement that it is originated leads to infinite regress, and what they responded with is a kind of nonsense that no rational person would adopt, which is that will is not willed just as desire is not desired. Its corruption is clear to every observer. For the originated will contains evidence of need for another will, and a rational proof cannot exist without its conclusion. As for desire, there is no proof that it needs another desire; rather, it may be desired or not desired, and both occur customarily. So desire is something that may be desired, while originated will is something that necessarily must be willed. Thus the difference is clear.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage refutes the objection that will (irada) is not willed, analogizing it to desire (shahwa). The objectors argued that if every originated will requires another will, this leads to infinite regress, so they denied the need for a will to be willed. The author refutes this by showing that the rational proof for the need of will in an originated will is present, whereas no such proof exists for desire. Moreover, desire is customarily both desired and not desired, so it is not analogous. The conclusion is that originated will necessarily requires another will, unlike desire. This is an objection-and-answer passage.

Key Terms
إرادة
شهوة
تسلسل
هوس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Will and desire are analogous in that neither needs to be willed/desired.
Correction: Will necessarily requires another will due to rational proof, while desire does not require another desire.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the key difference between originated will (الإرادة الحادثة) and desire (الشهوة)?
A) Will cannot be willed but desire can.
B) Originated will necessarily requires another will, while desire does not necessarily require another desire.
C) Both require another of their kind.
D) Will is eternal but desire is originated.

Key Takeaway
The key difference between originated will and desire is that will necessarily requires another will, while desire does not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a decision, do you ever stop to consider what willed that will? How often do you trace your own desires back to their root, and what does that reveal about your need for a higher Will?
?
The objectors used a false analogy to avoid an uncomfortable conclusion. In your own life, where are you using false analogies to justify not following the truth? What would change if you accepted the full weight of the evidence?
?
If every originated will requires another will, then your free choices are part of a chain. Does that chain end with you, or do you acknowledge that your ability to will is itself willed by another? Answer honestly—what does your daily reliance on God's creation say about your belief in His will?

Originated States Imply Accidents in Allah
Sanusi argues that affirming originated states implies accidents in Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming originated states (ahwal) in Allah forces one to admit that accidents subsist in Him, contrary to those who deny accidents in the divine essence.

Core Idea
Originated states and accidents are equivalent in proving change in Allah.

This lesson examines a logical argument by Sanusi against those who affirm an originated will but deny its subsistence in Allah, instead claiming that its states subsist in Him. Sanusi shows that this distinction is untenable: originated states are no different from accidents in implying origination and change in the divine essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, focusing on the key terms 'حوادث', 'أحوال', 'قيام', 'ذات'. Then read the literal translation.
Notice the structure: 'يلزمهم أيضا' introduces a necessary consequence.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage contrasts two views: those who deny accidents in Allah but affirm originated states. Understand what each view entails.
The argument is an 'ilzam' – forcing an opponent to accept an unwelcome conclusion.
3
Analyze the equivalence claim
Sanusi states 'لا فرق في الدلالة على الحدوث' – no difference in indicating originatedness. Why are states and accidents equivalent?
Both involve renewal (تجدد) upon the essence, which indicates change.
4
Reflect on the implication
If originated states are affirmed, then accidents must subsist in Allah, which is a position the opponent rejects. This shows the inconsistency of their position.
This argument upholds divine transcendence (تنزيه) by rejecting any change in Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يلزمهم أيضاً : قيامُ الحوادثِ بذاتِهِ تعالى ؛ إذ الإرادة الحادثة وإن لم يقولوا بقيامها بذاتِهِ جلَّ وعلا .. فقد قالوا بقيام أحوالها الحادثة بذاته تعالى ، ولا فرق في الدلالة على الحدوثِ بين تجدُّدِ الأحوالِ المعنوية الحادثة على الذاتِ وبين تجدُّدِ معانيها
Then it also follows for them: the subsistence of originated accidents in His essence, Exalted is He. For the originated will, even if they do not affirm its subsistence in His essence, Mighty and Exalted, they have affirmed the subsistence of its originated states in His essence. And there is no difference in indicating origination between the renewal of originated moral states upon the essence and the renewal of their meanings.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage presents an additional argument (ilzam) against those who affirm an originated will but deny that it subsists in Allah, instead claiming that its states (ahwal) subsist in Him. The author argues that there is no difference between these states and accidents (hawadith) in terms of indicating origination. Therefore, if they affirm originated states in Allah, they are forced to admit that accidents subsist in Him, which is a position they reject. This is a logical required argument (ilzam).

Correction: States are equivalent to accidents in implying originatedness, as both involve renewal upon the essence.

B) They are forced to admit that accidents subsist in Him because there is no difference between states and accidents in indicating originatedness.
C) They affirm that will is eternal.
D) They deny will altogether.

Key Takeaway
Affirming originated states in Allah logically entails affirming accidents in Him, undermining the denial of change in the divine essence.

Proof of the Correlation of Attributes and Essence
Sanusi proves attributes are distinct from essence via two arguments and the analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main proofs Sanusi uses to establish that divine attributes are distinct from the essence, and why the analogy of the absent to the witnessed is valid.

Core Idea
Attributes are not the essence itself; if they were, God would be contradictory.

Sanusi now turns to prove a foundational point: the divine attributes (such as power, will, knowledge) are distinct from God's essence. He offers two arguments: one based on our observation of the world, and another showing the absurdity of identifying attributes with the essence. He then addresses a potential objection by explaining the proper use of analogy between God and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice the two-part structure: first the correlation proof, then the analogy.
2
Identify the two proofs for correlation
Find the two arguments Sanusi gives for the correlation (talazum) between the attributes (ma'ani) and their being qualifications (ma'nawiyya). The first is based on the witnessed world (shahid), the second on the impossibility of the essence being multiple things.
The second proof leads to the famous problem of 'sawad halawa'.
3
Understand the impossibility argument
If attributes were the essence itself, the essence would have to be power, will, knowledge, etc. simultaneously. This is impossible because a single thing cannot have contradictory properties (e.g., being opposite and not opposite, requiring a locus and not requiring it).
This absurdity is known from the logical problem of blackness-sweetness (sawad halawa).
4
Examine the analogy of the absent to the witnessed
Since the Mu'tazila accept that in this world a knower is distinct from his knowledge, they must apply the same logic to God (the absent) using analogy. The key condition is that there must be a common factor (jami') between the two cases to avoid falling into ta'til (divesting God of attributes) or tashbih (anthropomorphism).
This analogy is a central method in Sunni kalam.
5
Reflect on how this avoids ta'til and tashbih
By using analogy with a common factor, one can affirm attributes for God that are analogous to creation without implying similarity. This preserves divine transcendence while avoiding outright denial of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دلائل تلازم المعاني والمعنوية
إمَّا لتحققِ تلازمهما في الشاهد ، وإمَّا لأنَّها لو ثبَتَتْ بالذاتِ للزمَ أنْ تكونَ الذات قدرة إرادة علماً ، ثم كذلك ما بعدها ؛ لثبوتِ خاصيَّةِ هذه الصفاتِ لها ، وكونُ الشيء الواحدِ ذاتاً معنى.. محالٌ ؛ لأنَّه يلزمُ أَنْ يُضادَّ وألا يُضادَّ ، وأنْ يستلزم وجود محلّ ولا يستلزمه ، وذلك جمْع بينَ متنافيين ، وأن يكون الوجودانِ فأكثر وجوداً واحداً على القول بنفي الأحوالِ (۱) ، وأصلُ ذلك : المسألة المشهورة بـ ( سواد حلاوة )(۲)
اعلم : أنَّ المعتزلة لمَّا ساعدَتْ على أنَّ العالِمَ القادر الحيَّ المريد في الشاهد عالم بعلم وقادر بقدرةٍ ومريد بإرادة وحي بحياة .. ألزمهم أهل السنّة رضي الله عنهم اعتبار الغائبِ بالشاهد ؛ قالوا(۳) : والجمعُ بين الغائبِ والشاهد يفتقر إلى جامعِ ، وإلا جرَّ إلى التعطيل والتشبيه
Proofs of the correlation of the meanings and the things meant: Either due to the fact that their correlation is realized in the witnessed world, or because if they were established by the essence, then the essence would have to be power, will, knowledge, and so on for the rest, due to the existence of the property of these attributes for it; and the fact that one thing being both essence and meaning is impossible; because it would necessitate that it be opposite and not opposite, and that it require the existence of a locus and not require it, and that is combining two contradictories, and that the two or more existences be one existence according to the denial of states (ahwal). The origin of that is the famous issue of 'sawad halawa' (blackness-sweetness). Know that when the Mu'tazila agreed that in the witnessed world the knower, powerful, living, willing is knowing by knowledge, powerful by power, willing by will, and living by life, the Ahl al-Sunna, may Allah be pleased with them, compelled them to apply the analogy of the absent based on the witnessed. They said: The combination between the absent and the witnessed requires a commonality, otherwise it leads to ta'til (divesting God of attributes) and tashbih (anthropomorphism).
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalil) for the correlation (talazum) between divine attributes (ma'ani) and the essence (dhat) they qualify (ma'nawiyya). Sanusi presents two lines of argument. First, the correlation is established empirically in the witnessed world (shahid): attributes are distinct from their subjects. Second, if attributes were identical to the essence, the essence would have to be multiple incompatible things simultaneously (e.g., power, will, knowledge), which is impossible. The impossibility is spelled out: a single thing would have to be both opposite and not opposite, require a locus and not require it, etc. This absurdity is known from the logical problem of 'sawad halawa' (blackness-sweetness). Then Sanusi presents the Sunni argument using qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (analogy of the absent to the witnessed). Since the Mu'tazila admit that in this world a knower is distinct from his knowledge, they must apply the same logic to God, otherwise they fall into ta'til (stripping God of attributes) or tashbih (likening God to creation). The key condition is that there must be a common factor (jami') between the two cases to make the analogy valid.

Key Terms
دلائل تلازم المعاني والمعنوية
الشاهد
الغائب
الأحوال
سواد حلاوة
التعطيل
التشبيه
قياس الغائب على الشاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Using analogy between God and creation implies anthropomorphism (tashbih).
Correction: The text shows that analogy is used precisely to avoid both ta'til and tashbih by requiring a common factor that preserves transcendence while affirming attributes.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if attributes were established by the essence itself (thabatat bi al-dhat)?
A) The essence would become multiple distinct things
B) The essence would be simple and indivisible
C) The attributes would be negated entirely
D) The analogy of the absent and witnessed would be invalid

Key Takeaway
The correlation of attributes and essence is proven both by observed reality and logical necessity, and the analogy from the witnessed world is valid provided there is a common factor that avoids both stripping God of attributes and likening Him to creation.

The Impossibility of Attributes Being Identical with the Essence
Sanusi argues against identity of attributes and essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's argument that divine attributes cannot be identical with the essence, and why that would lead to contradiction.

Core Idea
If attributes were identical with Allah's essence, the essence would become multiple contradictory things.

Sanusi presents a sophisticated argument against the view that Allah's attributes are merely identical with His essence. He draws on both empirical reasoning from the created world and logical deduction to show that such identity would lead to absurdities. This passage challenges us to think carefully about the relationship between God's essence and His attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two arguments
Notice that Sanusi gives two possible ways to prove the entailment: either from the witnessed world or from the logical consequence of identity.
2
Understand the contradiction
Focus on the statement that 'one thing being both essence and attribute' is impossible because it requires combining opposites.
The contradiction involves being both opposite and not opposite, and necessitating and not necessitating a locus.
3
Relate to the Sawad Halawa example
This example illustrates the problem: a single thing cannot simultaneously be black and sweet without contradiction.
The issue of 'Sawad Halawa' is a classic example of combining contradictory properties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دلائل تلازم المعاني والمعنوية ] إمَّا لتحققِ تلازمهما في الشاهد ، وإما لأنَّها لو ثبَتَتْ بالذاتِ للزمَ أنْ تكونَ الذات قدرة إرادة علماً ، ثم كذلك ما بعدها ؛ لثبوتِ خاصيَّةِ هذه الصفاتِ لها ، وكونُ الشيء الواحدِ ذاتاً معنى.. محالٌ ؛ لأنَّه يلزمُ أَنْ يُضادَّ وألا يُضادَّ ، وأنْ يستلزم وجود محلّ ولا يستلزمه ، وذلك جمْع بينَ متنافيين ، وأن يكون الوجودانِ فأكثر وجوداً واحداً على القول بنفي الأحوال (۱) ، وأصلُ ذلك : المسألة المشهورة بـ ( سواد حلاوة )(۲)
Evidence for the entailment of meanings and the meaning-like: Either because their entailment is realized in the witnessed, or because if they were established by the essence, then the essence would necessarily be power, will, knowledge, and then similarly for what follows; due to the establishment of the specific property of these attributes for it, and that one thing is essence and attribute is impossible; because it would require that it be opposite and not opposite, and that it necessitate the existence of a locus and not necessitate it, and that is combining two contradictories, and that the two existences or more be one existence upon the denial of states (ahwal) (1). And the origin of that is the well-known issue of (Sawad Halawa) (2).

Commentary
This passage is an argument against the view that the divine attributes are identical with the essence. The author presents two possible reasons for the entailment of attributes: either based on observation in the created world (al-shahid) or because if attributes were identical with the essence, the essence would become multiple distinct things. Then he shows that making one thing both essence and attribute leads to contradictions, such as being both opposite and not opposite, necessitating and not necessitating a locus. The argument concludes that this is impossible, especially on the denial of states (ahwal). The reference to the 'Sawad Halawa' issue is an example of such a contradiction.

Proof Through Entailment and Refutation of an Objection
Sanusi refutes an objection about causality in the witnessed and absent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the method of entailment (talāzum) to prove divine attributes and how he refutes the objection that causality in the witnessed world does not apply to God.

Core Idea
Absence of evidence is not evidence of absence.

In this passage, Sanusi continues his proof for divine attributes by explaining the entailment between the seven attributes of meaning and the attributes of concepts. He then addresses an objection: opponents claim that rulings in the witnessed are only caused by possibility, but possibility is absent in God. Sanusi refutes this by distinguishing between evidence and what it indicates, and by exposing a logical inconsistency in the opponents' own position. This lesson will guide you through the argument and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify key terms
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Note the terms: تلازم, صفات المعاني, أوصاف معنوية, عكس الدليل, عكس العلة. These are central to the argument.
2
Understand the entailment argument
The author refers to his earlier statement about entailment in the witnessed. Grasp that the seven attributes of meaning (knowledge, power, etc.) are entailed by the attributes of concepts (being knowing, etc.).
This sets up the analogy for the absent (God).
3
Analyze the objection
Opponents argue that in the witnessed world, rulings are caused by possibility; since God's actions are necessary, the causal link doesn't apply to Him.
This objection uses 'aks al-dalīl (reverse of evidence).
4
Follow the refutation
The author responds: possibility is just evidence, not the cause. Its absence doesn't negate the causation. Then he points out that if the opponents deny the causes (attributes of concepts), they must also deny the effects (attributes of meaning), but they don't. This inconsistency refutes them.
5
Reflect on the logical lesson
Appreciate the principle: absence of evidence is not evidence of absence. Also note the power of exposing internal inconsistency in an opponent's position.
This is a key argumentation skill in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلى البرهان بهذا الطريق - وهو طريقُ التلازم - أشرتُ بقولي : ( إما لتحققِ تلازمهما في الشاهدِ ) أي : تلازم الأوصاف السبع المعنوية وصفاتِ المعاني ، وقد عرفت فيما مضى تفسيرهما (١) ، والمجرور - وهو قولي : ( لتحقق ) - يتعلق بالفعل مِنْ قولي قبلُ : ) تلازمها معان ) . وقولهم : إِنَّ الأحكامَ إِنَّما عُلِّلَتْ في الشاهد لجوازها ، والجواز منتف في أحكامِهِ تعالى .. إلزام منهم لعكس الدليل (۲) ؛ وهو لا يُلزم ، وإبطال لعكس العلة ، وهو لازم ؛ فإِنَّ الجواز في الشاهدِ دليل على تعليل الأحكام المعنويَّة بمعانيها ، فلا يلزمُ مِنْ عدمِهِ في حقٌّ الغائب عدم المدلول الذي هو التعليلُ ؛ لأنَّهُ لا يلزمُ مِنْ عدمِ الدليل عدم المدلول ، وهم قالوا : يلزمُ ذلكَ ، وصفاتُ المعاني علل للصفات المعنويَّةِ ، فيلزمُ مِنْ عدمها عدمُها ؛ لأنَّهُ يلزمُ مِنْ عدم العلَّةِ عدم المعلول ، وهم قالوا بعدم لزوم ذلكَ ؛ لأنَّهم نَفَوا في حق الغائبِ صفاتِ المعاني ، وأثبتوا معلولاتها ؛ وهي الصفات المعنوية ، فقد عكسوا المعقول (۳)
And I pointed to the proof through this way – and it is the way of entailment (talāzum) – by my saying: 'Either due to the realization of their entailment in the witnessed,' meaning the entailment of the seven attributes of meaning (al-ṣifāt al-ma'nawiyya) and the attributes of concepts (ṣifāt al-ma'ānī), and you have already known their explanation earlier. The passive (i.e., the genitive phrase) – which is my saying: 'due to realization' – is attached to the verb from my saying before: 'their entailment with meanings.' And their saying: 'Indeed, rulings in the witnessed are only caused due to their possibility, and possibility is absent in His rulings, exalted' – this is an imposition on their part of the reverse of the evidence, and it does not bind, and an invalidation of the reverse of the cause, which is necessary. For possibility in the witnessed is evidence for the causation of moral attributes (aḥkām ma'nawiyya) by their meanings, so the absence of possibility in the case of the absent does not necessitate the absence of the indicated thing which is the causation, because the absence of the evidence does not necessitate the absence of the indicated thing. And they said that it does necessitate that, and the attributes of concepts are causes for the attributes of meaning, so the absence of the causes necessitates the absence of the effects, because the absence of the cause necessitates the absence of the effect. And they said that this does not necessarily follow, because they denied in the case of the absent the attributes of concepts while affirming their effects, which are the attributes of meaning, so they have reversed what is rational.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the argument for divine attributes using the method of entailment (talāzum). The author first clarifies that his earlier phrase 'due to the realization of their entailment in the witnessed' refers to the mutual entailment between the seven attributes of meaning (e.g., knowledge, power, will) and the attributes of concepts (e.g., being knowing, being powerful, being willing). Then he addresses an objection: opponents argue that rulings (aḥkām) in the witnessed world are caused by possibility, but since God's actions are not possible in the same way, the causal link does not apply to God. The author refutes this by distinguishing between the evidence and what it indicates. Possibility in the witnessed is evidence that moral attributes are caused by their meanings; but the absence of that evidence in the case of God does not mean the causation is absent, because absence of evidence is not evidence of absence. Then the author turns the tables: the opponents themselves say that attributes of concepts are causes for attributes of meaning, so if they deny the attributes of concepts in God, they should also deny the attributes of meaning. Yet they affirm the latter while denying the former, which is inconsistent – they have 'reversed what is rational'.

Key Terms
التلازم (al-talāzum)
الأوصاف السبع المعنوية (al-ṣifāt al-sab' al-ma'nawiyya)
صفات المعاني (ṣifāt al-ma'ānī)
الأحكام (al-aḥkām)
الجواز (al-jawāz)
عكس الدليل ('aks al-dalīl)
العلة (al-'illa)
المعلول (al-ma'lūl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If possibility is absent in God, then the causation (talīl) is absent.
Correction: The author shows that possibility is only evidence, not the cause itself; absence of evidence does not prove absence of the indicated thing.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the absence of possibility in God's rulings not negate the causation of attributes of meaning by attributes of concepts?
A) Because possibility is not a cause but merely a condition
B) Because the absence of evidence does not necessitate the absence of the indicated thing
C) Because God's rulings are not subject to causality
D) Because the opponents have already admitted the causation

Key Takeaway
The author demonstrates that denying the attributes of concepts (causes) while affirming their effects (attributes of meaning) is logically inconsistent, and that the absence of possibility in God does not undermine the causal link established through entailment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever affirmed a truth about Allah (like His knowledge or power) while denying the attribute that necessarily entails it, perhaps because you felt uncomfortable with the logical implications? What does this passage teach you about the danger of such inconsistency?
?
In your own life, when you lack clear evidence for something about Allah (like His wisdom behind a trial), do you find yourself doubting the reality of that attribute? How does Sanusi's principle—'absence of evidence is not evidence of absence'—challenge that tendency?
?
This passage accuses the opponents of 'reversing what is rational.' Are there areas in your understanding of the deen where you might be accepting effects without their causes, or vice versa, due to convenience or habit? Take a moment to identify one such area and resolve to seek consistency.

Divine Attributes

Proof from Impossibility of Attributes Being the Essence
Sanusi argues that if attributes were by essence, impossible contradictions follow.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's second proof that the attributes are not identical to the essence, based on the logical principle that sharing a most specific essential necessitates sharing a more general essential.

Core Idea
If the essence itself were knowledge and power, contradictions would follow.

Sanusi now presents a second proof for the necessity of distinct attributes. The argument uses a logical principle: if two things share a most specific property, they must share a more general property. Since the divine essence has the specific properties of knowledge and power, it would have to be these very things if there were no additional attributes. This leads to absurdities, proving attributes are not the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof's claim
Read Sanusi's statement that if attributes were established by the essence without additional meanings, then the essence would have to be those attributes (power, will, knowledge, etc.).
Notice that Sanusi refers to 'those seven descriptions'.
2
Understand the entailment principle
Sanusi explains that sharing in a most specific essential (e.g., rationality) necessitates sharing in a more general essential (e.g., animality). Analyze how this principle applies to the divine essence's properties.
Note the difference between 'cause' (Mu'tazila view) and 'entailment' (Ash'ari view).
3
Apply to knowledge and power
The essence has specific properties: knowledge relates to all objects comprehensively, power enables all possibilities. If these properties were not from attributes, the essence itself would be identical to knowledge and power.
Sanusi says 'necessarily' – this is a logical necessity given the premise.
4
Consider the Mu'tazila position
Sanusi notes this proof is even stronger against the Mu'tazila, because they hold that sharing in the most specific is a cause of sharing in the more general. This makes the identity claim even more problematic for them.
Sanusi will later object to the Mu'tazila cause-effect view.
5
Preview the absurdities
Sanusi hints that the consequent (essence being the meanings) leads to impossibilities, such as being opposite and not opposite simultaneously. This shows the proof is valid and the premise false.
These absurdities will be elaborated in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولي : ( وإما لأنَّها لو ثبتَتْ بالذاتِ ) فهو دليل آخر على إثباتِ الصفاتِ ، وتقريرُهُ أنْ يُقالَ : لو ثبتَتْ تلك الأوصافُ السبع بالذاتِ مِنْ غيرِ معانٍ تقومُ بها .. لَلَزِمَ أنْ تكونَ الذات قدرةً إرادةً علماً حياةً ... إلى آخرها وبيان الملازمة : أنَّهُ تقرَّرَ أنَّ الاشتراك في الأخص الذاتي يلزم منه الاشتراك في الأعم الذاتي ؛ كالاشتراك في الناطقية مثلاً يُوجِبُ الاشتراك في الأعم الذاتي وهي الحيوانيَّةُ ، وذلك عين حقيقة الإنسان، فيلزمُ أنَّ المشارك للإنسان في الناطقيَّةِ يكون إنساناً ، وقد ثبت للذاتِ العلية في مسألتنا خاصَّيَّةُ العلم مِنَ التعلُّق بالمتعلقاتِ على وجه الإحاطة والكشف ، وخاصّيَّةُ القدرةِ مِنْ تأتي وجودِ الممكنات بها ، فيلزم إذا لم تكن للذاتِ صفةٌ زائدة عليها أن تكون هي بنفسها عِلْماً قدرةً على الضرورة ، ولا يخفى عليك إجراء الإلزام في باقي الصفاتِ السبع . وهذا على أصل المعتزلة ألزم ؛ فإنَّ الاشتراك في الأخص يُوجِبُ عندهم الاشتراك في الأعم ؛ أي: هو عِلَّةٌ لهُ ، ونحن نقولُ : يلازمه ، لا أنَّهُ عِلَّةٌ لهُ ، وسيأتي الاعتراض عليهم في ذلك . وبالجملة : فيلزمُ على كلا القولين أنَّ الذات التي ثبتت لها في نفسها خواص تلك المعاني يجب أن تكونَ نفس تلك المعاني وأمَّا بيان بطلان التالي - وهو لزومُ أنْ تكونَ الذات عين تلك المعاني - فإليهِ أشرتُ بقولي : ( لأنَّهُ يلزمُ أنْ يُضادَّ وألا يُضادَّ . . . ) إلى آخره ؛ يعني : أنه يلزم على كونِ الذاتِ نفس المعنى لوازم كلُّها مستحيلة :
And as for my saying: 'Or because if they were established by the essence,' it is another proof for the establishment of the attributes. Its explanation is that it is said: If those seven descriptions were established by the essence without meanings subsisting in it, then it would necessitate that the essence be power, will, knowledge, life... to the end. And the explanation of the entailment: It has been established that sharing in the most specific essential necessitates sharing in the more general essential, like sharing in rationality, for example, necessitates sharing in the more general essential, which is animality, and that is the very reality of man, so it necessitates that the one who shares with man in rationality be a man. And it has been established for the supreme essence in our question the specific property of knowledge of being related to objects of knowledge in a comprehensive and disclosing manner, and the specific property of power of the possibility of existence of contingents by it. So it necessitates, if the essence has no additional attribute, that it itself be knowledge, power necessarily. And it is not hidden from you to apply the entailment to the remaining seven attributes. And this is more binding according to the Mu'tazila, because sharing in the most specific necessitates according to them sharing in the more general, i.e., it is a cause for it. And we say: it entails it, not that it is a cause for it. And the objection against them will come later. And in sum: it is necessitated on both opinions that the essence which has been established as having in itself the properties of those meanings must be those very meanings. As for the demonstration of the falsity of the consequent - which is the necessity that the essence be the very meanings - I indicated to it by my saying: 'Because it would necessitate that it be opposite and not opposite...' to the end, meaning: it necessitates upon the essence being the meaning itself impossibilities, all of them:
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage presents a second proof for the attributes. Sanusi argues that if the seven attributes (knowledge, power, will, etc.) were established by the essence itself without additional meanings, then the essence would have to be each of those attributes (power, knowledge, etc.), which is impossible. He explains the entailment using the principle that sharing in a most specific essential (e.g., rationality) necessitates sharing in a more general essential (e.g., animality). Here, the Supreme Essence has the specific properties of knowledge (comprehensive relation to objects) and power (ability to create possibilities); if these are not from additional meanings, the essence itself must be knowledge and power, leading to absurdities. Sanusi notes that this argument is even stronger against the Mu'tazila, who treat the specific as a cause of the general rather than a mere entailment. He concludes that on both views, the essence would have to be identical to those meanings, and then he references his upcoming refutation of that consequent (that it would lead to contradictions like being both subject to opposites and not subject to opposites). The passage ends by listing that impossibilities follow.

الأعم الذاتي
المعنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if the essence has specific properties (like knowledge and power), then the essence itself must be those properties.
Correction: Sanusi argues this would lead to absurdities, so the properties must come from distinct attributes, not the essence itself.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi say this proof is more binding on the Mu'tazila?
A) Because they accept the principle of entailment between specific and general essentials.
B) Because they believe sharing in the most specific is a cause of sharing in the more general, so the conclusion is even stronger.
C) Because they deny the attributes of meanings entirely.
D) Because they agree with the proof's conclusion.

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that assuming attributes are identical to the essence leads to an absurd identity of the essence with multiple distinct properties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you believe that Allah's knowledge and power are not merely His essence but distinct attributes, how does this affect your conviction that He knows every detail of your life and is able to change your situation? Are you living as if you truly believe that His knowledge is infinite and His power is absolute? When you sin in secret, do you remember that His attribute of knowledge is not just 'His essence knowing' but a distinct, active, and all-encompassing awareness that holds you accountable?
?
Sanusi's argument reveals that if attributes were the essence, contradictions would follow. How does this strengthen your reliance on the distinct names and attributes of Allah? Do you approach Allah through His attributes individually (like Ar-Rahman, Al-Aleem, Al-Qadir) or do you blur them into a vague concept of 'essence'? What would change in your worship if you consistently reflected on the distinct reality of each attribute?

Proof That Attributes Are Distinct from the Essence
Sanusi proves that if attributes are not distinct, the essence must be identical with them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's proof that the seven attributes must be distinct meanings (maʿānī) subsisting in the essence, and not identical with the essence itself.

Core Idea
If attributes were not distinct, the essence would have to be those attributes—an impossibility.

Sanusi now presents a crucial proof for the Ashʿarī position that the divine attributes are not identical with the essence but are additional meanings subsisting in it. The argument hinges on a logical principle about essential properties. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the logical entailment
The passage presents a logical principle: sharing in a more specific essential property (al-akhaṣṣ al-dhātī) necessitates sharing in a more general essential property (al-aʿamm al-dhātī). Find the example of rationality and animality.
This principle is key to the proof.
3
Apply the principle to God's essence
See how Sanusi applies this to God: the essence has specific properties of knowledge and power. If these are not from distinct attributes, the essence itself must be those properties.
The conclusion is that the essence would be knowledge and power, which is absurd.
4
Compare with the Mu'tazila view
Sanusi notes that the argument is even stronger according to the Mu'tazila because they see the entailment as causal. Understand the difference between 'cause' (ʿilla) and 'concomitance' (mulāzama).
This comparison highlights a key difference between Ashʿarī and Muʿtazilī methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إثباتِ الصفاتِ ، وتقريرُهُ أنْ يُقالَ : لو ثبتَتْ تلك الأوصافُ السبع بالذاتِ مِنْ غيرِ معانٍ تقومُ بها .. لَلَزِمَ أنْ تكونَ الذات قدرة إرادةً علماً حياة ... إلى آخرها
وبيان الملازمة : أنَّهُ تقرَّرَ أنَّ الاشتراك في الأخص الذاتي يلزم منه الاشتراك في الأعم الذاتي ؛ كالاشتراك في الناطقية مثلاً يُوجِبُ الاشتراك في الأعم الذاتي وهي الحيوانيَّةُ ، وذلك عين حقيقة الإنسان، فيلزمُ أنَّ المشارك للإنسان في الناطقيَّةِ يكون إنساناً ، وقد ثبت للذاتِ العلية في مسألتنا خاصَّيَّةُ العلم مِنَ التعلُّق بالمتعلقاتِ على وجه الإحاطة والكشف ، وخاصّيَّةُ القدرةِ مِنْ تأتي وجودِ الممكنات بها ، فيلزم إذا لم تكن للذاتِ صفةٌ زائدة عليها أن تكون هي بنفسها عِلْماً قدرةً على الضرورة ، ولا يخفى عليك إجراء الإلزام في باقي الصفاتِ السبع . وهذا على أصل المعتزلة ألزم ؛ فإنَّ الاشتراك في الأخص يُوجِبُ عندهم الاشتراك في الأعم ؛ أي: هو عِلَّةٌ لهُ ، ونحن نقولُ : يلازمه ، لا أنَّهُ عِلَّةٌ لهُ ، وسيأتي الاعتراض عليهم في ذلك (1) . وبالجملة : فيلزمُ على كلا القولين أنَّ الذات التي ثبتت لها في نفسها خواص تلك المعاني يجب أن تكونَ نفس تلك المعاني
Establishing the attributes, and its formulation is that it is said: If those seven descriptions (awṣāf) were established in the essence without meanings (maʿānī) subsisting in it, then it would necessarily follow that the essence itself is power, will, knowledge, life... etc. The demonstration of the entailment (mulāzama): It has been established that sharing in the most specific essential (al-akhaṣṣ al-dhātī) entails sharing in the more general essential (al-aʿamm al-dhātī); for example, sharing in rationality (nāṭiqiyya) necessitates sharing in the more general essential, which is animality (ḥayawāniyya), and that is the very reality of man. So it follows that the one who shares with man in rationality is a man. And it has been established for the sublime Essence in our question the specific property (khāṣṣiyya) of knowledge, which is the relation to objects of knowledge in the mode encompassing and disclosing, and the specific property of power, which is the bringing about of possible things by it. So it necessarily follows that if there is no attribute additional to the essence, then the essence itself is knowledge and power of necessity, and it is not hidden from you to apply the entailment to the remaining seven attributes. And this is more necessary according to the principle of the Muʿtazila; for with them sharing in the most specific necessitates sharing in the more general; i.e., it is a cause for it, while we say: it entails it, not that it is a cause for it. And the objection against them in that will come (1). In summary: it follows on both sayings that the essence which has in itself the specific properties of those meanings must be those very meanings.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhān) that the divine attributes (ṣifāt) are distinct from the essence (dhāt). It begins by stating what it aims to prove: if the seven attributes belong to the essence without additional meanings subsisting in it, then the essence itself would be those attributes. The author then demonstrates the entailment using a logical principle: sharing in a more specific essential property necessitates sharing in a more general essential property. For example, sharing in 'rationality' necessitates sharing in 'animality', because rationality is a specific type of animality. Applied to God: if the essence has the specific property of knowledge (i.e., encompassing all objects of knowledge) and power (i.e., bringing about possibles), and if these properties are not from distinct attributes, then the essence itself must be those properties—thus the essence would be knowledge and power. The author notes that this argument is even stronger for the Muʿtazila, who hold the entailment as a causal relation, whereas the Ashʿarīs see it as mere concomitance. The passage concludes that according to both views, the essence possessing these properties must be identical with them if no distinct attributes exist.

Key Terms

الملازمة
الأخص الذاتي
الأعم الذاتي
خواص المعاني
المعتزلة
الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes what it tries to prove (that attributes are distinct).
Correction: The proof does not assume the conclusion; it uses a shared logical principle about essential properties to show that denying distinct attributes forces identity with the essence, which is absurd.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi prove would follow if the seven attributes are not distinct meanings subsisting in the essence?
A) The essence would have no attributes at all.
B) The essence itself would be those attributes (e.g., knowledge, power).
C) The attributes would be eternal but separate from God.
D) The attributes would be created.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's proof shows that if divine attributes were not distinct meanings, the essence would have to be identical with those attributes, which is rationally impossible, so attributes must be distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the essence of God were simply identical with His attributes, what would that mean for your ability to truly know Him? How would you distinguish between His knowledge and His power in your worship? Does this proof press you to admit that God is beyond your mental categories, and are you comfortable with that beyondness?
?
Sanusi's proof shows that denying distinct attributes forces the absurd conclusion that God would be a composite of multiple things. But do you ever, in your heart, treat God as if He were a collection of nice attributes rather than a single, simple, majestic Essence? Where in your life do you reduce Him to manageable concepts? Resolve today to repent of any mental reduction of God and to affirm His unfathomable unity.

The First Absurd Consequence: Contrariety Contradiction
Sanusi enumerates the first impossible consequence: the essence would be both a contrary and not a contrary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why identifying the essence with knowledge would force a contradiction between being a contrary and not being a contrary.

Core Idea
If the essence were knowledge, it would simultaneously be a contrary and not a contrary.

Sanusi begins his refutation by showing that if the essence were identical with an attribute like knowledge, it would have to be both a contrary of something (e.g., ignorance) and not a contrary, because essences themselves are not subject to contrariety. This contradiction proves the impossibility of identity.

Guided Path
1
Read the first consequence passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the first absurd consequence. Notice how Sanusi sets up the contradiction: the essence as knowledge would be contrary to ignorance, but as essence it cannot be contrary.
Contrariety (taḍād) is a property of accidental meanings, not of essences.
2
Understand the contradiction
The essence would have to be both a contrary (because it is knowledge) and not a contrary (because it is an essence). This is impossible, so the identity cannot hold.
3
Apply the same logic to other attributes
Sanusi says the same applies to power, will, and other attributes. Try to reason through one other attribute to see the pattern.
This shows the argument is general, not limited to knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا بيان بطلان التالي - وهو لزومُ أنْ تكونَ الذات عين تلك المعاني - فإليهِ أشرتُ بقولي : ( لأنَّهُ يلزمُ أنْ يُضادَّ وألا يُضادَّ . . . ) إلى آخره ؛ يعني : أنه يلزم على كونِ الذاتِ نفس المعنى لوازم كلُّها مستحيلة :
أحدها : كونُ الذاتِ ضدّاً لشيءٍ غير ضدّ له ؛ وذلكَ أَنَّ الذات إذا كانَتْ نفس العلم لزمَ أنْ تُضادَّ الجهل مثلاً ؛ لأنَّها علم ، والعلمُ يُضادُّ الجهلَ ، وألا تُضادَّهُ ؛ لأنَّها ذات ، والذاتُ لا تُضادُّ الجهل ولا غيرَه ؛ لأنَّ التضادَّ مِنْ خواص المعنى ، ولا تَتَصفُ بهِ الذواتُ ، وافهم مثل هذا في القدرة والإرادة وباقي الصفاتِ
الثاني مِنَ اللوازم : وجود المحلَّ وعدم وجودِهِ ؛ وذلكَ أَنَّ المعنى ملزوم لوجود المحلَّ ، والذات ملزومةٌ لعدمه ، فإذا كانَتِ الذاتُ نفس المعنى لزم وجود لازميهما المذكورين (۱) ؛ لاستحالة وجودِ الملزوم بدون لازمه .
الثالثُ مِنَ اللوازم : اتحاد الوجودين بل الوجوداتِ ؛ أي : صيرورتها وجوداً واحداً ؛ لأنَّ الذات إذا كانَتْ عين تلك الصفاتِ فقدِ اتَّحد وجودها بوجود تلك الصفاتِ ؛ أي : صارَ الجميع وجوداً واحداً ، وقد قدَّمنا برهان استحالة اتحاد الشيء بغيره عند استحالته في حقِّهِ تعالى (۲) ؛ وذلكَ أنَّ الشيء لوِ اتَّحد بغيرِهِ ؛ أي : صارَ معَهُ شيئاً واحداً .. لم يخلُ : إمَّا أَنْ تنعدم حقيقةُ كلِّ واحدٍ منهما ، أو تُوجَدَ ، أو تنعدم حقيقة أحدهما دونَ الآخرِ ، والأقسام كلها ممتنعة.
As for the demonstration of the falsehood of the consequent—which is the necessity that the essence be identical with those meanings—I have indicated it by my saying: 'For it would necessarily follow that it is a contrary and not a contrary...' etc.; meaning: upon the assumption that the essence is the very meaning, consequences (lawāzim) follow, all of which are impossible:
The first of them: That the essence is a contrary of something while not being a contrary of it. That is because if the essence is knowledge itself, it would necessarily be a contrary of ignorance, for example, because it is knowledge, and knowledge is contrary to ignorance, and it would not be a contrary because it is an essence, and the essence is not contrary to ignorance or anything else, because contrariety (taḍād) is among the properties of meanings, and essences are not described by it. Understand likewise concerning power, will, and the rest of the attributes.
The second of the consequences: The existence of the substrate and the non-existence of it. That is because the meaning necessitates the existence of the substrate, while the essence necessitates its non-existence. So if the essence is the very meaning, then the existence of both of their two necessaries would be necessary, because it is impossible for the necessitated (malzūm) to exist without its necessary (lāzim).
The third of the consequences: The union of the two existences, rather the existences—i.e., their becoming one existence. Because if the essence is identical with those attributes, then its existence would be united with the existence of those attributes; i.e., all would become one existence. And we have already presented the proof of the impossibility of a thing becoming united with another, given its impossibility with respect to Him, exalted (2). That is because if a thing were united with another, i.e., become one thing with it, it would not be free from the possibilities: either the reality of each one ceases to exist, or they both exist, or the reality of one ceases without the other. All these divisions are impossible.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is the refutation of the consequent that the essence would be identical with the attributes. Sanusi presents three impossible consequences (lawāzim) that would follow from such identity, proving that identity cannot hold. The first consequence: the essence would simultaneously be a contrary of something (e.g., knowledge is contrary to ignorance) and not be a contrary (because essences are not subject to contrariety). This is a contradiction. The second consequence: a meaning (like knowledge) requires a substrate (maḥall) in which it subsists, while the essence requires no substrate. If they are identical, then the substrate would both exist and not exist for the same thing—another contradiction. The third consequence: the multiple existences of the essence and the attributes would have to become one existence. But union of two things is impossible, as previously proven: either both realities are annihilated, both remain, or one is annihilated—all absurd. Thus, the original identity is impossible, proving that attributes are distinct from the essence.

Key Terms
التضاد
الذات
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if God knows, then His knowledge is just His essence, so there is no real distinction.
Correction: Sanusi shows this leads to a contradiction: the essence would have to be both a contrary and not a contrary, which is absurd.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why can the essence not be identical with knowledge?
A) Because knowledge is not an attribute of God.
B) Because the essence would then be both a contrary and not a contrary.
C) Because the essence cannot know.
D) Because knowledge requires a substrate.

Key Takeaway
The first absurd consequence shows that identity would force the essence to have opposite properties, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you have ever thought of God's attributes as simply identical to His essence, what does this logical argument reveal about your willingness to examine and correct your theological assumptions? Are you ready to embrace a more precise understanding, even if it challenges your comfort?
?
The passage argues that contradiction is impossible in theology. In what areas of your faith—beliefs about God, destiny, or worship—might you be holding onto contradictions? Will you commit to resolving them by deeper study, even if it means letting go of long-held ideas?

The Second Absurd Consequence: Substrate Existence and Non-Existence
Sanusi presents the second consequence: the substrate would both exist and not exist.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why identifying the essence with an attribute would require both the existence and non-existence of a substrate.

Core Idea
If the essence were identical with an attribute, the substrate would have to both exist and not exist.

The second impossible consequence arises from the nature of attributes requiring a substrate and the essence not requiring one. If they are identical, the same thing would both require and not require a substrate, leading to another contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the second consequence passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the second consequence. Identify the two requirements: attributes need a substrate (maḥall) to exist in, while the essence does not.
Substrate means something that supports the attribute, like a body supporting color.
2
Understand the contradiction
If essence = attribute, then the same thing would require a substrate (because it is an attribute) and not require a substrate (because it is an essence). Both cannot be true.
This is a direct logical impossibility.
3
Connect to the broader argument
This shows that the identity hypothesis leads to a necessary existence and non-existence of the same thing, which is absurd.

Source Text

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's attributes are truly distinct from His essence yet not separate, do you ever find yourself treating one attribute as if it were the whole of God, neglecting others? How can you ensure your worship engages with His full revealed nature?
?
This argument shows that denying the real distinction of attributes leads to logical impossibility. Have there been times when you have reduced God's attributes to mere 'names' or 'concepts' to avoid theological difficulty? What must you change in your understanding to submit to this truth?
?
The contradiction here is clear: a thing cannot both require and not require a substrate. Are there areas in your life where you are trying to hold together two contradictory beliefs about God without resolving them? Will you commit to studying His attributes with the same rigor as Sanusi?

The Third Absurd Consequence: Union of Existences
Sanusi presents the third consequence: the union of existences and its impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why identifying the essence with multiple attributes would force all existences to become one, which is impossible.

Core Idea
If the essence were identical with the attributes, all their existences would have to unite into one, which is absurd.

The third consequence deals with multiplicity. If the essence is identical with the attributes, then the existence of the essence and the existence of each attribute would be one and the same. But this leads to the impossibility of a thing uniting with another, as previously proven. Sanusi recalls that union would require either both realities to be annihilated or one to be annihilated, all of which are impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the third consequence passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the third consequence. Notice that it involves multiple attributes, so multiple existences would have to become one.
Sanusi says 'the union of the two existences, rather the existences', meaning all.
2
Recall the proof against union
Sanusi references an earlier proof that a thing cannot become united with another. The possibilities are all impossible: both realities vanish, both persist, or only one vanishes.
This proof is assumed from earlier in the text.
3
Apply to the identity hypothesis
If essence = attribute, then existence of essence and attribute are one. But if there are multiple attributes, all would have to be one existence, which is impossible because distinct things cannot become one without absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

According to Sanusi, what is the third impossible consequence of attribute-essence identity?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the oneness of God, have you ever unconsciously treated His attributes as separate parts that could be added together? How does this logical proof challenge that mental picture, and what must change in your understanding of tawhid as a result?
?
If the essence and attributes cannot be identical, yet they are not separate existences, how does this shape your trust that God's mercy, justice, and power are not conflicting but fully integrated? Is there a situation in your life where you struggle to see how God's attributes work together, and how does this lesson help you hold both truth and trust?
?
The proof shows that union of existences is impossible because it leads to contradictions. Are there areas in your own life where you try to unite contradictory things—like holding onto sin while claiming to love God, or mixing worldly goals with spiritual ones? What would it look like to let go of that false union and seek true tawhid in your intentions?

Three Necessary Consequences of Essence-Attribute Identity and the Proof of Impossibility of Union
Sanusi enumerates three absurd consequences if essence were identical with attributes and proves union impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three necessary consequences that follow if the divine essence were identical with the attributes, and the general proof that union of distinct things is impossible.

Core Idea
If essence and attributes were identical, contradictions would multiply — proving that union is rationally impossible.

Sanusi tackles a central problem: Are Allah's attributes identical to His essence or distinct? He argues that if they were identical, three absurd consequences follow — each leading to a contradiction. He then provides a general proof that union of two things is inconceivable. This lesson walks through each consequence and the proof that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the first consequence
Notice how Sanusi shows that if essence were identical to knowledge, it would both have to be opposite to ignorance (because knowledge is opposite) and not opposite (because essences are not opposed). This is a direct contradiction.
This applies to all attributes.
2
Read the second consequence
Here, the attribute requires a subject in which it inheres, while the essence requires no subject. If they were identical, both the existence and non-existence of a subject would be necessitated, which is impossible.
3
Read the third consequence and the proof of impossibility of union
If essence and attributes were identical, their existences would become one. Sanusi then provides a general proof that union of two things is impossible by examining three possibilities, all of which are false.
4
Read the summary and reflect
Sanusi concludes by pointing to his own poetic summary of the three consequences. Reflect on how each consequence demonstrates the rational necessity of distinguishing essence from attributes.

أحدها : كونُ الذاتِ ضدّاً لشيءٍ غير ضدّ له ؛ وذلكَ أَنَّ الذات إذا كانَتْ نفس العلم لزمَ أنْ تُضادَّ الجهل مثلاً ؛ لأنَّها علم ، والعلمُ يُضادُّ الجهلَ ، وألا تُضادَّهُ ؛ لأنَّها ذات ، والذاتُ لا تُضادُّ الجهل ولا غيرَه ؛ لأنَّ التضادَّ مِنْ خواص المعنى ، ولا تَتَصفُ بهِ الذواتُ ، وافهم مثل هذا في القدرة والإرادة وباقي الصفاتِ. الثاني مِنَ اللوازم : وجود المحلَّ وعدم وجودِهِ ؛ وذلكَ أَنَّ المعنى ملزوم لوجود المحلَّ ، والذات ملزومةٌ لعدمه ، فإذا كانَتِ الذاتُ نفس المعنى لزم وجود لازميهما المذكورين ؛ لاستحالة وجودِ الملزوم بدون لازمه . الثالثُ مِنَ اللوازم : اتحاد الوجودين بل الوجوداتِ ؛ أي : صيرورتها وجوداً واحداً ؛ لأنَّ الذات إذا كانَتْ عين تلك الصفاتِ فقدِ اتَّحد وجودها بوجود تلك الصفاتِ ؛ أي : صارَ الجميع وجوداً واحداً ، وقد قدَّمنا برهان استحالة اتحاد الشيء بغيره عند استحالته في حقِّهِ تعالى ؛ وذلكَ أنَّ الشيء لوِ اتَّحد بغيرِهِ ؛ أي : صارَ معَهُ شيئاً واحداً .. لم يخلُ : إمَّا أَنْ تنعدم حقيقةُ كلِّ واحدٍ منهما ، أو تُوجَدَ ، أو تنعدم حقيقة أحدهما دونَ الآخرِ ، والأقسام كلُّها باطلة ، فالاتحاد المقسم إليها يكون باطلا ؛ ضرورة انحصارِهِ في أقسام كلُّ واحدٍ منها باطل. أما بطلان انعدام الحقيقتينِ : فلانه يلزم أن يكون الموجود غيرهما ، واتحادهما يمنعُ مِنْ ذلكَ. وأما بطلان وجودِهما معاً : فلأنَّهُ يُوجِبُ أنْ يكونَ الموجود اثنين . والاتحادُ يُوجِبُ أن يكون الموجود واحداً لا اثنين. وأما بطلان وجودِ أحدهما دونَ الآخرِ : فلأن الاتحاد يقتضي تحقق الوجودِ لكلّ واحدٍ منهما على وجهِ لا يكونُ فيه تعدُّد ، لا عدم أحدهما وبقاء الآخر . ويلزم أيضاً على الاتحادِ في تلك الصفاتِ : اجتماع لوازمها المتنافية في شيءٍ واحدٍ ، فإنَّ بعضها يتعلَّقُ ، وبعضها لا ، وبعضها يُؤثر ، وبعضها لا ، وبعضها يُضادُّ ما لا يُضادُّهُ الآخرُ. وبالجملة : فاتحاد الشيء مع غيرِهِ ممَّا لا يُعقَلُ مطلقاً. وإلى الأوَّلِ مِنْ هذه اللوازم أشرتُ بقولي : ( لأنَّه يلزمُ أَنْ يُضادَّ وألا يُضادَّ ) . وإلى الثاني أشرتُ بقولي : ( وأن يستلزم وجود محل ولا يستلزمه ) . وإلى الثالث أشرتُ بقولي : ( وأنْ يكونَ الوجودانِ فأكثر وجوداً واحداً ) .
The first of them: that the essence should be opposite to something which is not its opposite; that is, if the essence were knowledge itself, it would necessarily be opposite to ignorance, for example, because it is knowledge, and knowledge is opposite to ignorance, and also not be opposite to it, because it is essence, and the essence is not opposite to ignorance or anything else, because opposition is among the properties of the meaning (al-maʿnā), and essences (al-dhāt) are not characterized by it. Understand the same for power, will, and the rest of the attributes. The second of the necessary consequences: the existence of the subject (al-maḥall) and the non-existence of it; that is, the meaning (al-maʿnā) necessitates the existence of the subject, while the essence necessitates its non-existence, so if the essence were the meaning itself, the two mentioned necessities would both be required, because the necessitated cannot exist without its necessary consequence. The third of the necessary consequences: the union of the two existences, indeed of the existences; that is, their becoming one existence; because if the essence were identical to those attributes, then its existence would be united with the existence of those attributes; that is, all would become one existence. And we have already presented the proof of the impossibility of the union of a thing with another, given its impossibility with respect to Him, exalted. And that is because if a thing were to unite with another—that is, become with it one thing—it would not be free from: either the reality of each of them would cease to exist, or both would exist, or the reality of one of them would cease without the other. And all these divisions are false, so the union which is divided into them must be false, because it is necessarily confined to divisions each of which is false. As for the falsity of the annihilation of both realities: it would require that what exists be other than them, and their union prevents that. As for the falsity of both existing together: it would require that what exists be two, and union requires that what exists be one, not two. As for the falsity of one existing without the other: because union necessitates the realization of existence for each of them in a manner without multiplicity, not the non-existence of one and the remaining of the other. And there also follows, upon the union regarding those attributes, the coming together of their mutually contradictory necessary consequences in one thing; for some of them are related and some are not, some are effective and some are not, and some are opposite to what the other is not opposite to. In sum: the union of a thing with another is absolutely inconceivable. And to the first of these necessary consequences I alluded by my saying: 'For it would necessarily be opposite and not be opposite.' And to the second I alluded by my saying: 'And that it would necessitate the existence of a subject and not necessitate it.' And to the third I alluded by my saying: 'And that the two existences, or more, would be one existence.'
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage is a proof by necessary consequences (lawāzim) that the divine essence (al-dhāt) cannot be identical with the attributes (al-ṣifāt). The argument proceeds by enumerating three absurd consequences that would follow if essence and attributes were the same, and then gives a general proof against the very possibility of union (ittiḥād) between distinct things.

Sanusi first states the first necessary consequence: If the essence were identical to an attribute like knowledge, it would both have to be opposite (against ignorance) and not opposite (because essences are not opposed), which is contradictory. He asks the reader to apply the same reasoning to other attributes like power and will.

The second consequence: The meaning (al-maʿnā) — here a mental consideration referring to an attribute — requires a subject (maḥall) in which it inheres, while the essence requires no subject. If they were the same, both the existence and non-existence of a subject would be necessitated, which is impossible.

The third consequence: If the essence were identical to the attributes, then their existences would become one existence. This leads to the general problem of union (ittiḥād).

Sanusi then invokes a previously established proof that union of two things is impossible. He analyzes the concept: If two things become one, either both realities (ḥaqīqatān) are annihilated, both remain, or one remains and the other is annihilated. Each case is absurd: annihilation of both yields a third thing, not the union; both remaining together yields two, not one; and one remaining with the other annihilated violates the nature of union, which requires each to exist without multiplicity.

Furthermore, if the attributes were united with the essence, contradictory properties would coexist in one thing (e.g., some attributes have effect, some not; some are opposite to what others are not), which is impossible.

Sanusi concludes that union of a thing with another is absolutely inconceivable (mā lā yuʿqalu muṭlaqan).

Finally, he points back to his own earlier poem (qawl) where he summarized each of the three consequences in condensed phrases.

ذات
صفة
تضاد
محل
معنى
لازم
اتحاد
وجود
حقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Identifying the attributes with the essence solves the problem of multiplicity.
Correction: This passage shows that identity leads to even greater absurdities, such as contradictions and impossibility of union.

Checkpoint
What is the third necessary consequence (lāzim) mentioned by Sanusi if the essence were identical to the attributes?
A) The essence would have to be opposite to something it is not opposite to.
B) The union of the two existences, making all one existence.
C) The existence and non-existence of a subject would both be required.
D) The attributes would become separate entities.

Key Takeaway
Union of the divine essence with the attributes is rationally impossible because it leads to contradictions and absurd consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the fact that union (ittiḥād) is ‘absolutely inconceivable,’ how does that affect the way you think about Allah’s oneness? Are there areas where you inadvertently imagine God’s attributes as separate parts or as fully merged without distinction?
?
The proof uses logical contradictions to protect tawhid. Have you ever felt that logic and faith are in conflict? How does this lesson challenge you to embrace rational scrutiny as a tool to deepen, rather than weaken, your conviction?
?
Sanusi states that if essence were identical to an attribute, contradictions would follow. Are there areas in your life where you try to hold together contradictory beliefs or practices, thinking they can be ‘united’ without conflict? What does this logical clarity demand of you in terms of intellectual honesty?
