The Three Impossibilities of Union
Sanusi enumerates three divisions of union and proves each invalid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible divisions of union between two realities and why each one is logically invalid.

Core Idea
Union is impossible because every way it could happen leads to a contradiction.

Sanusi employs a powerful logical tool: exhaustive division (taqsīm). He breaks down the concept of union into every conceivable scenario and demonstrates that each one is self-contradictory. This forces the conclusion that union itself is rationally impossible. Let's examine these three scenarios and see why they fail.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the three divisions
Focus on the middle section of the Arabic passage where Sanusi explains each of the three possibilities for union: both cease to exist, both exist together, or one exists without the other.
This is the heart of the proof by elimination.
2
Analyze the first division
Consider 'both cease to exist.' If both realities vanish, what remains must be something else. But union requires them to be united, so nothing else should exist. This is contradictory.
The term 'in‘idām' means non-existence.
3
Analyze the second division
Consider 'both exist together.' If both coexist as one, the result would be two things existing as one, which is impossible because union requires oneness, not twoness.
Union implies a single entity, not a pair.
4
Analyze the third division
Consider 'one exists without the other.' Union requires each to be realized in a way without multiplicity, so one cannot simply disappear while the other remains. This too is contradictory.
Union implies mutual presence, not absence of one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلُّها باطلة ، فالاتحاد المقسم إليها يكون باطلا (١) ؛ ضرورة انحصارِهِ في أقسام كلُّ واحدٍ منها باطل
أما بطلان انعدام الحقيقتينِ : فلانه يلزم أن يكون الموجود غيرهما ، واتحادهما يمنعُ مِنْ ذلكَ
وأما بطلان وجودِهما معاً : فلأنَّهُ يُوجِبُ أنْ يكونَ الموجود اثنين . والاتحادُ يُوجِبُ أن يكون الموجود واحداً لا اثنين
وأما بطلان وجودِ أحدهما دونَ الآخرِ : فلأن الاتحاد يقتضي تحقق الوجودِ لكلّ واحدٍ منهما على وجهِ لا يكونُ فيه تعدُّد ، لا عدم أحدهما وبقاء الآخر . ويلزم أيضاً على الاتحادِ في تلك الصفاتِ : اجتماع لوازمها المتنافية في شيءٍ واحدٍ ، فإنَّ بعضها يتعلَّقُ ، وبعضها لا ، وبعضها يُؤثر ، وبعضها لا ، وبعضها يُضادُّ ما لا يُضادُّهُ الآخرُ
وبالجملة : فاتحاد الشيء مع غيرِهِ ممَّا لا يُعقَلُ مطلقاً
وإلى الأوَّلِ مِنْ هذه اللوازم أشرتُ بقولي : ( لأنَّه يلزمُ أَنْ يُضادَّ وألا يُضادَّ )
وإلى الثاني أشرتُ بقولي : ( وأن يستلزم وجود محل ولا يستلزمه ) . وإلى الثالث أشرتُ بقولي : ( وأنْ يكونَ الوجودانِ فأكثر وجوداً واحداً )
All of them are invalid, so the union which is divided into them is invalid (1); due to the necessity of its being confined to divisions each of which is invalid.
As for the invalidity of the non-existence of the two realities: because it necessitates that what exists is other than them, and their union prevents that.
As for the invalidity of their existence together: because it necessitates that what exists be two. And union necessitates that what exists be one, not two.
As for the invalidity of the existence of one of them without the other: because union requires the realization of existence for each of them in a manner without multiplicity, not the absence of one and the remaining of the other. And it also necessarily follows from union in those attributes: the combination of their mutually contradictory necessary properties in one thing, for some of them relate, and some do not, and some have effect, and some do not, and some are opposite to what the other is not opposite.
In summary: the union of a thing with another is absolutely unintelligible.
And to the first of these necessary properties I referred with my saying: (because it would necessitate it to be opposite and not opposite).
And to the second I referred with my saying: (and that it entail the existence of a locus and not entail it). And to the third I referred with my saying: (and that two or more existences be one existence).
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage is a proof by elimination (taqsīm) against the possibility of union (ittiḥād) between two distinct realities. The author first states that all divisions of union are invalid, so union itself is invalid because it must fall under one of those divisions. He then details three possible divisions: (1) both realities cease to exist, (2) both exist together as one, or (3) one exists without the other. Each is shown to be impossible. For (1), if both cease to exist, then what exists would be something other than them, but union would prevent that other from existing—contradiction. For (2), if both exist together, then the resulting entity would be two, but union requires it to be one. For (3), if only one exists, then union would require both to exist without multiplicity, not one absent. Furthermore, if union were possible, the necessary properties (lawāzim) of the two attributes would coexist in one subject, which is impossible because they are contradictory (e.g., one relates and the other does not, one has effect and the other does not, one is opposite to something the other is not). The author concludes that union is absolutely unintelligible. He then refers to three key necessary properties he mentioned in his poetic lines: (a) being opposite and not opposite, (b) entailing and not entailing a locus, and (c) two or more existences becoming one.

Key Terms
الاتحاد (ittiḥād)
الانعدام (in‘idām)
الوجود (wujūd)
التقسيم (taqsīm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that union could be a combination where both realities partially cease or partially remain.
Correction: The passage shows that any conceivable scenario (both exist, one exists, or none exist) leads to contradiction, so union is impossible in all forms.

Checkpoint
Which division of union involves both realities ceasing to exist?
A) وجودهما معاً
B) انعدام الحقيقتين
C) وجود أحدهما دون الآخر
D) اجتماع اللوازم

Key Takeaway
Union between two distinct realities is impossible because each of its three logical divisions leads to a contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even subtly, blurred the line between God's attributes and those of created beings? How does this logical proof challenge you to maintain the absolute distinction between Creator and creation in your own thinking?
?
If union is impossible, what does that mean for your relationship with God? Are you seeking a closeness that respects His complete otherness, or are you trying to reduce God to something you can comprehend or merge with emotionally?
?
This passage uses pure logic to guard your tawḥīd. When you recite 'lā ilāha illā Allāh', do you truly feel the weight of its negation—that nothing can be united with God's essence? How will this understanding now shape your worship and reliance on Him alone?

The Contradictory Consequences of Union
Sanusi concludes that union leads to contradictory necessary properties and is unintelligible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why union would force contradictory properties to coexist and why this makes union unintelligible.

Core Idea
Union would combine opposites, which is rationally absurd.

Having shown the three divisions impossible, Sanusi raises an additional argument: even if union were possible, it would force mutually exclusive properties (like 'relating' and 'not relating') to exist in the same subject. He concludes that union is absolutely unintelligible. This lesson explores this deeper layer of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding part of the passage
Focus on the section starting with 'ويلزم أيضاً على الاتحاد...' up to the end. This contains the argument about contradictory necessary properties and the final conclusion.
This part reinforces the impossibility from a different angle.
2
Understand 'necessary properties' (lawāzim)
Every attribute has certain necessary consequences. For example, knowledge entails that the knower is knowing. If two different attributes were united, their contradictory consequences would have to coexist.
The examples given: some relate, some do not; some have effect, some do not; some are opposite to what others are not.
3
Grasp the final verdict
Sanusi states that 'the union of a thing with another is absolutely unintelligible' — meaning reason itself cannot conceive of it. This is the strongest conclusion.
The phrase 'مما لا يعقل مطلقاً' is emphatic.
4
Connect to the poetic references
The author refers to his own poetic lines summarizing three specific contradictions: being opposite and not opposite, entailing a locus and not entailing it, and two existences becoming one.
These are concrete examples of the contradictory lawāzim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلُّها باطلة ، فالاتحاد المقسم إليها يكون باطلا (١) ؛ ضرورة انحصارِهِ في أقسام كلُّ واحدٍ منها باطل
أما بطلان انعدام الحقيقتينِ : فلانه يلزم أن يكون الموجود غيرهما ، واتحادهما يمنعُ مِنْ ذلكَ
وأما بطلان وجودِهما معاً : فلأنَّهُ يُوجِبُ أنْ يكونَ الموجود اثنين . والاتحادُ يُوجِبُ أن يكون الموجود واحداً لا اثنين
وأما بطلان وجودِ أحدهما دونَ الآخرِ : فلأن الاتحاد يقتضي تحقق الوجودِ لكلّ واحدٍ منهما على وجهِ لا يكونُ فيه تعدُّد ، لا عدم أحدهما وبقاء الآخر . ويلزم أيضاً على الاتحادِ في تلك الصفاتِ : اجتماع لوازمها المتنافية في شيءٍ واحدٍ ، فإنَّ بعضها يتعلَّقُ ، وبعضها لا ، وبعضها يُؤثر ، وبعضها لا ، وبعضها يُضادُّ ما لا يُضادُّهُ الآخرُ

All of them are invalid, so the union which is divided into them is invalid (1); due to the necessity of its being confined to divisions each of which is invalid.
As for the invalidity of the non-existence of the two realities: because it necessitates that what exists is other than them, and their union prevents that.
As for the invalidity of their existence together: because it necessitates that what exists be two. And union necessitates that what exists be one, not two.
As for the invalidity of the existence of one of them without the other: because union requires the realization of existence for each of them in a manner without multiplicity, not the absence of one and the remaining of the other. And it also necessarily follows from union in those attributes: the combination of their mutually contradictory necessary properties in one thing, for some of them relate, and some do not, and some have effect, and some do not, and some are opposite to what the other is not opposite.
In summary: the union of a thing with another is absolutely unintelligible.
And to the first of these necessary properties I referred with my saying: (because it would necessitate it to be opposite and not opposite).
And to the second I referred with my saying: (and that it entail the existence of a locus and not entail it). And to the third I referred with my saying: (and that two or more existences be one existence).

Commentary
This passage is a proof by elimination (taqsīm) against the possibility of union (ittiḥād) between two distinct realities. The author first states that all divisions of union are invalid, so union itself is invalid because it must fall under one of those divisions. He then details three possible divisions: (1) both realities cease to exist, (2) both exist together as one, or (3) one exists without the other. Each is shown to be impossible. For (1), if both cease to exist, then what exists would be something other than them, but union would prevent that other from existing—contradiction. For (2), if both exist together, then the resulting entity would be two, but union requires it to be one. For (3), if only one exists, then union would require both to exist without multiplicity, not one absent. Furthermore, if union were possible, the necessary properties (lawāzim) of the two attributes would coexist in one subject, which is impossible because they are contradictory (e.g., one relates and the other does not, one has effect and the other does not, one is opposite to something the other is not). The author concludes that union is absolutely unintelligible. He then refers to three key necessary properties he mentioned in his poetic lines: (a) being opposite and not opposite, (b) entailing and not entailing a locus, and (c) two or more existences becoming one.

?

How does the absolute rejection of union in this lesson challenge that mental image? What can you do to purify your conception of Allah's oneness starting today?
?
The author says union is 'absolutely unintelligible.' Are there areas of your belief where you accept contradictions or unclear ideas without seeking deeper understanding? What one article of faith will you strive to understand more rationally using the tools of kalam?
?

In your daily life, where do you subtly compromise the oneness of Allah by prioritizing worldly desires or seeking approval from others? What is one concrete action you can take this week to make tawḥīd more central to your intentions?

The Sawad Halawa Issue: Proving the Impossibility of Composite Divine Attributes
Sanusi presents the sawad halawa issue as the origin for denying composite divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the debate over combining two accidental properties in one substance (sawad halawa) serves as the logical foundation for denying composite divine attributes.

Core Idea
A single essence cannot combine two distinct accidental properties without contradiction.

Sanusi begins his discussion of divine attributes by tracing it back to a logical puzzle known as 'sawad halawa' (blackness that is sweetness). This seemingly trivial problem about two accidental properties in one substance becomes the key test case for whether eternal attributes can be combined in Allah. In this passage, Sanusi presents the disagreement, offers a proof from the verifiers, and records an important objection. Let's explore how this foundation works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage is dense; do not worry about every detail yet.
2
Identify the core issue
The passage revolves around a disagreement: can two distinct accidental properties (like blackness and sweetness) inhere in one substance? Notice that the author calls this the origin of the discussion on divine attributes.
The phrase 'أصل ذلك' points to this issue as foundational.
3
Follow the proof from the verifiers
The proof is a reductio ad absurdum: if blackness is sweetness, then the resulting subject would be both opposite to whiteness (from blackness) and not opposite (from sweetness)—a contradiction. Trace this logic step by step.
This proof assumes that distinct properties have distinct relations of opposition.
4
Understand the objection about states (ahwal)
A proponent argues that the issue only applies to those who affirm 'states' as real entities. Those who deny states would see the combination as meaning two existences become one, which is also impossible. Note how this objection does not undermine the conclusion.
Even if one denies states, the combination remains impossible by another argument.
5
Apply to divine attributes
The author states that this whole reasoning applies to eternal attributes. Reflect on why this matters for Islamic theology: it prevents attributing to Allah a composite nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
1 مسألة سوادٍ حلاوة
قوله : ( وأصلُ ذلكَ : المسألة المشهورة بـ « سوادِ حلاوة » )(١) ؛ يعني : أنَّ مبنى الكلام في منع اجتماع خاصيي الصفتين أو الصفاتِ لشيء واحد .. على هذه المسألة المشهورة ؛ وذلكَ أنَّ العقلاء اختلفوا : هل يجوز أن تكونَ خاصَّيَّتا عرضين مختلفين . . ثابتتين لذاتٍ واحدةٍ كسوادٍ هو حلاوة ؛ لاجتماع خاصيتي السوادِ والحلاوة ، أم لا ؟ فالذي أحالَ ذلكَ ـ وهو الحقُّ الذي لا مرية فيه - طرده في الصفاتِ الأزلية
ودليلُ المحققينَ على إبطالِ سوادٍ حلاوة : أَنَّهُ يلزم منه ثبوتُ التضاد ونفيه على موضوع واحدٍ ؛ فإنَّ السواد لا يُضادُّ الحلاوة ، ويُضادُّ البياضَ ، والحلاوة لا تُضادُّهُ ، فإذا اجتمعَتِ الخاصيَّتَانِ لذاتِ واحدة ثبت التضاد وانتفاؤُهُ ! وذلك محال
قال المقترح : ( واعلم : أنَّ مسألة ( سوادٍ حلاوةٍ ) إنَّما تلزم على مذهب مَنْ قالَ بثبوتِ الأحوالِ ، أمَّا مَنْ نفاها وقال : أخص وصف الشيء وجوده (٢) .. فمحصول القول باجتماع خاصيتين لذات واحدةٍ أن يكون الوجودانِ وجوداً واحداً ، وذلك محال ، وهذا كله يطرد في الصفاتِ الأزليَّةِ ، فلو ثبت لشيءٍ واحدٍ خاصيَّةُ القدرةِ والعلم. . لَلَزِمَ
1 Issue of blackness [that is] sweetness
His saying: (And the origin of that is the well-known issue of 'sawad halawa') (1); meaning that the foundation of the speech about preventing the combination of the properties of two attributes or attributes for one thing is upon this well-known issue. That is because the rational minds disagreed: is it permissible for the properties of two different accidents to be established for one essence, like blackness that is sweetness, due to the combination of the properties of blackness and sweetness, or not? The one who deemed that impossible—and it is the truth about which there is no doubt—applied it to the eternal attributes.
And the proof of the verifiers for abolishing sawad halawa: that it necessitates from it the establishment of opposition and its negation upon one subject. For blackness is not opposite to sweetness, and is opposite to whiteness, and sweetness is not opposite to it. So if the two properties are combined in one essence, opposition is established and negated! And that is impossible.
The proponent said: (And know that the issue of 'sawad halawa' only applies on the doctrine of the one who affirms states (ahwal). As for the one who denies them and says: the most specific description of a thing is its existence (2)… then the product of the saying that two properties combine in one essence is that two existences become one existence, and that is impossible. And all of this applies to the eternal attributes. So if for one thing the property of power and knowledge were established… it would necessitate)
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage is a focused discussion on a specific test case known as 'sawad halawa' (blackness that is sweetness). The author explains that the entire debate about combining the properties of two attributes in one essence originates from this well-known issue. He presents the disagreement among rational thinkers: can two distinct accidental properties (like blackness and sweetness) inhere in a single substance? The correct position, which he affirms, is that it is impossible, and this impossibility is extended to the eternal divine attributes. The proof from the verifiers (al-muḥaqqiqūn) is a reductio: if blackness is sweetness, then the resulting subject would be both opposite to whiteness (because blackness is opposite) and not opposite to whiteness (because sweetness is not opposite to whiteness)—a contradiction. Then the author records an objection from a proponent (al-muqtařiḥ) who argues that this issue only applies to those who affirm 'states' (aḥwāl) as real entities; those who deny states and hold that the most specific description of a thing is its existence itself would conclude that combining two properties is simply two existences becoming one, which is also impossible. The passage ends mid-sentence as the author appears to be continuing the response, but the core argument is clear: whether one affirms states or not, combining distinct properties in one essence leads to absurdity.

Key Terms
سواد حلاوة (sawād ḥalāwa)
خاصيَّة (khāṣṣiyya)
الأحوال (aḥwāl)
التضاد (taḍādd)
الصفات الأزلية (ṣifāt azaliyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the impossibility of 'sawad halawa' only applies if one affirms real 'states' (ahwal).
Correction: The passage shows that even if one denies states, the combination is impossible because it would require two existences to become one, which is also absurd.

Checkpoint
What is the main proof given by the verifiers (al-muḥaqqiqūn) against the possibility of 'sawad halawa'?
A) It would require two existences to become one
B) It would entail both the establishment and negation of opposition on one subject
C) It is only a problem for those who affirm states
D) It contradicts the definition of accidents

Key Takeaway
The 'sawad halawa' issue establishes that combining two distinct properties in one essence leads to logical contradiction, and this principle underlies the denial of composite divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The sawad halawa argument shows that combining contradictory properties in one essence is impossible. In your own life, are there contradictory beliefs about Allah that you have not examined? Will you commit to seeking knowledge to resolve them, no matter how uncomfortable it is?
?
This passage uses rigorous logic to defend tawhid. When you think about the oneness of Allah, does your heart feel a sense of awe that drives you to study more, or do you take it for granted? What concrete step will you take this week to deepen your understanding of His attributes?
?
The debaters in this passage were willing to engage in deep rational inquiry to safeguard divine perfection. Are you willing to struggle with difficult concepts to avoid falling into error about your Creator? What excuses have you been using to avoid such study?

The Problem of Blackness-Sweetness and Its Proof
Sanusi defines the problem of blackness-sweetness and provides the proof of impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the famous problem of 'sawad halawa' and the rational proof demonstrating that two distinct accident-characteristics cannot combine in one essence.

Core Idea
Two distinct characteristics cannot coexist in one essence without contradiction.

This lesson introduces the foundational problem of blackness-sweetness, which underlies the discussion of divine attributes. The rational proof presented by the verifiers shows that combining two distinct properties leads to a logical contradiction, establishing the impossibility of such a combination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the problem and the proof.
Notice that the author first defines the problem and then gives the proof.
2
Identify the two opposing positions
Identify the disagreement among rational thinkers: some allow combining characteristics, others (the correct view) forbid it.
3
Analyze the proof structure
Trace the logical steps: if blackness and sweetness combine, then the subject both opposes whiteness (since blackness opposes it) and does not oppose whiteness (since sweetness does not oppose it) -> contradiction.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the extension to divine attributes
Note that the author explicitly extends this ruling to eternal attributes, showing its relevance to theology.
This is a key move in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وأصلُ ذلكَ : المسألة المشهورة بـ « سوادِ حلاوة » )‏؛ يعني : أنَّ مبنى الكلام في منع اجتماع خاصيي الصفتين أو الصفاتِ لشيء واحد .. على هذه المسألة المشهورة ؛ وذلكَ أنَّ العقلاء اختلفوا : هل يجوز أن تكونَ خاصَّيَّتا عرضين مختلفين . . ثابتتين لذاتٍ واحدةٍ كسوادٍ هو حلاوة ؛ لاجتماع خاصيتي السوادِ والحلاوة ، أم لا ؟ فالذي أحالَ ذلكَ ـ وهو الحقُّ الذي لا مرية فيه - طرده في الصفاتِ الأزلية • ودليلُ المحققينَ على إبطالِ سوادٍ حلاوة : أَنَّهُ يلزم منه ثبوتُ التضاد ونفيه على موضوع واحدٍ ؛ فإنَّ السواد لا يُضادُّ الحلاوة ، ويُضادُّ البياضَ ، والحلاوة لا تُضادُّهُ ، فإذا اجتمعَتِ الخاصيَّتَانِ لذاتِ واحدة ثبت التضاد وانتفاؤُهُ ! وذلك محال قال المقترح : ( واعلم : أنَّ مسألة ( سوادٍ حلاوةٍ » إنَّما تلزم على مذهب مَنْ قالَ بثبوتِ الأحوالِ ، أمَّا مَنْ نفاها وقال : أخص وصف الشيء وجوده .. فمحصول القول باجتماع خاصيتين لذات واحدةٍ أن يكون الوجودانِ وجوداً واحداً ، وذلك محال ، وهذا كله يطرد في الصفاتِ الأزليَّةِ ، فلو ثبت لشيءٍ واحدٍ خاصيَّةُ القدرةِ والعلم. . لَلَزِمَ منه أنْ يُضادَّ الجهلَ وألا يُضادَّهُ ، وذلك محال ، أو يلزمُ أنْ يكونَ الوجودانِ وجوداً واحداً ، وهو محال )‏
His statement: (And the origin of that is the famous problem of 'blackness-sweetness') means that the basis of the discussion about preventing the combination of the characteristics of two attributes or qualities for one thing is this famous problem. And that is because the rational people disagreed: Is it permissible that the characteristics of two different accidents be established for one essence, like blackness that is sweetness, due to the combination of the characteristics of blackness and sweetness, or not? So the one who deemed that impossible—and it is the truth about which there is no doubt—extended it to the eternal attributes. And the proof of the verifiers for the invalidity of blackness-sweetness is that it entails the affirmation of opposition and its negation for one subject. For blackness does not oppose sweetness, and it opposes whiteness, and sweetness does not oppose it. So if the two characteristics combine in one essence, opposition is both established and negated! And that is impossible. The proposer said: (And know that the problem of 'blackness-sweetness' only applies according to the school of those who affirm states. As for those who deny states and say: the most specific description of a thing is its existence, then the result of saying that two characteristics combine in one essence is that two existences are one existence, and that is impossible. And all of this applies to the eternal attributes. So if for one thing the characteristic of power and knowledge were established, it would necessarily entail that it opposes ignorance and does not oppose it, and that is impossible, or that the two existences become one existence, which is impossible.)
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the impossibility of the famous problem of 'sawad halawa' (blackness-sweetness). The author begins by stating that the entire discussion about preventing the combination of characteristics of two attributes for one thing is based on this problem. He then explains the disagreement among rational thinkers: can two distinct accident-properties exist in one essence, such as blackness and sweetness? The correct position, which he affirms, is that this is impossible, and this ruling is extended to the eternal attributes (i.e., the divine attributes). The proof of the verifiers (al-muhaqqiqun) is given: if two characteristics like blackness and sweetness combine in one subject, then that subject would both oppose and not oppose the same thing—because blackness opposes whiteness but not sweetness, while sweetness does not oppose whiteness. This contradictory conjunction is impossible. After this, the author quotes al-muqtarih (the proposer) who clarifies a condition: this problem only applies to those who affirm the theory of states (ahwal). For those who deny states and hold that the most specific description of a thing is its very existence, combining two characteristics would mean that two existences become one, which is also impossible. This reasoning is then applied to the eternal attributes: if power and knowledge were both attributes of God, then He would both oppose ignorance (since power opposes ignorance) and not oppose it (since knowledge does not oppose ignorance), which is absurd; or the two existences would collapse into one, which is also absurd. Thus, the impossibility of combining distinct attribute-characteristics in one essence is firmly established.

Key Terms
سواد حلاوة
خاصية
تضاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The problem of blackness-sweetness is only about physical accidents and has no relevance to divine attributes.
Correction: The author explicitly extends the ruling to eternal attributes, showing that the same logical impossibility applies to multiple distinct divine attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the proof against 'sawad halawa', what contradiction arises if blackness and sweetness combine in one subject?
A) The subject would be both black and sweet, which is impossible.
B) The subject would both oppose whiteness (since blackness opposes it) and not oppose whiteness (since sweetness does not oppose it), which is impossible.
C) The subject would have two existences.
D) The subject would be both eternal and temporal.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of combining two distinct accident-characteristics in one essence is proved by the logical contradiction that arises from their properties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held contradictory beliefs about God, thinking He has both mercy and harshness in a way that would conflict? How does this logical proof of impossibility for combining contradictory characteristics help you to purify your understanding of His attributes? Commit to examining one belief you hold about God today to ensure it is free from contradiction.
?
This proof shows that reason is necessary to avoid logical errors in faith. Do you sometimes dismiss rational arguments in religious matters, relying only on emotion or tradition? Will you accept the discipline of rational proof, even when it challenges your comfortable assumptions? Decide one area of your faith where you will apply reasoning to strengthen your conviction.
?
If a single subject cannot contain two opposing characteristics, what does that demand of your worship? Are you trying to serve both God and your desires, which oppose each other? Reflect on a specific desire that conflicts with your devotion and resolve to eliminate that contradiction in your heart.

Applying the Ruling to Divine Attributes and the Condition of States
Sanusi applies the impossibility of combining characteristics to divine attributes and explains the dependency on the theory of states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the impossibility of combining characteristics applies to divine attributes and why the argument depends on the theory of states (ahwal).

Core Idea
The impossibility of combining characteristics extends to divine attributes, but only if one affirms the theory of states.

This lesson continues the discussion by applying the previous ruling to the eternal attributes. It also clarifies that the argument depends on the school of those who affirm states; for those who deny states, the impossibility is argued differently.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the second part of the text.
Notice the introduction of 'al-muqtarih' who provides a condition.
2
Identify the two schools regarding states
The passage distinguishes between those who affirm states (ahwal) and those who deny them.
3
Understand the argument for those who affirm states
For those who affirm states, combining characteristics leads to the same contradiction as before.
4
Understand the argument for those who deny states
For those who deny states, combining characteristics means two existences become one, which is impossible.
5
Apply to divine attributes
See how the same reasoning applies to power and knowledge in God.
This shows that divine attributes cannot be multiple distinct entities.

Refuting the Deniers of Attributes: Ta'lil as Talazum
Sanusi defends divine attributes by redefining ta'lil as talazum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari theologians defend divine attributes by redefining ta'lil as necessary concomitance (talazum), thereby preserving God's necessity and avoiding any implication of contingency.

Core Idea
Ta'lil in divine attributes means necessary concomitance, not efficient causation.

This passage presents a key Ash'ari response to those who deny real divine attributes. The objectors argue that attributes would make God dependent and thus contingent. The Ash'ari response redefines the very notion of 'causation' (ta'lil) in the divine context, explaining it as necessary concomitance (talazum) rather than efficient causation. By this move, the Ash'aris preserve both the reality of attributes and God's absolute necessity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objectors' argument that if attributes exist, they would entail ta'lil, which would imply that God is contingent (possible). Follow the logic of their conditional syllogism.
The objectors assume ta'lil means efficient causation.
2
Read the Ash'ari response
The response begins by denying the conditional (man' al-istithnā'iyya) and then redefines ta'lil in the divine context.
3
Grasp the concept of talazum
Understand that talazum (necessary concomitance) does not imply one entity causing the other. It is analogous to the relationship between substance and accident among contingent beings, but here applied to two necessary entities.
Examples: God's will is concomitant with His knowledge, etc.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this redefinition preserves divine transcendence and necessity while still affirming real, distinct attributes. Note also the restriction to the divine context and the difference in the observable world.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التعليلُ في المعنوية بمعنى التلازم. قالوا: يلزم من وجودها تعليل الواجب، وذلك مستلزم جوازه. قلنا: معنى التعليل هنا التلازم، لا إفادة العلة معلولها الثبوت. احتج القائلون بنفي الصفات بأنها لو وجدت للزم تعليل الواجب، والتالي باطل، فالمقدم مثله، والملازمة ظاهرة. وأما بطلان التالي: فلأن الواجب لو علل لكان ممكناً؛ من حيث إن ثبوته حينئذ يكون مستفاداً من غيره، فيكون له العدم باعتبار ذاته؛ بمعنى أنه لو خلي وذاته لم يكن إلا معدوماً، وهو حقيقة الممكن، والإمكان ينافي الوجوب لا محالة، وأيضاً الباري جل وعلا لا يتصف بصفة ممكنة؛ فإذا كون الشيء واجباً لا يجامع كونه معللاً. أجاب أئمتنا رضي الله عنهم: بمنع الاستثنائية؛ وذلك لأن التعليل إذا أطلق في صفات الباري تعالى - على القول بثبوت الأحوال - فليس معناه إلا التلازم؛ أي: هذه الصفة الواجبة له تعالى - كالعلم مثلاً - تلازم صفة أخرى واجبة له جل وعلا تسمى حالاً؛ كالعالمية مثلاً، وليس معناه أن صفة العلم أفادت العالمية الثبوت بعد أن كانت العالمية معدومة، وإلا لزم سبق العلم على العالمية ضرورة سبق المؤثر على أثره، ويلزم أيضاً اتصافه تعالى بالحوادث، وذلك كله محال. وإذا رجع التعليل إلى معنى التلازم لم يلزم منه تأثير العلة في معلولها؛ لأن التلازم كما يعقل بين الممكنين من غير تأثير لأحدهما في الآخر؛ كالجوهر والعرض.. كذلك يعقل بين الواجبين من غير تأثير أيضاً، كما نقول: إرادته تعالى تلازم علمه، وعلمه يلازم كلامه، وعلمه يلازم عالميته على القول بأن العالمية حال ثابتة. وقس على هذا. وإلى هذا الجواب أشرت بقولي: (قلنا: معنى التعليل) إلى آخره. ونكتة التقييد بالظرف في قولي: (معنى التعليل هنا) الإشارة إلى ما لأصحابنا من الاختلاف في معنى تعليل الأحوال المعنوية في الشاهد. (وأما على القول الآخر فلا تلازم؛ إذ لا تغاير بينهما، والشيء لا يلازم نفسه، وأما على الوجه والاعتبار فكالعدم).
Ta'lil in the [divine] states is in the sense of talazum. They said: From their existence follows ta'lil of the Necessary Being, and that necessitates its contingency. We said: The meaning of ta'lil here is talazum, not the cause's bestowing existence on its effect. The proponents of negating attributes argued that if they existed, ta'lil of the Necessary Being would be entailed, and the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise false, and the implication is apparent. As for the falsity of the consequent: because if the Necessary Being were caused, it would be possible; in that its existence would then be derived from another, so it would have non-existence in its essence; meaning that if left to its essence, it would be nothing but non-existent, and that is the reality of the possible, and possibility necessarily contradicts necessity. Also, the Creator, glorious and exalted, is not described with a possible attribute; so the fact that something is necessary is incompatible with its being caused. Our imams, may God be pleased with them, answered: by denying the conditional [syllogism]; because when ta'lil is used concerning the attributes of the Most High God – according to the view that affirms states – its meaning is nothing but talazum; that is: this attribute necessary for Him – like knowledge, for example – is necessarily concomitant with another attribute necessary for Him, may He be glorified, called a state; like being-knowing, for example. And its meaning is not that the attribute of knowledge bestows the existence of knowledgeability after knowledgeability was non-existent; otherwise the priority of knowledge over knowledgeability would be entailed due to the necessity of the cause preceding its effect, and also the description of the Most High with temporal events would be entailed, and all of that is impossible. And when ta'lil reverts to the meaning of talazum, it does not entail the effect of the cause on its effect; because talazum, just as it is conceivable between two contingent beings without one affecting the other – like substance and accident – so too it is conceivable between two necessary beings without effect either, as we say: His will, exalted, is concomitant with His knowledge, and His knowledge is concomitant with His speech, and His knowledge is concomitant with His knowledgeability according to the view that knowledgeability is a subsisting state. And analogize this. And to this answer I alluded by my saying: ('We said: the meaning of ta'lil' etc.). And the point of the restriction by the circumstance in my saying: ('the meaning of ta'lil here') is the indication of what our scholars hold of difference regarding the meaning of ta'lil of the states in the observable [world]. (And as for the other view, there is no talazum; since there is no distinction between them, and a thing does not necessitate itself; and as for the aspect and consideration, it is like non-existence).
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes an objection against affirming real divine attributes (sifat). The objectors argue that if attributes exist, they would necessitate 'ta'lil' (making the Necessary Being caused), which would imply that God is possible/contingent – a clear contradiction. Sanusi explains their reasoning: if attributes exist, they would be real entities distinct from God's essence, so God's existence would derive from them, making Him dependent and thus contingent. The Ash'ari response, given by 'our imams', is to deny the conditional argument (man' al-istithnā'iyya). They do so by redefining 'ta'lil' when applied to God. In the context of divine attributes – specifically on the view that affirms 'ahwal' (states of being qualified by attribute, e.g., 'knowledgeability' as distinct from knowledge itself) – 'ta'lil' does not mean efficient causation (إفادة الثبوت) but rather 'talazum' (necessary concomitance). For example, God's knowledge necessarily accompanies His state of being-knowing (al-'ālimiyya), but that does not mean knowledge causes that state to exist; rather, they are two necessary entities that always coexist, like substance and accident among contingent beings (though without temporal priority). This understanding avoids any implication of dependence or temporality. Sanusi then notes that he alluded to this in his earlier statement 'قلنا: معنى التعليل هنا' (We said: the meaning of ta'lil here). He further explains that the phrase 'here' refers to the divine context, indicating that Ash'ari scholars differ on the meaning of ta'lil in the observable world (al-shāhid), but in the divine context it is unanimously understood as talazum. The final parenthetical note clarifies that on the contrary view (denying states), no talazum would exist because there is no distinction between the attribute and the state.

Key Terms
التعليل
التلازم
الأحوال
الصفات
الوجوب
الإمكان
منع الاستثنائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Ash'aris affirm efficient causation in the divine attributes.
Correction: In fact, they only affirm necessary concomitance (talazum), preserving divine transcendence and necessity.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari response in this passage, what does 'ta'lil' mean when applied to divine attributes?
A) Efficient causation (إفادة الثبوت)
B) Necessary concomitance (التلازم)
C) Temporal creation (الإحداث)
D) Logical implication (الاستلزام)

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari defense of attributes redefines ta'lil as talazum, avoiding any implication of contingency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on God's attributes, do you ever inadvertently imagine them as separate causes acting upon each other? How does realizing that they are only necessary concomitants reshape your understanding of God's oneness and perfection?
?
If someone told you that believing in many attributes weakens tawhid, how would the concept of talazum help you respond? Does your heart now rest assured that God's attributes do not imply need or dependence?
?
Are you willing to deeply examine your own mental images of God and purge any trace of thinking that His attributes are like created causes? What step can you take today to ensure your belief in God's attributes is pure and free from anthropomorphism?

Ta'lil as Entailment in Divine Attributes
Sanusi defines ta'lil in divine attributes as mere entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that when 'ta'lil' is applied to God's attributes, it means logical entailment (talazum) and not efficient causation.

Core Idea
In God, ta'lil is entailment, not temporal causation.

Sanusi begins by clarifying that the term 'ta'lil' in the context of divine attributes does not carry the usual sense of efficient causation. Instead, it signifies a necessary relationship of entailment between two attributes, such as knowledge and the state of being knowing. This distinction is crucial to avoid attributing temporal priority or change to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage defining ta'lil
Read the first few lines where Sanusi states that ta'lil means merely entailment (talazum).
Notice the phrase 'ليس معناه إلا التلازم'.
2
Understand why causation is rejected
Consider the impossible consequences if ta'lil implied causation: knowledge would have to precede the state, implying temporality in God.
Sanusi mentions two impossibilities: priority of cause over effect, and God being subject to temporal events.
3
Memorize the core definition
The key takeaway is that ta'lil in divine attributes is a logical, not causal, relation.

التعليلَ إذا أُطلِقَ في صفاتِ الباري تعالى - على القول بثبوتِ الأحوالِ ـ فليس معناه إلا التلازم ؛ أي : هذه الصفةُ الواجبة له تعالى ـ كالعلم مثلاً - تلازم صفةً أخرى واجبةً له جلَّ وعلا تُسمَّى حالاً ؛ كالعالميَّةِ مثلاً ، وليس معناه أنَّ صفةَ العلم أفادَتِ العالمية الثبوت بعد أنْ كانَتِ العالمية معدومةً ، وإلا لزم سبق العلم على العالمية ضرورة سبْقِ المؤثّرِ على أثرِهِ ، ويلزم أيضاً اتصافُهُ تعالى بالحوادثِ ، وذلك كلُّه محال

وإلى هذا الجواب أشرتُ بقولي : ( قلنا : معنى التعليل .
إلى آخره .
ونكتة التقييد بالظرف في قولي : ( معنى التعليل هنا ) الإشارة إلى ما لأصحابنا مِنَ الاختلافِ في معنى تعليل الأحوال المعنوية في الشاهد ؛ وذلكَ أنَّهم قد اختلفوا : إذا خلق الله تعالى في ذاتِ الجوهرِ عِلْماً مثلاً ، ولزم ذلكَ العلمَ ثبوتُ عالميَّتِهِ على القول بثبوتِ الحالِ .. فهلِ الصانعُ تعالى فعل المعنى والحال اللازمة له ، أو فعل المعنى ، والمعنى لملازمته الحال وعدم تعقلها بدونه . . هو الذي أفاد ثبوت الحال ؟
فذهبَ المُحققون إلى الأوَّلِ ، وهو الحقُّ الذي لا شكّ فيه . ومعنى التعليلِ عند هؤلاء شاهداً وغائباً : ثبوت التلازم بينهما في طرفي النفي والإثباتِ لا أزيد .
وأَمَّا مَنْ قالَ مِنَ المُتكلّمينَ : إِنَّ الفاعل يفعل المعنى ، والمعنى يُوجِبُ الحال ، ولم يفعل الفاعلُ الحالَ أصلاً . . فقوله في ذلكَ باطل قطعاً ؛ لأنَّ تلك العلَّةَ إِنْ أَثَرَتْ في ثبوتِ الحال مع التقدم .. لزم تأخُرُ المعلولِ عن علَّتِهِ بالزمان ، وهو محال ، وإِنْ أَثَرَتْ في الثبوتِ معَ مصاحبة وجودها له.. لزم عدم تقدم المؤثر على أثره ، ولزمَ التحكُمُ ؛ إذْ ليسَ إسناد وجودِ العلَّة للفاعل المختار وهي أفادَتْ ثبوت الحال .. بأَولى مِنْ إسنادِ ثبوتِ الحالِ للفاعل وهي أفادَتْ ثبوت تلكَ العلة ، بل طلب الحال للمعنى أقوى مِنْ طلبِ المعنى له ؛ لأنَّ الحال
When 'ta'lil' (causal explanation) is applied to the attributes of God Most High — according to the position affirming the existence of states (ahwal) — its meaning is nothing but entailment (talazum); that is: this necessary attribute of Him — like knowledge for example — entails another necessary attribute of Him, called a state (hal), such as 'being knowing' for example. And it does not mean that the attribute of knowledge brings about existence of 'being knowing' after 'being knowing' had been non-existent; otherwise the priority of knowledge over 'being knowing' would follow necessarily from the priority of the cause over its effect, and also His being qualified by temporal events would follow, and all that is impossible. And if ta'lil returns to the meaning of entailment, then no causation of the cause in its effect follows from it; because entailment, just as it is understood between two contingent things without one affecting the other — like substance and accident — likewise it is understood between two necessary things also without any effect, as we say: His will entails His knowledge, and His knowledge entails His speech, and His knowledge entails His 'being knowing' according to the position that 'being knowing' is a subsisting state. And analogize to this. And to this answer I referred by my saying: 'We said: the meaning of ta'lil... etc.' And the point of the temporal restriction in my saying 'the meaning of ta'lil here' is to indicate the difference of opinion among our scholars concerning the meaning of ta'lil of the states (ahwal) in the witnessed realm; for they differed: when God Most High creates in the substance itself, for example, knowledge, and the subsistence of its 'being knowing' necessarily follows from that knowledge according to the position affirming the state — does the Creator Most High do the attribute (ma'na) and the state (hal) necessary for it, or does He do the attribute, and the attribute, due to its entailment of the state and the inconceivability of the state without it, is what brings about the subsistence of the state? The verifiers (muhaqqiqun) went for the first, which is the truth about which there is no doubt. And the meaning of ta'lil according to them, in the witnessed and the unseen, is the subsistence of entailment between the two in the aspects of negation and affirmation, nothing more. As for whoever among the mutakallimun said: 'The agent does the attribute, and the attribute necessitates the state, and the agent does not do the state at all' — his saying in that is absolutely false; because if that cause produced an effect in the subsistence of the state while being prior, then the effect's being posterior in time to its cause would follow, which is impossible; and if it produced an effect in the subsistence while accompanying its existence, then the lack of priority of the cause over its effect would follow, and arbitrariness would follow; since attributing the existence of the cause to the free agent while it brought about the subsistence of the state is not more appropriate than attributing the subsistence of the state to the agent while it brought about the subsistence of that cause. Rather, the state's need for the attribute is stronger than the attribute's need for it, because the state...
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument about the meaning of 'ta'lil' (causal explanation) when predicated of God's attributes, specifically within the framework of the theory of states (ahwal). The author, Sanusi, begins by clarifying that when ta'lil is used for God's attributes, it does not imply efficient causation or temporal priority. Instead, it means pure entailment (talazum): a necessary attribute of God, like knowledge, entails another necessary attribute called a state, such as 'being knowing'. This is not to say that knowledge produces 'being knowing' after the latter was non-existent, because that would require the cause to precede the effect in time, making God subject to temporal accidents, which is impossible. If ta'lil is understood as entailment, then no causal influence (ta'thir) is involved. Entailment can hold between two necessary things just as it holds between two contingent things like substance and accident without one acting on the other. For example, God's will entails His knowledge, His knowledge entails His speech, and His knowledge entails His being knowing (on the view that being knowing is a subsisting state). The author then notes that he referred to this answer in his earlier statement 'We said: the meaning of ta'lil...'. Next, he introduces a controversy among the mutakallimun concerning the meaning of ta'lil of states in the witnessed world (al-shahid). The question is: when God creates knowledge in a substance, and the state of 'being knowing' necessarily follows, does God create both the attribute (ma'na) and the state (hal), or does He create only the attribute, and the attribute itself brings about the state? Sanusi reports that the 'verifiers' (muhaqqiqun) adopt the first position, which is the truth. For them, ta'lil in both the witnessed and unseen realms is nothing but the subsistence of entailment between the two in both affirmation and negation. He then refutes the second position, which claims that the agent (God) does the attribute and the attribute necessitates the state without the agent doing the state. This is false for two reasons: if the cause (the attribute) produces the state while being prior in time (temporal priority), that would make the state temporally posterior to its cause, which is impossible because it would ascribe temporal sequence to God's actions. If instead the cause accompanies the state without temporal priority, then the cause is not prior to its effect — but this would imply arbitrary assignment of causality without reason, because there is no basis to attribute the existence of the state to the cause rather than to the agent directly. In fact, the state's dependence on the attribute is stronger than the attribute's need for the state, but the argument is cut off at the end of the page.

Key Terms
التعليل (ta'lil)
التلازم (talazum)
الحال (hal)
العالمية (al-`alamiyya)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ta'lil in God implies a causal chain with temporal priority.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies it means only logical entailment, not causation.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what does 'ta'lil' mean when applied to God's attributes on the theory of states?
A) Efficient causation
B) Entailment without temporality
C) Creation from nothing
D) Anthropomorphic attribution

Key Takeaway
Ta'lil in divine attributes is a relation of entailment, not causal production.

How does Sanusi's explanation that it is pure entailment challenge you to rethink your mental picture of God? What would it mean for your trust in God's unchanging nature if you corrected this?

Entailment Without Causation in Both Contingent and Necessary Realms
Sanusi argues that entailment between divine attributes involves no causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that entailment (talazum) can exist between two entities without one affecting the other, and this principle applies to both contingent and necessary attributes.

Core Idea
Entailment does not require causation; it holds even between necessary attributes.

In the previous lesson, we learned that ta'lil in God's attributes is entailment, not causation. Sanusi now reinforces this by drawing an analogy: just as entailment holds between contingent things like substance and accident without causation, it likewise holds between necessary attributes of God. This prevents any misunderstanding that entailment implies causal influence.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy
Read the passage where Sanusi compares entailment between contingent things (substance/accident) to entailment between necessary things (God's attributes).
Note the phrase 'كما يُعقَلُ بينَ الممكنينِ'.
2
Identify the examples
He gives concrete examples: His will entails His knowledge, knowledge entails speech, etc.
This shows the range of entailment relations.
3
Understand the conclusion
Conclude that entailment does not imply one entity being the creator/cause of the other.
Sanusi says 'من غير تأثير لأحدهما في الآخر'.

ونكتة التقييد بالظرف في قولي : ( معنى التعليل هنا ) الإشارة إلى ما لأصحابنا مِنَ الاختلافِ في معنى تعليل الأحوال المعنوية في الشاهد ؛ وذلكَ أنَّهم قد اختلفوا : إذا خلق الله تعالى في ذاتِ الجوهرِ عِلْماً مثلاً ، ولزم ذلكَ العلمَ ثبوتُ عالميَّتِهِ على القول بثبوتِ الحالِ .. فهلِ الصانعُ تعالى فعل المعنى والحال اللازمة له ، أو فعل المعنى ، والمعنى لملازمته الحال وعدم تعقلها بدونه . . هو الذي أفاد ثبوت الحال ؟
فذهبَ المُحققون إلى الأوَّلِ ، وهو الحقُّ الذي لا شكّ فيه . ومعنى التعليلِ عند هؤلاء شاهداً وغائباً : ثبوت التلازم بينهما في طرفي النفي والإثباتِ لا أزيد .
وأَمَّا مَنْ قالَ مِنَ المُتكلّمينَ : إِنَّ الفاعل يفعل المعنى ، والمعنى يُوجِبُ الحال ، ولم يفعل الفاعلُ الحالَ أصلاً . . فقوله في ذلكَ باطل قطعاً ؛ لأنَّ تلك العلَّةَ إِنْ أَثَرَتْ في ثبوتِ الحال مع التقدم .. لزم تأخُرُ المعلولِ عن علَّتِهِ بالزمان ، وهو محال ، وإِنْ أَثَرَتْ في الثبوتِ معَ مصاحبة وجودها له.. لزم عدم تقدم المؤثر على أثره ، ولزمَ التحكُمُ ؛ إذْ ليسَ إسناد وجودِ العلَّة للفاعل المختار وهي أفادَتْ ثبوت الحال .. بأَولى مِنْ إسنادِ ثبوتِ الحالِ للفاعل وهي أفادَتْ ثبوت تلكَ العلة ، بل طلب الحال للمعنى أقوى مِنْ طلبِ المعنى له ؛ لأنَّ الحال
When 'ta'lil' (causal explanation) is applied to the attributes of God Most High — according to the position affirming the existence of states (ahwal) — its meaning is nothing but entailment (talazum); that is: this necessary attribute of Him — like knowledge for example — entails another necessary attribute of Him, called a state (hal), such as 'being knowing' for example. And it does not mean that the attribute of knowledge brings about existence of 'being knowing' after 'being knowing' had been non-existent; otherwise the priority of knowledge over 'being knowing' would follow necessarily from the priority of the cause over its effect, and also His being qualified by temporal events would follow, and all that is impossible. And if ta'lil returns to the meaning of entailment, then no causation of the cause in its effect follows from it; because entailment, just as it is understood between two contingent things without one affecting the other — like substance and accident — likewise it is understood between two necessary things also without any effect, as we say: His will entails His knowledge, and His knowledge entails His speech, and His knowledge entails His 'being knowing' according to the position that 'being knowing' is a subsisting state. And analogize to this. And to this answer I referred by my saying: 'We said: the meaning of ta'lil... etc.' And the point of the temporal restriction in my saying 'the meaning of ta'lil here' is to indicate the difference of opinion among our scholars concerning the meaning of ta'lil of the states (ahwal) in the witnessed realm; for they differed: when God Most High creates in the substance itself, for example, knowledge, and the subsistence of its 'being knowing' necessarily follows from that knowledge according to the position affirming the state — does the Creator Most High do the attribute (ma'na) and the state (hal) necessary for it, or does He do the attribute, and the attribute, due to its entailment of the state and the inconceivability of the state without it, is what brings about the subsistence of the state? The verifiers (muhaqqiqun) went for the first, which is the truth about which there is no doubt. And the meaning of ta'lil according to them, in the witnessed and the unseen, is the subsistence of entailment between the two in the aspects of negation and affirmation, nothing more. As for whoever among the mutakallimun said: 'The agent does the attribute, and the attribute necessitates the state, and the agent does not do the state at all' — his saying in that is absolutely false; because if that cause produced an effect in the subsistence of the state while being prior, then the effect's being posterior in time to its cause would follow, which is impossible; and if it produced an effect in the subsistence while accompanying its existence, then the lack of priority of the cause over its effect would follow, and arbitrariness would follow; since attributing the existence of the cause to the free agent while it brought about the subsistence of the state is not more appropriate than attributing the subsistence of the state to the agent while it brought about the subsistence of that cause. Rather, the state's need for the attribute is stronger than the attribute's need for it, because the state...
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument about the meaning of 'ta'lil' (causal explanation) when predicated of God's attributes, specifically within the framework of the theory of states (ahwal). The author, Sanusi, begins by clarifying that when ta'lil is used for God's attributes, it does not imply efficient causation or temporal priority. Instead, it means pure entailment (talazum): a necessary attribute of God, like knowledge, entails another necessary attribute called a state, such as 'being knowing'. This is not to say that knowledge produces 'being knowing' after the latter was non-existent, because that would require the cause to precede the effect in time, making God subject to temporal accidents, which is impossible. If ta'lil is understood as entailment, then no causal influence (ta'thir) is involved. Entailment can hold between two necessary things just as it holds between two contingent things like substance and accident without one acting on the other. For example, God's will entails His knowledge, His knowledge entails His speech, and His knowledge entails His being knowing (on the view that being knowing is a subsisting state). The author then notes that he referred to this answer in his earlier statement 'We said: the meaning of ta'lil...'. Next, he introduces a controversy among the mutakallimun concerning the meaning of ta'lil of states in the witnessed world (al-shahid). The question is: when God creates knowledge in a substance, and the state of 'being knowing' necessarily follows, does God create both the attribute (ma'na) and the state (hal), or does He create only the attribute, and the attribute itself brings about the state? Sanusi reports that the 'verifiers' (muhaqqiqun) adopt the first position, which is the truth. For them, ta'lil in both the witnessed and unseen realms is nothing but the subsistence of entailment between the two in both affirmation and negation. He then refutes the second position, which claims that the agent (God) does the attribute and the attribute necessitates the state without the agent doing the state. This is false for two reasons: if the cause (the attribute) produces the state while being prior in time (temporal priority), that would make the state temporally posterior to its cause, which is impossible because it would ascribe temporal sequence to God's actions. If instead the cause accompanies the state without temporal priority, then the cause is not prior to its effect — but this would imply arbitrary assignment of causality without reason, because there is no basis to attribute the existence of the state to the cause rather than to the agent directly. In fact, the state's dependence on the attribute is stronger than the attribute's need for the state, but the argument is cut off at the end of the page.

Key Terms
الممكن (mumkin)
الواجب (wajib)
الجوهر (jawhar)
العرض (`arad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Entailment always implies one thing is the cause of another.
Correction: Sanusi shows entailment can exist without causation, as in substance and accident.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, can entailment exist without causation?
A) No, causation is always implied.
B) Yes, as between substance and accident.
C) Only in the contingent realm.
D) Only in the divine realm.

Key Takeaway
Entailment is a broader relation than causation; it can exist between two things without one causing the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself imagining that God's knowledge 'causes' His speech, as if there were a sequence in the Divine? How does Sanusi's argument challenge that picture? Will you consciously remind yourself that Allah's attributes are not causally separate?
?
If entailment does not require causation even among necessary attributes, what does that reveal about the absolute oneness and simplicity of Allah? How should that truth change the way you relate to Him in worship today?
?
Think of a relationship in your life where you confuse correlation with causation. How does the lesson's example of substance and accident help you see the difference? Will you apply this insight to avoid false causal assumptions about Allah?

The Controversy Over Ta'lil of States in the Witnessed Realm
Sanusi presents and resolves a dispute about the role of the attribute and state in creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate among mutakallimun about whether God creates both the attribute and the state or just the attribute, and the correct position according to Sanusi.

Core Idea
God creates both the attribute and the state; the idea that the attribute alone necessitates the state is false.

After establishing that ta'lil means entailment, Sanusi turns to a specific dispute within the school: when God creates an attribute like knowledge in a substance, does He also create the state (e.g., being knowing) that necessarily attaches, or does the attribute itself necessitate the state without divine creation? The verifiers affirm that God creates both, and Sanusi refutes the opposing view.

Guided Path
1
Read the dispute description
Read the passage where Sanusi states the controversy: does God create both the attribute and the state, or just the attribute?
Notice the two positions.
2
Identify the verifiers' position
Sanusi says the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) hold that God creates both, and that ta'lil is merely entailment.
3
Understand the refutation
Read the argument against the second position: if the attribute necessitates the state, either temporal sequence or arbitrariness follows.

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument about the meaning of 'ta'lil' (causal explanation) when predicated of God's attributes, specifically within the framework of the theory of states (ahwal). Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some say God creates only the attribute, and the attribute itself creates the state.
Correction: Sanusi says this is absolutely false; God creates both the attribute and the state.

Checkpoint
According to the verifiers (muhaqqiqun), what does God create in the example of knowledge and the state of being knowing?
A) Only the attribute (knowledge)
B) Only the state (being knowing)
C) Both the attribute and the state
D) Neither, as they are eternal

Key Takeaway
The correct position is that God creates both the attribute and its entailed state; the attribute does not independently necessitate the state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, you naturally assume that causes produce their effects: fire burns, rain waters. But this lesson insists that even the relationship between an attribute and its state is directly created by Allah. When you look at a cause and effect in your own life—like studying leading to knowledge—do you ever stop to realize that Allah is the one who creates both the studying and the knowledge? How can you train your heart to see His hand in every link of causation, rather than assuming the link is automatic?
?
The opponents’ view is called 'arbitrary' (tahakkum) because attributing the state to the attribute rather than to God is without basis. Is there an area in your life where you have been attributing outcomes to secondary causes—your effort, your skills, other people—while forgetting that Allah is the sole creator of both the cause and the effect? What would change in your attitude if you truly believed that every state you experience is directly created by Him?
?
Sanusi says the correct understanding of ta'lil is 'merely the subsistence of entailment between them, nothing more.' Do you ever try to explain God's actions by thinking that something in His creation simply 'must' happen because of some inherent property? This lesson challenges that. In what specific situation do you need to stop saying 'it just naturally happens' and instead say 'Allah creates it'? Make a concrete resolve today to replace one such habitual explanation with a direct acknowledgment of His creation.

Refuting the Objection: Origin Does Not Imply Causation
Sanusi refutes the objection that being an origin grounds causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why being an 'origin' (aṣl) does not automatically make something a cause, and how this relates to the necessary attributes of Allah.

Core Idea
Being an origin does not make something a cause; true effecting requires divine attributes.

Sanusi now tackles an objection: some argue that a cause is preferred because it is an origin (aṣl) for its effect. He refutes this by showing there is no necessary connection between being an origin and being an effecter. The argument leads to a deeper point about the unique causation belonging to Allah's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the objection and refutation
Find where Sanusi presents the objection that the cause is preferred because it is an origin. Then locate his first counter-argument: there is no necessary connection between being an origin and being an effecter.
The refutation begins with 'قيل لهم'.
3
Understand the reductio argument
Sanusi then uses a reductio ad absurdum: if being an origin implied independence in causing, then Allah would only create substances and they would independently cause accidents, which is false. Reflect on why this is absurd.
4
Grasp the final distinction
Read the concluding part where Sanusi clarifies that the objection would only apply to created attributes, not to Allah's necessary attributes. For divine attributes, causation only means concomitance (talāzum).
This is the key theological point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا تُعقَلُ متميّزةً إلا باعتبار معناها ، بخلافِ العكس
فإن أجابوا بترجيح العلة للتأثير لكونها أصلاً .. قيل لهم : لا ملازمة بين كون الشيء أصلاً وكونِهِ مُؤثراً ، وإِنَّما يصح التأثيرُ لِمَنْ وجبَتْ لهُ صفاتُ الألوهيَّة ؛ مِنْ كمال العلم والقدرة والإرادة والحياة والوحدانية إلى غير ذلكَ مِنَ الصفاتِ التي لا تليقُ إلا بالله جلَّ وعلا ، ولو كانَ كونُ الشيء أصلاً لغيره يقتضي استقلاله بإثباتِ غيرِهِ له .. لَلزِمَ أنْ يكونَ تعالى إنَّما أوجد الجواهر وهي تستقلُّ الملازم بإيجاد الأعراض ! وذلك معلوم البطلان
•
وبالجملة : فهذا القولُ باطل ، وعلى تقديرِ صحتِهِ فإنَّما يصح باعتبار صفاتِنا الحادثة هي وأحوالها ، فأمكنَ إِسنادُها إلى مُؤثرٍ ، وأَمَّا صفاته جل وعلا فكلُّها واجبةٌ ، والواجبُ مِنْ لازمه وجوبُ القدمِ والبقاء ؛ إذ الوجوبُ نفْيُّ قَبولِ الانتفاء ، وما لا يقبلُ الانتفاء فلا انتفاء له سابقاً ولا لاحقاً ، وفي ذلك تحقيق قدمِهِ وبقائه ، فلم يصح إسنادُهُ لمقتض أصلاً ، فلا معنى للتعليلِ إِنْ أُطلِقَ فيها إلا التلازم .
It is not understood as distinct except by consideration of its meaning, contrary to the opposite. For if they answer by preferring the cause for effect because it is an origin, it is said to them: There is no necessary connection between a thing being an origin and it being an effecter. Rather, effecting is valid only for one whose attributes of divinity are necessary: perfect knowledge, power, will, life, and oneness, and other attributes that befit only Allah, Exalted and Mighty. And if a thing being an origin for another required its independence in establishing that other, then it would necessarily follow that Allah, Exalted, only brought into existence the substances while they independently necessitate the accidents! And that is known to be false. In summary: This statement is false, and if it were assumed correct, it would only be valid with regard to our created attributes and their states, so their attribution to an effecter is possible. As for His attributes, Exalted and Mighty, they are all necessary. The necessary entails by necessity the necessity of pre-eternity and permanence, for necessity is the negation of the possibility of non-existence. And whatever does not admit non-existence has no non-existence prior or subsequent. In that is the verification of His pre-eternity and permanence. So their attribution to a ground is not valid at all. Hence there is no meaning for causation (al-ta'līl) if it is applied to them except concomitance (al-talāzum).
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection regarding the meaning of causation (ta'līl). It begins by stating a principle: a thing is not understood as distinct except by its meaning. Then it presents the objection: some might argue that the cause is preferred for effect because it is an origin (aṣl). The refutation follows: there is no necessary connection between being an origin and being an effecter. True effecting requires the attributes of divinity—knowledge, power, will, life, oneness, etc. If being an origin implied independence in establishing the other, then it would absurdly imply that substances independently cause accidents, which is false. The passage then concludes that this claim is false, and if it were true, it would only apply to created attributes, not to God's attributes, which are necessary. Necessity negates any possibility of non-existence, so divine attributes cannot be caused or grounded; any talk of causation in this context only means concomitance (talāzum).

Key Terms
علة
تأثير
أصل
ملازمة
صفات الألوهية
وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that being an origin (aṣl) automatically makes something a cause.
Correction: The text refutes this by showing that effecting requires divine attributes and that being an origin does not imply a necessary connection to causing.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the claim that being an origin (aṣl) suffices for being an effecter (mu'aththir)?
A) Because an origin is always independent and does not need anything else.
B) Because true effecting requires divine attributes, and mere origin does not imply effecting.
C) Because the objector is a Mu'tazilite and their view is rejected by consensus.
D) Because the cause must be temporal, but God's attributes are eternal.

Key Takeaway
Being an origin does not entail being a cause; true causation is exclusive to the necessary divine attributes, and for Allah's attributes, causation only means concomitance.

?
This lesson distinguishes between created and divine attributes, showing that Allah's attributes are necessary and cannot be caused. What specific step will you take to deepen your awe of His self-subsistent and eternal nature?

Does your heart truly find rest in the fact that His attributes are beyond any external cause? If not, what belief do you need to uproot today?

The Consensus That Ta'lil in Divine Attributes is Invalid
Sanusi concludes that ta'lil in God's attributes is invalid by consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why ta'lil (causation) in God's attributes is invalid by consensus and how the two views on the meaning of ta'lil both lead to the same conclusion that God's attributes are necessary, not possible.

Core Idea
Ta'lil in God's attributes is invalid by consensus—no attribute came into being after non-existence.

This passage from Sanusi addresses a key dispute: can we speak of causation (ta'lil) in God's attributes? Al-Muqtarih presents two views on what ta'lil means—concomitance or necessitation—and shows that neither implies that God's attributes are possible beings. The conclusion is clear: ta'lil in the sense of bringing into existence after non-existence is unanimously rejected for God's attributes. The only remaining debate is merely verbal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two meanings of ta'lil
Read the passage and identify the two views on the meaning of causation (ta'lil): one as concomitance (talazum) and the other as necessitation (that the meaning necessitates the judgment). Notice how each view is defended.
The first view allows both possibles and necessaries to be concomitant; the second view argues that the judgment is necessary only by virtue of its meaning.
2
Follow the argument that possibility (imkan) does not apply
The passage summarizes al-Muqtarih's answer: the possibility alleged by the Mu'tazila does not follow in either view. Understand why: the possible admits non-existence by itself, but God's judgments do not. Under the second view, the attributes' meanings are necessary, so the attributes share one essence with them, making them not possible.
This part is crucial: it refutes the Mu'tazilite objection that causation would imply possibility in God.
3
Grasp the final consensus and the remaining verbal dispute
Read the final conclusion: causation (ta'lil) in God's attributes, in the sense of providing existence after non-existence, is invalid by consensus of Ahl al-Sunna and even all Muslims. The only remaining dispute is over the permissibility of using the term 'ta'lil' verbally.
Understand the scope: the consensus is on the meaning, not the word.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وظاهرُ كلامِ المُقترح : أَنَّ الخلاف جارٍ أيضاً في تعليل الواجب ؛ فإنَّه قد قال في تقريرِ الجواب عن شبهةِ المعتزلة التي سبق تقريرها : ( مَنْ قالَ بأنَّ التعليلَ معناه التلازم . . يقولُ : قد يتلازم الممكنانِ وقد يتلازم الواجبانِ ، ولا منافاةَ ، ومَنْ قالَ بأنَّ المعنى يُوجِبُ .. قالَ : الحكم لا يجب إلا باعتبارِ وجوب معناه ، فإِنَّا قلنا : إِنَّهُ لا يُعقَلُ متميّزاً إلا باعتباره ، ولا يثبتُ فيهِ اختلاف ولا تماثل باعتبار معقوليَّتِهِ ، وإنَّما يثبتُ فيهِ ذلكَ باعتبار معناه الموجب له ، فكيف يُنفَى ما باعتبارِهِ وجبَ ؟! ( انتهى )
وحاصل جوابِهِ : أَنَّ الإمكانَ الذي ألزمَتْهُ المعتزلة في تعليل الأحكام الواجبة لا يلزم على كلا المذهبين في معنى التعليل ؛ لأنَّ الممكن هو الذي يقبلُ العدمَ لذاتِهِ ، وأحكام الباري تعالى لا تقبلُ العدم لذاتها ، أمَّا على القولِ الأَوَّلِ في معنى التعليل فظاهر ، وأمَّا على الثاني فلمَّا كانَتْ لا تُعقَلُ إلا بمعانيها فوجوب معانيها وجوب لها ، وكأنَّها معها ذات واحدةٌ ؛ إذْ لا ذاتَ للأحوالِ متميّزةً حتى يُقال : إنَّها تقبل العدمَ في ذاتِها ، وإنَّما استفادَتِ الوجوبَ مِنْ غيرِها ، فتكون ممكنة .
والحاصل : أنَّ التعليلَ في صفاتِهِ تعالى بمعنى إفادة الإثباتِ عن عدم .. لا يصح بإجماع أهل السنَّةِ ، بل بإجماع المسلمين ، وبقي النزاع في مُجرَّدِ إطلاق لفظي
And the apparent meaning of the speech of al-Muqtarih: that the dispute also applies to causation (ta'līl) of the Necessary. For he said in the exposition of the answer to the doubt of the Mu'tazila which was previously presented: 'Whoever says that causation (al-ta'līl) means concomitance (al-talāzum) says: two possibles may be concomitant and two necessaries may be concomitant, and there is no contradiction. And whoever says that the meaning necessitates says: the judgment is not necessary except by consideration of the necessity of its meaning. For we said: it is not understood as distinct except by consideration of it, and neither difference nor similarity is established in it by consideration of its intelligibility; rather, that is established in it by consideration of its meaning that necessitates it. So how can what is necessary by virtue of it be negated?' (end). The summary of his answer: the possibility (al-imkān) that the Mu'tazila alleged in the causation of necessary judgments does not follow in either of the two doctrines on the meaning of causation. For the possible is that which admits non-existence by itself, and the judgments of the Creator, Exalted, do not admit non-existence by themselves. As for the first doctrine on the meaning of causation, it is obvious. As for the second, since they are not understood except by their meanings, the necessity of their meanings is necessity for them, and it is as if they are one essence with them. For there is no essence of the states (al-aḥwāl) as distinct such that it could be said they admit non-existence in themselves. Rather, they acquire necessity from other than themselves, so they would be possible. The conclusion: causation (al-ta'līl) in His attributes, Exalted, in the sense of providing existence after non-existence is not valid by consensus of Ahl al-Sunna, rather by consensus of the Muslims, and the dispute remains over merely verbal usage.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage addresses whether the dispute over causation (ta'līl) extends to the Necessary Being's attributes. It first quotes al-Muqtarih, who explains the two views on the meaning of causation. The first view holds that causation means concomitance (talāzum)—two possibles or two necessaries can be concomitant without contradiction. The second view holds that the meaning necessitates the judgment, arguing that the judgment is necessary only by virtue of its meaning's necessity. Al-Muqtarih then asks: how can what is necessary by virtue of its meaning be negated? Next, the passage summarizes the answer: the possibility alleged by the Mu'tazila does not apply in either view because the possible admits non-existence by itself, while God's judgments do not admit non-existence by themselves. Under the second view, the attributes' meanings are necessary, so the attributes share one essence with those meanings; thus, they are not possible. The final conclusion is that causation in God's attributes, in the sense of bringing into existence after non-existence, is invalid by consensus of Ahl al-Sunna and even all Muslims. The only remaining dispute is over the permissibility of verbal usage of the term 'causation'.
