إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that causation (ta'lil) can be applied to God's attributes in the same sense as to created things, implying that attributes could be possible or originated.
Correction: The text asserts that ta'lil in the sense of bringing into existence after non-existence is invalid by consensus, because God's attributes are necessary and do not admit non-existence.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion regarding causation (ta'līl) in God's attributes?
A) It is valid only if it means concomitance.
B) It is valid by consensus of the Mu'tazila.
C) It is not valid in the sense of providing existence after non-existence by consensus of Ahl al-Sunna and all Muslims.
D) It is valid only for the attributes of action.

Key Takeaway
Ta'lil in God's attributes (meaning bringing into existence after non-existence) is invalid by consensus of Ahl al-Sunna and all Muslims; only verbal usage of the term remains disputed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage declares a consensus that ta'lil in God's attributes is invalid. When you think about God's mercy or power, do you ever unconsciously imagine them as something that came to be? How might this passage reshape your very mental image of the Divine?
?
If only a verbal dispute remains over the term 'ta'lil', then what about arguments you may have had on this topic? Were they rooted in substance or mere words? Will you seek clarity before entering such debates from now on?
?
The Mu'tazila's error was to ascribe possibility to divine attributes. Examine your own heart: do you treat any of God's attributes as if they were contingent or subject to change? What concrete step will you take today to affirm their eternal necessity in your worship?

Response to Mu'tazila on Causality and Possibility
Sanusi responds to the Mu'tazila on possibility and causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concept of possibility (imkan) does not apply to divine attributes according to Sanusi, and how this refutes the Mu'tazila argument from causation.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are necessary by themselves, not possible.

This passage addresses a key Mu'tazili objection: if divine attributes are caused (mu'allala), they must be possible (contingent). Sanusi refutes this by clarifying that possibility applies only to what can admit non-existence, but divine attributes cannot. The response examines two Sunni views on causation and concludes that any wording suggesting temporality or deficiency in Allah is prohibited.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: a quotation from a commentary followed by Sanusi's summary response.
The first part (ending with 'انتهى') is a quotation; the main response begins at 'وحاصل جوابه'.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila argument
The Mu'tazila argued that if divine attributes are caused, they must be possible (i.e., contingent). Grasp the logic: causation implies possibility.
Possibility (imkan) is defined as 'admitting non-existence by its essence'.
3
Analyze Sanusi's response
Sanusi denies that divine judgments admit non-existence. He then applies this to both views on the meaning of causation (ta'lil). For the first view, it's obvious. For the second view (the theory of states/ahwal), he explains that the necessity of the meanings makes the attributes necessary.
The key is that states (ahwal) have no independent essence, so they cannot be said to admit non-existence.
4
Conclude with the final ruling
Sanusi states that ta'lil in the sense of bringing into existence from non-existence is rejected by consensus. The remaining dispute is merely verbal. The truth is to prohibit any expression implying temporality or deficiency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُتميّزاً إلا باعتباره، ولا يثبتُ فيهِ اختلاف ولا تماثل باعتبار معقوليَّتِهِ، وإنَّما يثبتُ فيهِ ذلكَ باعتبار معناه الموجب له، فكيف يُنفَى ما باعتبارِهِ وجبَ؟! (انتهى). وحاصل جوابِهِ: أَنَّ الإمكانَ الذي ألزمَتْهُ المعتزلة في تعليل الأحكام الواجبة لا يلزم على كلا المذهبين في معنى التعليل؛ لأنَّ الممكن هو الذي يقبلُ العدمَ لذاتِهِ، وأحكام الباري تعالى لا تقبلُ العدم لذاتها، أمَّا على القولِ الأَوَّلِ في معنى التعليل فظاهر، وأمَّا على الثاني فلمَّا كانَتْ لا تُعقَلُ إلا بمعانيها فوجوب معانيها وجوب لها، وكأنَّها معها ذات واحدةٌ؛ إذْ لا ذاتَ للأحوالِ متميّزةً حتى يُقال: إنَّها تقبل العدمَ في ذاتِها، وإنَّما استفادَتِ الوجوبَ مِنْ غيرِها، فتكون ممكنة. والحاصل: أنَّ التعليلَ في صفاتِهِ تعالى بمعنى إفادة الإثباتِ عن عدم.. لا يصح بإجماع أهل السنَّةِ، بل بإجماع المسلمين، وبقي النزاع في مُجرَّدِ إطلاق لفظي. والحق: منع كل ما يوهم حدوثاً أو نقصاً في ذاته تعالى أو في صفاته جل وعلا.
It is distinguished only by its consideration, and neither difference nor similarity is established in it according to its intelligibility, but only according to its meaning that necessitates it. So how can that be negated by virtue of which it is necessary? (End). The summary of his response is: The possibility (imkan) that the Mu'tazila imposed in the causation (ta'lil) of necessary judgments does not follow on either view regarding the meaning of causation, because the possible is that which admits non-existence by its essence, and the judgments of God Most High do not admit non-existence by their essences. As for the first view on the meaning of causation, it is clear. As for the second, since they are not intelligible except through their meanings, the necessity of their meanings is a necessity for them, and they are as if one essence with them, because states (ahwal) have no distinct essence such that it can be said they admit non-existence in their essence; rather, they derive necessity from other than themselves, so they would be possible. The conclusion: Causation (ta'lil) in His attributes, meaning the production of affirmation from non-existence, is not valid by consensus of Ahl al-Sunna, nay, by consensus of Muslims. The dispute remains merely over the verbal usage. The truth is the prohibition of everything that suggests temporality or deficiency in His essence or in His attributes, may He be exalted and glorified.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is the continuation of a response to an objection. It begins with a quotation from a commentary (Sharh al-Irshad), then provides the summary of the response. The author argues that the concept of possibility (imkan) that the Mu'tazila use to argue for the contingency of judgments does not apply to divine attributes. He explains that on both interpretations of causation (ta'lil), the attributes are necessary by themselves. On the first view, it is obvious. On the second, since attributes are only conceivable through their meanings, the necessity of the meanings entails the necessity of the attributes. The passage concludes that the correct position is to reject any wording that implies temporality or deficiency, and that the dispute is merely over verbal usage.

Misconception: The Mu'tazila's argument about possibility applies to divine attributes themselves, implying contingency.
Correction: The possibility (imkan) that the Mu'tazila invoke concerns the causation (ta'lil) of judgments, not the attributes themselves; divine judgments do not admit non-existence by their essences.

Key Takeaway

Refuting the Philosophers' Denial of Divine Attributes
Sanusi presents the philosophers' objection and begins his refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the philosophers' argument that divine attributes imply need and thus contradict God's necessary nature.

Core Idea
Attributes do not imply deficiency in God; the philosophers' notion of 'need' is the wrong kind.

The philosophers argued that if God had attributes, those attributes would be in need of His essence, contradicting His self-sufficiency. Sanusi responds by distinguishing between need that implies origination and the necessary entailment between essence and attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' argument
Read the Arabic passage and translation to grasp the philosophers' reasoning that attributes necessitate need.
Pay attention to the terms 'افتقار' and 'وجوب'.
2
Identify the key concepts
Notice the terms 'افتقار' (need), 'وجوب' (necessity), 'ملازمة' (entailment), and 'حادث' (originated). Understanding these is crucial.
3
Follow the refutation
Sanusi's answer denies the entailment: need that implies originatedness is not what we claim for attributes. Attributes are necessary of existence.
The refutation starts with 'والجواب: منع الملازمة'.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Consider the unfinished part: what does Sanusi mean by 'need as inseparability'? This nuance shows that the type of need is critical.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the core refutation is complete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم: أنَّ الفلاسفة قدِ احتجّتْ على نفْي الصفاتِ بما يَقرُبُ مِنْ شبهة المعتزلة السابقة، فقالوا: لو وُجِدَتِ الصفاتُ لَلزِمَ أَنْ تَكونَ مفتقرةً إلى الذاتِ؛ لاستحالة قيام الصفة بنفسها، ولأنَّ بعضَها شرط في الباقي؛ كالحياة التي هي شرط في القدرة والعلم والإرادة، فيلزمُ أنْ يكونَ المشروط مفتقراً إلى الشرط ومتأخّراً عنه في العقل والافتقار ينافي الوجوبَ؛ إذ الواجب مستغن على الإطلاق، وذلكَ منافٍ للافتقار، والحاجةُ والتقدم على واجب الوجودِ محال. والجواب: منع الملازمة؛ فإنَّ الافتقار إلى الغيرِ يقتضي أنَّ المفتقِرَ يفيده الغير الوجود، فيكون حادثاً، ونحن لا ندعي ذلك، بل نقولُ: إِنَّ صفاتِهِ تعالى كلَّها واجبة الوجودِ، غنية عن المقتضي بإطلاق. وإن عنيتُم بالافتقار الملازمة وعدم انفكاك أحد الوجودين عنِ
Know that the philosophers (falasifa) have argued for the negation of attributes with an argument close to the previous sophism of the Mu'tazila. They said: If attributes existed, they would necessarily need (muftaqira) the essence, because it is impossible for an attribute to subsist by itself, and because some of them are conditions for the others—like life, which is a condition for power, knowledge, and will. So the conditioned would be in need of the condition and posterior to it in reason, and need (iftiqar) contradicts necessity (wujub) because the Necessary is absolutely self-sufficient, and that is incompatible with need. Need and priority over the Necessary Existence are impossible. The answer: The entailment (mulazama) is denied; for need of another entails that the one in need receives existence from the other, so it would be originated (hadith). We do not claim that; rather, we say: All His attributes are necessary of existence, absolutely independent of any efficient cause. And if you mean by 'need' the inseparability and non-detachment of one existence from...
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument against divine attributes and the beginning of its refutation. The philosophers argue that if attributes existed, they would be in need (muftaqira) of the essence, either because an attribute cannot subsist by itself or because some attributes are conditions for others (like life is a condition for power). This need contradicts the necessary nature of God, who is absolutely self-sufficient. The answer denies the entailment (mulazama): need in the sense of receiving existence from another would imply origination (hadith), but the attributes are not originated; they are themselves necessary of existence. The passage ends with a distinction about another meaning of need (inseparability), which is not completed in this window.

وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the refutation accepts the premise that attributes need the essence, but merely argues that this need does not contradict necessity.
Correction: Sanusi does not accept that attributes are in need in the sense of receiving existence; he denies that the philosophers' notion of need applies. Attributes are necessary of existence, so the type of need that would imply originatedness is absent.

Checkpoint
What is the core of Sanusi's refutation of the philosophers' argument that attributes imply need?
A) He accepts the need but argues it does not contradict necessity.
B) He denies that attributes are distinct entities.
C) He denies the entailment, because need in the sense of receiving existence from another would imply originatedness, which is not the case.
D) He argues that the philosophers misunderstand the nature of life as a condition.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes the philosophers by denying that the attributes' dependence on the essence implies deficiency, because that dependence is not a need for existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
If God's attributes are inseparable from Him and absolutely independent, then His power, knowledge, and will are always perfectly active. How does that confront your excuses for sin—like 'I couldn't help it' or 'it was out of my control'?
